Praiseworthy Opinion in Rhetoric
Author defines praiseworthy opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a praiseworthy opinion (al-ra'y al-mahmud) and explain its role as a universal premise in rhetorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Praiseworthy opinion is a universal premise used in rhetoric, unquantified, often major with omitted minor.

In Islamic logic, not all premises are created equal. Some are accepted merely because they are praiseworthy in a community. This lesson unpacks the definition of 'praiseworthy opinion' (al-ra'y al-mahmud) and shows how it functions in rhetorical arguments, setting the stage for understanding the nature of certainty in syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the Arabic term 'الرأي المحمود' in the passage. Note that it is being defined as a type of premise.
2
Extract the essential characteristics
From the definition: it is universal (كلية), praiseworthy (محمودة), about existence/action (كائن أو غير كائن, صواب فعله أو غير صواب), and occurs in rhetoric without quantification (مهملة).
3
Analyze the example
The author gives: 'Envious people are hostile, and friends advise sincerely.' Note that the minor premise (e.g., 'This person is envious' or 'This person is a friend') is omitted. The major is stated.
This omission is typical of rhetorical syllogisms (enthymemes).
4
Understand the rhetorical context
Such premises aim to persuade an audience, not to demonstrate certainty. They are accepted as good by the audience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرأي المحمود فإنه عبارة عن مقدمة محمودة كلية في أن كذا كائن أو غير كائن صواب فعله أو غير صواب، وتوجد دائما في الخطابة مهملة. وإذا عمل منها قياس ففي الأغلب تصرّح بتلك المقدمة على أنها كبرى؛ فحذف الصغرى كقولهم إن الحساد يعادون والأصدقاء ينصحون، فاعتبر بما عرفت. واعلم بأن القياس هو الذي ينتج نتيجة علمية على ما عرف من قبل في الأقيسة الاقترانية والشرطية الاستثنائية. فأما ما ينتج ظنية فذلك قياس بصورته لا الحقيقة، والله أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
As for 'praiseworthy opinion' (al-ra'y al-mahmud), it is a universal premise that is praiseworthy, about whether something is or is not, or whether its action is right or wrong, and it always occurs in rhetoric without quantification. When a syllogism is constructed from it, most often that premise is stated explicitly as the major; so the minor is omitted, like their saying: 'Envious people are hostile, and friends advise sincerely.' So consider from what you have learned. And know that the syllogism is what produces a certain conclusion, as previously known in conjunctive and conditional-exceptional syllogisms. As for what produces 'probable' (conclusions), that is a syllogism in form only, not in reality. And God knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and destination.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage defines praiseworthy opinion (al-ra'y al-mahmud) as a universal premise used in rhetoric, often without quantification. When used in a syllogism, the major premise is stated and the minor is omitted. An example is given: 'Envious people are hostile, and friends advise sincerely' – which implies a conclusion about specific individuals. The author then makes a general remark: a true syllogism produces certain knowledge; any argument that yields only probability is a syllogism in form only, not in reality. This underscores the Aristotelian ideal that demonstration produces certainty.

Key Terms
الرأي المحمود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does a praiseworthy opinion typically appear in a rhetorical syllogism?
A) As the minor premise.
B) As the major premise with the minor omitted.
C) As both premises.
D) As the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Praiseworthy opinion is a universal premise used in rhetoric, often stated as the major with the minor omitted, aiming at persuasion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premise 'Justice is good' (praiseworthy opinion), construct the implicit rhetorical syllogism by supplying the missing minor premise to reach a conclusion about a specific action.
?
Identify the praiseworthy opinion in this rhetorical argument: 'Scholars are trustworthy; so trust this scholar.'

Certainty in Syllogism vs. Probable Argument
Author discusses certainty vs probability in syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a true syllogism that yields certainty and an argument that is a syllogism only in form yielding probability.

Core Idea
Only scientific syllogisms yield certainty; probabilistic arguments are syllogisms in form only.

The author closes the passage with an important epistemological distinction: not every argument that looks like a syllogism produces certain knowledge. This lesson helps you apply that criterion to evaluate arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the author's claim about certainty
Locate the sentence: 'القياس هو الذي ينتج نتيجة علمية' (the syllogism is what produces a certain conclusion). Note that this is presented as previously known.
2
Identify the statement about probability
Find: 'فأما ما ينتج ظنية فذلك قياس بصورته لا الحقيقة' (as for what produces probable, that is a syllogism in form only, not in reality).
3
Contrast the two
A true syllogism must have premises that yield certainty (scientific premises). A probabilistic argument may have the correct figure and mood but lacks certain premises.
The author implies that praiseworthy opinions are not certain, so arguments using them are only syllogisms in form.
4
Apply to the praiseworthy opinion example
The syllogism 'Envious people are hostile; this person is envious; therefore this person is hostile' would be a true syllogism only if the major premise is certain. Since it is merely a praiseworthy opinion, the argument is rhetorical, not demonstrative.

Source Text

As for 'praiseworthy opinion' (al-ra'y al-mahmud), it is a universal premise that is praiseworthy, about whether something is or is not, or whether its action is right or wrong, and it always occurs in rhetoric without quantification. This underscores the Aristotelian ideal that demonstration produces certainty.

A true syllogism requires certain premises; arguments with probable premises are syllogisms in form only.

Praiseworthy Opinion and the True Nature of Qiyas
Definition of praiseworthy opinion and distinction of certain vs probable qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of a praiseworthy opinion and distinguish a certain syllogism from a merely probable one.

Core Idea
A syllogism is only truly a syllogism when it produces certainty; probable arguments are mere forms.

In this passage, the author defines a key concept from rhetoric—the praiseworthy opinion—and then draws a sharp epistemological line: only syllogisms that yield certainty (natija 'ilmiyya) deserve the name 'qiyas'; those that yield only probability are qiyas in form only. This distinction is crucial for understanding the hierarchy of argumentation in Islamic logic, where demonstration (burhan) reigns supreme.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'الرأي المحمود'. What is the author defining? It is a type of premise used in rhetoric.
The definition starts with 'عبارة عن'.
2
Extract genus and differentia
The genus is 'مقدمة' (premise). The differentia include being 'محمودة' (commendable), 'كلية' (universal), and its typical use as a suppressed major premise in rhetoric.
Also note the content: it asserts that something is or is not, or an action is correct or incorrect.
3
Distinguish certain vs probable qiyas
The author distinguishes true qiyas (producing certainty) from arguments that are qiyas only in form (producing probability). Identify the criterion: only those with certain premises yield certainty.
The phrase 'بصورته لا الحقيقة' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «الرأي» المحمود فإنه عبارة عن مقدمة محمودة كلية في أن كذا كائن أو غير كائن صواب فعله أو غير صواب، وتوجد دائما في الخطابة مهملة. وإذا عمل منها قياس ففي الأغلب تصرّح بتلك المقدمة على أنها كبرى؛ فحذف الصغرى كقولهم إن الحساد يعادون والأصدقاء ينصحون»، فاعتبر بما عرفت. واعلم بأن القياس هو الذي ينتج نتيجة علمية على ما عرف من قبل في الأقيسة الاقترانية والشرطية الاستثنائية. فأما ما ينتج «ظنية» فذلك قياس بصورته لا الحقيقة، والله أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
As for the praiseworthy 'opinion', it is a universal commendable premise that something is or is not, or its being done is correct or incorrect, and it always occurs in rhetoric as a suppressed premise. When a syllogism is composed from it, most often that premise is made explicit as the major, while the minor is omitted, as in their saying: 'The envious are hostile and friends advise sincerely.' So take heed from what you have learned. And know that the syllogism is what produces a certain result, as previously known in categorical and conditional-exceptive syllogisms. As for what produces a 'probable' result, that is a syllogism in its form only, not in reality. And Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the refuge.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage defines a type of premise used in rhetoric called 'praiseworthy opinion'. It is a universal premise that is commendable, stating that something is or is not, or that an action is correct or incorrect. Such premises are typically used as suppressed premises in rhetorical arguments; most often they are stated as the major premise, and the minor is omitted. The author gives an example: 'The envious are hostile and friends advise sincerely.' This likely implies a conclusion like 'So-and-so is envious, therefore he is hostile.' The author then reminds that the real syllogism (qiyas) produces certain knowledge (natija 'ilmiyya), as established in the sections on categorical and conditional syllogisms. Any syllogism that yields only probable (zanni) knowledge is a syllogism only in form, not in reality, because its premises do not guarantee certainty. This distinction underscores the epistemological hierarchy in logic: only demonstrative syllogisms yield certainty, while rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms yield only probability or persuasion.

Key Terms
الرأي المحمود
قياس
نتيجة علمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In this passage, what is the status of a syllogism that produces only a probable (zanni) result?
A) It is a true syllogism in form and reality.
B) It is a syllogism in form only, not in reality.
C) It is a defective syllogism that should be avoided.
D) It is a syllogism that yields certainty when the minor is suppressed.

Key Takeaway
True qiyas yields certainty; probable arguments are qiyas only in form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Necessity of Rhetoric for Establishing True Beliefs
Author explains the great benefit of rhetoric and its role in convincing the masses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why rhetoric is necessary for establishing true beliefs in the public and distinguish its role from that of demonstration.

Core Idea
Rhetoric bridges the gap between demonstrative truth and popular conviction.

This lesson examines the author's argument for the supreme benefit of rhetoric. He grounds it in the social nature of humans and the inadequacy of demonstration for the masses. Understanding this helps us appreciate the role of persuasive discourse in Islamic propagation and education.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about rhetoric's benefit
Locate the sentence where the author states the craft of rhetoric is 'of great benefit'. This is the main thesis of the passage.
The term 'صناعة الخطابة' refers to the art of rhetoric.
2
Trace the reasoning from human partnership to need for true judgments
Follow the chain: humans are social (tashāruk) → partnership necessitates interaction and trade → true judgments are needed for welfare. This shows rhetoric's benefit is rooted in social necessity.
The author uses 'التشارك' to emphasize the cooperative nature of human life.
3
Note the limitation of demonstration
Find where the author says demonstration ('البرهان') is of little benefit in carrying the masses to the true creed. This is the key contrast.
Demonstration requires strict premises and is only accessible to trained minds.
4
Conclude the role of rhetoric
The author concludes that rhetoric is the art charged with establishing true beliefs in souls. This is the primary virtue of rhetoric.
Rhetoric uses persuasive means suitable for the general public.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مشاركات الخطابة لغيرها من الصنائع ومخالفاتها له. إن صناعة الخطابة عظيمة النفع جدا؛ وذلك لأن الأحكام الصادقة فيما عدل وحسن أفضل نفعًا وأعم على الناس جدوى من أضدادها؛ وذلك لأن نوع الإنسان مشتق بالتشارك، والتشارك محوج إلى التعامل والتجاور؛ والتعامل والتجاور يحوّجان إلى أحكام صادقة في الأمور العلمية التي بها ينتظم شمل المصلحة؛ وهذه الأحكام تحتاج أن تكون مقررة في النفوس متمكنة من العقائد وقد بينا أن البرهان قليل الجدوى في حمل الجمهور على العقد الحق، وبينا أن الخطابة هي المتكلفة فيه؛ فإحدى فضائل هذه الصناعة عيادها في تقرير هذه الأعراض في الأنفس.
Chapter on the participations of rhetoric with other arts and its differences from them. Indeed, the craft of rhetoric is of great benefit; this is because true judgments concerning what is just and good are more beneficial in benefit and more general in utility for people than their opposites; this is because the human species is composed through partnership, and partnership necessitates transaction and neighborliness; and transaction and neighborliness necessitate true judgments in scientific matters by which the fabric of welfare is ordered; and these judgments need to be established in souls and firmly rooted in beliefs, and we have shown that demonstration is of little benefit in carrying the masses to the true creed, and we have shown that rhetoric is the one charged with that; so one of the virtues of this craft is its recourse in establishing these (true) beliefs in souls.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the shared aspects and differences between rhetoric and other logical arts. The author begins by asserting the great benefit of the art of rhetoric, grounding this in the fact that true judgments—especially those concerning justice and goodness—are far more beneficial and widespread in their utility for people than false or unjust judgments. The reasoning is that human society is founded on partnership (tashāruk), which requires interaction and trade (ta'āmul wa tajāwur), and these in turn necessitate sound judgments in practical and theoretical matters that ensure collective welfare. These judgments must be firmly established in people's souls as beliefs. The author then references a previous point: demonstration (burhān) is of little use in convincing the masses to adopt the true creed; rather, rhetoric is the art entrusted with this task. Therefore, one of the primary virtues of rhetoric is precisely its role in implanting these beliefs in souls. This passage sets the stage for a detailed comparison with other arts like dialectic and poetry.

Key Terms
صناعة الخطابة
البرهان
التشارك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary benefit of rhetoric?
A) It produces certain knowledge like demonstration.
B) It convinces the masses of true beliefs when demonstration fails.
C) It is more logical than demonstration.
D) It replaces the need for demonstration entirely.

Key Takeaway
Rhetoric is essential because it makes true judgments accessible to the common people, who cannot be moved by demonstration alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Rhetoric's Role in Helping the Weak in Reasoning Grasp Sound Judgments
Author discusses how rhetoric aids those weak in reasoning in accepting sound judgments about particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the role of rhetoric from that of dialectic and demonstration in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Rhetoric makes sound practical judgments accessible to those who cannot reason independently.

This passage from the logic text explains how rhetoric assists individuals who are weak in reasoning to accept sound judgments about particular matters. It contrasts rhetoric with demonstration and dialectic, showing how each is suited to different audiences and types of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cognitive tools discussed
Look for demonstration (burhān), dialectic (jadal), and rhetoric (khiṭāba) in the passage. Also note sound reasoning (ta‘aqqul) which is the basis for particular judgments.
Sound reasoning is not a tool but the faculty being aided.
2
Contrast the domains of demonstration and sound reasoning
Demonstration deals with universal theoretical matters and relies on truth. Sound reasoning deals with particular matters and relies on deliberation and reflection.
The passage explicitly says demonstration is for 'al-umūr al-kulliyya al-naẓariyya'.
3
Determine the function of dialectic
Dialectic helps establish demonstrative truths for those who have descended from the rank of demonstration, i.e., cannot follow the proof.
This shows dialectic as a teaching tool for weaker minds.
4
Determine the function of rhetoric
Rhetoric helps establish sound judgments about particulars for those weak in reasoning themselves.
It is the counterpart of dialectic for practical reasoning.
5
Explain why rhetoric is needed
Not everyone can engage in pure reasoning; rhetoric makes practical reasoning accessible to the common person.
The passage uses 'man yad‘ufu ‘an al-‘aql bi-nafsihi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فإن في الأمور الجزئية أحكامًا يوجبها التعقل الصحيح؛ وليس التعقل الصحيح مبنيا على المخاطبة والمحاورة بل قانونه الروية والنظر كما أن البرهان في الأمور الكلية النظرية مبني على الحق دون المحاورة . ثمَّ الذي يصح بالبرهان من تلك الأمور إذا أريد تقريره في نفس من تسفّل عن رتبة البرهان كان الجدل أعون شيء على تقريره كذلك المدرك بالتعقل إذا أريد أن يقرر في نفس من يضعف عن العقل بنفسه كانت الخطابة أعون شيء عليه.
And moreover, in particular matters there are judgments required by sound reasoning; and sound reasoning is not based on address and dialogue, rather its rule is deliberation and reflection, just as demonstration in theoretical universal matters is based on truth without dialogue. Then that which is established by demonstration from those matters, if one intends to establish it in a soul that has descended from the rank of demonstration, dialectic is the most helpful thing for its establishment. Similarly, that which is perceived by sound reasoning, if one intends to establish it in a soul that is weak in reasoning itself, rhetoric is the most helpful thing for it.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage extends the argument by considering particular (juz'iyya) matters, which require sound reasoning (ta'aqqul) rather than demonstration. The author clarifies that sound reasoning in particulars follows a different law (qānūn) than dialogue; it is based on deliberation (rawiyya) and reflection (nazar). Then he draws a parallel: just as for universal theoretical matters, when someone is incapable of grasping demonstration, dialectic helps establish that truth, so for particular matters perceived by sound reasoning, when someone is weak in reasoning, rhetoric helps establish it. This highlights rhetoric's role in making sound practical judgments accessible to those who cannot reason independently.

Key Terms
التعقل
الروية
الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is rhetoric especially helpful for particular matters?
A) It is based on universal premises.
B) It aids those weak in reasoning.
C) It establishes demonstration.
D) It replaces deliberation.

Key Takeaway
Rhetoric bridges the gap between sound practical reasoning and those who cannot perform it, making sound judgments accessible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Virtuous Rhetoric vs Dialectic on Opposites
Comparison of rhetoric and dialectic on opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish virtuous particular rhetoric from dialectic and other logical arts based on their relationship to arguing opposites and commitment to the better side.

Core Idea
Rhetoric and dialectic alone handle opposites, but virtuous rhetoric picks the better side.

In this passage, the author explains the unique position of rhetoric among the logical arts. While demonstration, sophistry, and poetry each have limited scope, rhetoric and dialectic share the ability to address opposites. However, the author emphasizes that virtuous rhetoric is not neutral; it deliberately chooses the better side and bases itself on better premises. This distinction is essential for understanding how rhetoric can be ethically used in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
List the arts mentioned
Identify all the crafts (ṣanā‘i‘) named: demonstration (burhāniyya), dialectic (jadal), rhetoric (khiṭāba), sophistry (sūfisṭā’iyya), and poetry (shi‘riyya).
2
Find the common feature of rhetoric and dialectic
The passage states that both rhetoric and dialectic are used for opposites (al-mutaḍāddān). No other craft can do that beneficially.
3
Note the restriction on simultaneity
Rhetoric does not claim the same thing is both true and false at the same time, and dialectic does not argue both sides simultaneously except in exercise (riyāḍa). This prevents contradiction.
4
Extract the definition of virtuous particular rhetoric
The phrase 'al-khiṭāba al-juz’iyya al-fāḍila' is defined as that which takes the better side (al-ṭaraf al-afḍal) and starts from better premises.
5
Compare with other arts
Demonstration is for one side only, sophistry is for deception, poetry for imagination. Only rhetoric and dialectic are useful for opposites, but virtuous rhetoric is distinguished by its commitment to the better.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا لم يكن المدبر من الناس مستحقاً لأن يخاطب بالصحيح من البيان العلمي فيما ينبغي أن يعتقد، أو بالبيان التعقلي فيما ينبغي أن يعمل: فإذا كانت لنا قوة خطابية تمكننا من إقناع المخاطب بما يحمده ويظنه ويقبلها ويناسب قدره ويشاكله فصناعة الخطابة من الصنائع التي يقنع بها في المتضادين كما أن صناعة الجدل كانت صناعة يقاس بها على المتضادين. وليس على أن الخطابة يقنع بها في وقت واحد أن هذا الشيء بعينه كان؛ وإنه بعينه لم يكن، ولا على أن يكون الجدل أيضًا يرام به القياس على المتقابلين معا في زمان بعينه إلا في الرياضة. وليس من الصنائع المنتفع بها صناعة نفيس فيها على المتقابلين غير الجدل والخطابة؛ إذ البرهانية لأحدهما لا غير، والسوفسطائية للتغليط لا للإقناع، والشعرية للتخييل كذلك لكن الخطابية وإن كانت بهذه الصفة. فالخطابة الجزئية الفاضلة هي التي الطرف الأفضل؛ ونبتدئ من المقدمات التي هي أفضل.
And if the manager among people does not deserve to be addressed with the correct scientific statement in what should be believed, or with the rational statement in what should be done: then if we have a rhetorical power that enables us to persuade the addressee with what he praises, thinks, accepts, and suits his capacity and matches him, then the craft of rhetoric is among the crafts by which one is persuaded in opposites, just as the craft of dialectic was a craft by which one measures opposites. But it is not that rhetoric persuades at the same time that this very thing was and that it was not, nor is dialectic sought to measure both at the same time except in exercise. And there is no beneficial craft that can be used on opposites except dialectic and rhetoric; demonstration is for one side only, sophistry is for deception not persuasion, and poetry is for imagination likewise, but rhetoric (though it has this description). Thus the virtuous particular rhetoric is that which is on the better side; and we begin from premises that are better.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage discusses the unique capability of rhetoric to argue on opposite sides, similar to dialectic. The author cautions that rhetoric does not claim the same thing is both true and false at the same time; rather, it can persuade in favor of one side or the other. Dialectic, too, can argue both sides but only in exercise. Among the arts, only dialectic and rhetoric are useful for opposites; demonstration is confined to one side, sophistry to deception, and poetry to imagination. The virtuous rhetoric, however, chooses the better side and uses better premises. This emphasizes that rhetoric is not neutral but prefers truth.

Genuine vs. Apparent Persuasion: The Medical Analogy in Rhetoric
Author illustrates rhetoric's virtue through medical analogy and distinguishes genuine from apparent persuasion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine rhetorical persuasion from apparent persuasion according to the criteria given in the passage.

Core Idea
Genuine rhetoric persuades by its own premises; apparent rhetoric persuades only when mistaken for something else.

This passage from the logic textbook explores the virtue of rhetoric through a compelling analogy with medicine. Just as a doctor treats within capacity, an orator uses speech to persuade, sometimes genuinely, sometimes deceptively. The key distinction is how the premises themselves produce assent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the medical analogy
The author compares the orator to a doctor. What are the limits of the doctor's obligation? How does this parallel the orator's duty?
The doctor is not required to cure every patient but to treat correctly as far as possible; similarly, the orator must use the best available means of persuasion.
2
Locate the criterion for genuine vs apparent persuasion
Find the sentence that states the difference. Genuine persuasion's premises naturally incline the mind to assent upon first hearing; apparent persuasion's premises only gain assent because they are mistaken for something else.
The passage says: 'the premises of the genuine one, when their meanings are settled in the mind, incline toward assent... whereas that which is seen as persuader only gains assent for being other than what it is.'
3
Apply the distinction to a rhetorical argument
Consider a common da'wa argument: 'The Quran is a miracle, therefore it is from Allah.' Determine whether the premise 'The Quran is a miracle' leads to natural assent (genuine) or requires prior belief (apparent).

These are among the virtues of rhetoric: I mean its ability to act in persuasion sometimes on one side and sometimes on the other; and its ruling is like the ruling of the limbs of a human, for they are prepared to act in pleasuring and paining; and a person defends himself and his body with what he shares with other animals, but what makes him excellent is that he is able to defend himself with what is specific to him, namely the tongue and expression, so he uses them to be just, to permit, to do good, to do evil, and he can act in opposites. And just as the doctor is not obligated to cure every patient of every disease but treats according to the correct way as much as possible, so too the orator must undertake to bring forth action and stratagems as much as possible in every subject. And just as in absolute dialectic there is a genuinely dialectical syllogism and a dialectical syllogism by analogy, so too in rhetoric. And the difference between the genuine persuader and that which is seen as persuader is that the premises of the genuine one, when their meanings are settled in the mind, incline toward assent in the first opinion, at the hearing of the listener; whereas that which is seen as persuader only gains assent for being other than what it is. And it is not far that persuasion by analogy is included in the craft; and some of the craft is from insight and knowledge, and some not from that side but from error or intent, such as likening to evil, for dialectic is only dialectic
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage continues to describe virtues of rhetoric: its capacity to persuade on both sides. The author uses an analogy with human limbs that can cause pleasure or pain; similarly, speech (tongue and expression) can be used for good or evil. He compares the orator to a doctor who does his best within his capacity. Then he draws a parallel with dialectic: just as there are genuine and analogical dialectical syllogisms, so in rhetoric. He distinguishes genuine persuasion from apparent persuasion: genuine premises naturally lead to assent upon hearing, while apparent ones gain assent only because they are taken for something else. He notes that persuasion by analogy can be part of the craft, but parts of the craft may arise from insight or from error or deceptive intent, as in likening to evil. The passage ends abruptly with a statement about dialectic.

Rhetoric: The Art of Persuasion on Opposites
Author discusses rhetoric's capacity for persuasion on opposites and compares it to other arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine persuasion from apparent persuasion in rhetoric and explain why rhetoric alone among the arts can argue both sides of an issue.

Core Idea
Rhetoric uniquely combines genuine faculty and resemblance to persuade on opposites.

This passage explores the nature of rhetoric as an art of discourse. The author compares rhetoric to dialectic, demonstration, sophistry, and poetics, highlighting its unique ability to persuade on both sides of an issue. Crucially, he distinguishes between genuine persuasion and mere resemblance, and shows how rhetoric contains both. Understanding this distinction is key to evaluating rhetorical arguments in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arts compared
The author mentions five arts: demonstration (burhān), dialectic (jadal), sophistry (safsata), poetics (shiʿr), and rhetoric (khaṭāba). Note what each is characterized by.
Demonstration is for one side, sophistry for deception, poetics for imagination.
2
Note which arts can argue opposites
The author states that only dialectic and rhetoric can argue both sides of an issue simultaneously. This is a key distinguishing feature.
Dialectic can also do this, but rhetoric is the focus.
3
Distinguish genuine vs. apparent persuasion
Genuine persuasion (al-muqniʿ al-ḥaqīqī) occurs when the premises naturally lead to assent when their meanings are grasped. Apparent persuasion makes the listener assent as if to something else.
This is a crucial epistemological distinction.
4
Understand rhetoric's dual nature
Rhetoric contains both a genuine faculty (quwwa) and resemblance (tashbīh). Resemblance can be used in a general sense (aiding assent) or a specific sense (for promotion and obfuscation, resembling sophistry). The specific sense does not belong to the true art.
This explains why rhetoric is not purely deceptive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون الجدل أيضًا يرام به القياس على المتقابلين معًا في زمان بعينه إلا في الرياضة. وليس من الصنائع المنتفع بها صناعة نفيس فيها على المتقابلين غير الجدل والخطابة؛ إذ البرهانية لأحدهما لا غير، والسوفسطائية للتغليط لا للإقناع، والشعرية للتخييل كذلك لكن الخطابية وإن كانت بهذه الصفة. فالخطابة الجزئية الفاضلة هي التي الطرف الأفضل؛ ونبتدئ من المقدمات التي هي أفضل. فهذه من فضائل الخطابة أعني اقتدارها على التصرف في الإقناع تارة في طرف وتارة في الطرف الآخر؛ وحكم هذا كحكم أعضاء الإنسان فإنها معدة للتصرف في الإلذاذ والإيلام؛ والمرء ينصر نفسه وبدنه بما هو مشارك لسائر الحيوانات فيما أحسن به أن يكون قادرًا على نصرة نفسه بما يخصه وهو اللسان والبيان فيعدل به ويجوز ويحسن ويسيء ويمكن به من التصرف في المتقابلات. وكما أن الطبيب ليس عليه أن يشفي كل مريض من كل مرض بل يعالج على طريق الصواب ما أمكن فكذلك الخطيب عليه أن يتكلف من إيراد العمل والحيل ما يمكن إيراده في كل باب وكما أن في الجدل المطلق قياسًا جدليا بالحقيقة وقياسًا جدليًا بحسب التشبيه كذلك في الخطابة والفرق بين المقنع الحقيقي وبين الذي يرى مقنعا أن مقدمات الحقيقي إذا تقرر معانيها في الذهن مال إلى التصديق بها في بادي الرأي طرف السمع السامع، والذي يرى مقنعا إنما وقع به التصديق على أنها غيرها. ولا يبعد أن يكون المقنع بالتشبيه داخلا في الصناعة؛ وقد كانت الصناعة بعضها عن بصيرة ومعرفة، وبعضها لا عن تلك الجهة بل عن غلط أو قصد ومشبَّهة للشر مثلا، فالجدل إنما هو جدل لتلك القوة، والسفسطة إنما هي سفسطة لتلك المشبهة الروية. وأما الخطابة ففيها قوة ومشبَّهة معًا؛ والمشبَّهة قد تستعمل في مثل هذا الموضع على وجهين: عاما فيقال مشبهة لمشبَّهة إيقاع التصديق فيكون الخطابة معدّة نحو أن يكون مع القوة مشبهة، وخاصًا. وتخصيص تلك المشبهة بمشبَّهة الترويج والتلبيس، وتلك المشبَّهة تشبها لمشبهة السوفسطائية؛ وليست تناسب القوة الصناعية.
Dialectic also aims at syllogism on opposites together at a specific time except in exercise. Among the useful arts, there is no art that deals with opposites except dialectic and rhetoric; because demonstration deals with only one side, sophistry is for deception not persuasion, and poetry is for imagination likewise, but rhetoric is characterized by this. The excellent particular rhetoric is that which takes the better side; and we begin from the premises that are better. So among the virtues of rhetoric is its ability to act in persuasion sometimes on one side and sometimes on the other; and its ruling is like the ruling of human limbs which are prepared for action in pleasure and pain; and a person supports himself and his body with what is shared by other animals, and he is best able to support himself with what is specific to him, namely the tongue and eloquence, by which he does justice and allows, does good and evil, and by which he can act on opposites. Just as it is not incumbent on the physician to cure every patient of every disease, but he treats as correctly as possible, so too the rhetorician must undertake to bring about action and devices as much as possible in every topic. And just as in absolute dialectic there is a syllogism that is truly dialectical and a syllogism dialectical by resemblance, so too in rhetoric. The difference between what is genuinely persuasive and what is considered persuasive is that when the meanings of the premises of the genuine one are established in the mind, it inclines to assent at first glance to the hearing of the listener, while that which is considered persuasive only occurs assent to it as if it were something else. It is not unlikely that persuasion by resemblance enters into the art; for the art sometimes comes from insight and knowledge, and sometimes not from that aspect but from error or intent and something resembling truth, for example. Thus dialectic is dialectic for that faculty, and sophistry is sophistry for that deceptive resemblance. As for rhetoric, it has both faculty and resemblance; and resemblance may be used in such a place in two ways: generally, it is called resemblance for bringing about assent so that rhetoric is prepared to be with the faculty a resemblance; and specifically, that resemblance is specified as the resemblance of promotion and obfuscation, and that resemblance resembles sophistry; and it is not suitable for the industrial faculty.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of rhetoric and its relationship to other arts of discourse. The author begins by noting that dialectic (jadal) can argue both sides of an issue simultaneously, except in training exercises. Among the arts, only dialectic and rhetoric have this capacity to deal with opposites; demonstration (burhan) is restricted to one side, sophistry (safsata) aims at deception, and poetry (shi'r) at imagination. The author then explains that the excellent form of rhetoric is that which chooses the better side and starts from better premises. A key virtue of rhetoric is its power to persuade on either side, analogous to human limbs which can produce both pleasure and pain, or the tongue which can speak justly or unjustly. Just as a physician is not required to cure every patient but does his best, the rhetorician must apply persuasive techniques as best he can in every case. The author distinguishes between genuine persuasion and apparent persuasion: genuine persuasion occurs when the premises naturally lead to assent in the listener's mind, whereas apparent persuasion makes the listener assent to something as if it were something else. The author then clarifies that rhetoric contains both a genuine faculty (quwwa) and resemblance (tashbīh), whereas dialectic is purely based on faculty and sophistry on deceptive resemblance. Resemblance in rhetoric can be used in two senses: a general sense where it aids assent (so rhetoric has both faculty and resemblance), and a specific sense where resemblance is used for promotion and obfuscation, resembling sophistry. However, this specific resemblance does not belong to the true art of rhetoric.

Dissecting the Definition of Rhetoric
Definition of rhetoric with component analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to dissect the definition of rhetoric into its four components (faculty, undertaking, possible persuasion, particular matters) and explain how each component distinguishes rhetoric from other arts.

Core Idea
Rhetoric is a faculty for persuasive speech across all particular matters, not just one field.

In this passage, the author provides a precise definition of rhetoric and unpacks each term. Rhetoric is not merely the ability to speak persuasively; it is a developed disposition (malaka) that intentionally pursues persuasion in any particular subject. This definition sets rhetoric apart from arts like dialectic and medicine by its universal scope. Let's walk through the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
The author defines rhetoric (al-khataba) as 'a faculty that undertakes possible persuasion in each one of particular matters.' This is the core definition. Note the key terms: quwwa (faculty), tatakallaf (undertakes), al-iqna' al-mumkin (possible persuasion), and fi kulli wahid min al-umur al-mufrada (in each one of particular matters).
2
Unpack 'quwwa' as a psychological disposition
The author clarifies that 'quwwa' here means a psychological disposition (malaka) from which volitional acts arise. It is stronger than bare ability (qudra), which every human has, because disposition requires cultivation and produces consistent action.
Later Avicennan psychology distinguishes quwwa from malaka, but here they are synonymous.
3
Understand 'tatakallaf' and 'al-iqna' al-mumkin'
'Tatakallaf' (undertakes) implies intentional effort, not coercion. 'Al-iqna' al-mumkin' (possible persuasion) means rhetoric aims at persuasion to the extent possible, without guaranteeing certainty. This distinguishes it from demonstration (burhan) which yields certainty.
4
Grasp 'fi kulli wahid min al-umur al-mufrada'
This phrase indicates that rhetoric covers every particular (juz'i) in any category (maqula). Unlike medicine, which is restricted to health matters, rhetoric has no specific genus—it applies universally. This universal scope is the key differentia.
'Al-umur al-mufrada' (particular matters) contrasts with universals, emphasizing rhetoric's focus on concrete cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرح حد هذه الصناعة وهي الخطابة فنقول إن الخطابة قوة تتكلف الإقناع الممكن في كل واحد من الأمور المفردة؛ فقولنا قوة نعني بها ملكة نفسانية يصدر عنها أفعال إرادية وهي أقوى من القدرة الساذجة؛ إذ القدرة قد توجد في كل إنسان بخلاف الملكة. وقولنا تتكلف يفهم منه أنه تتعاطى فعلا وذلك إما عن قصد وإرادة وهو الغرض، أو لا عن قصد وإرادة مؤثرة بل عن إرادة مستكرهة؛ وليس هذا هو الغرض في هذا المعنى. وقولنا الإقناع الممكن هو نفس الفعل الذي تتكلفه، ومعناه ما يمكن من الإقناع. وقولنا في كل واحد واحد من الأمور المفردة معناه في أي جزئي كان من الجزئيات، وفي أي مقولة من المقولات؛ فقولنا المفردة يدل على المقولة، وقولنا كل واحد يدل على أن كل جزئي من كل مقولة فهو موضوع له. وبهذا الشرح يظهر الفصل بين هذه الصناعة وغيرها من الجدل وغيره. وكذلك بينها وبين الطلب وغيره أيضًا؛ فإنا إذا قلنا تكلف الإقناع الممكن في كل واحد من الأمور المفردة، ودللنا على موضوع الخطابة كما تر حرح من ذلك الطب فإنه يشبه أن يكون الطب إنما يقنع ما يمكن من الإقناع في أمور مفردة، ويخص نوعا ما. فيكون جملة قولنا يتكلف الإقناع الممكن في كل واحد من الأمور المفردة يفرّق بين الطب وغيره؛ إذ الخطابة لا تنسب إلى جنس معين. ثمَّ لقائل أن يقول الطب ليس إلا
As for the explanation of the definition of this art, which is rhetoric, we say: rhetoric is a faculty that undertakes possible persuasion in each one of particular matters. So our saying 'faculty' (quwwa) we mean by it a psychological disposition (malaka) from which volitional acts issue, and it is stronger than bare ability (qudra); for ability may exist in every human, unlike disposition. And our saying 'undertakes' (tatakallaf) indicates that it engages in an action, either by intention and will which is the purpose, or not by effective intention and will but by coerced will; and this is not the purpose in this meaning. And our saying 'possible persuasion' (al-iqnā‘ al-mumkin) is the very action that it undertakes, and its meaning is what is possible of persuasion. And our saying 'in each one of particular matters' means in any particular among particulars, and in any category among categories; so our saying 'particular matters' indicates the category, and our saying 'each one' indicates that every particular from every category is a subject for it. And with this explanation the distinction appears between this art and others like dialectic and others. And likewise between it and inquiry (ṭalab) and others also; for if we say 'undertakes possible persuasion in each one of particular matters', and we indicate the subject of rhetoric, as you see [?] that medicine resembles that it persuades what is possible of persuasion in particular matters, and specifies a certain type. So the totality of our saying 'undertakes possible persuasion in each one of particular matters' distinguishes between medicine and others; for rhetoric is not attributed to a specific genus. Then one may say: medicine is nothing but...
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition (hadd) of rhetoric. The author defines rhetoric as 'a faculty that undertakes possible persuasion in each one of particular matters.' He then unpacks each component of the definition. First, 'faculty' (quwwa) is explained as a psychological disposition (malaka) from which volitional acts arise, and it is stronger than mere ability (qudra), which can exist in any human, whereas disposition requires cultivation. Second, 'undertakes' (tatakallaf) implies that the action is done intentionally, not under coercion. Third, 'possible persuasion' (al-iqnā‘ al-mumkin) refers to the action itself, emphasizing that rhetoric does not guarantee persuasion but aims at what is possible. Fourth, 'in each one of particular matters' explains that rhetoric covers every particular (juz’ī) in any category (maqūla). The author highlights that this definition distinguishes rhetoric from other arts like dialectic and inquiry (ṭalab). He contrasts rhetoric with medicine, which also deals with particular matters but is restricted to a specific genus (health). The definition shows that rhetoric is universal in its subject matter, not tied to a specific field. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing with an objection about medicine.

الأمور المفردة

The Three Types of Rhetorical Arguments
Classification and three types of artificial rhetorical assents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of artificial rhetorical assents and identify which one is appropriate for past versus future matters.

Core Idea
Persuasion relies on three pillars: confirming the claim, projecting character, and leading the listener step by step.

In this passage, the author moves beyond the general definition of the orator's faculty and presents a detailed classification of rhetorical assents. He distinguishes between natural and artificial persuasion, and then dissects the artificial into three distinct types. Understanding these categories will sharpen your ability to analyze sermons, debates, and da'wa arguments in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the broader classification
Note that the author first separates rhetorical assents into artificial (constructed) and non-artificial. Focus on the artificial ones, as they are the subject of the three types.
Non-artificial assents are not discussed further here.
2
Recognize the three tools
The three types are: (1) al-‘amud or tathbit (the pillar/confirmation), (2) al-samat (the speaker's demeanor), and (3) al-istidraj (gradual drawing of the listener). Memorize these terms and their Arabic names.
These are not mutually exclusive but serve different functions.
3
Analyze the purpose of each
Tathbit aims directly at the goal or supports it via other topics like praising oneself or blaming the opponent. Samat concerns delivery and is mainly effective for past events (complaint, apology, praise, blame). Istidraj moves the listener gradually toward the conclusion.
Samat does not guarantee truth for future claims.
4
Apply the distinction to a real case
Consider a khutba: the imam uses tathbit when citing Qur'anic proofs, uses samat through his tone and appearance, and uses istidraj when he starts with general reminders and narrows to a specific command. Identify each part.
Practice with actual khutbas or debates.

وهو ملكة على الإقناع الممكن لكن الإقناع للطبيب بالعرض ومن حيث هو فاعل فعل الخطيب إلا أنه ليس بذلك خطيبًا. وكيف وإنه ليس له ملكة على أن يقنع في كل شيء؛ وإنما الخطيب خطيبًا بهذه الملكة لا بأفعالها التي تصدر عنها في أشياء معينة. ثم التصديقات الخطابية قد تكون صناعية، وقد لا تكون؛ وهذه لا تكون بمثله مسائل لوجود الأمر الذي يدعو إليه. وأما الصناعية فإنها إما تكون من فكرتنا وباختيالنا فنحن نستنبط المواضع والأنواع الخطابية، ونعلم ترتيب القياس الخطابي وما يتعلق به هذا من جهة الأصل. فأما من جهة الاستعمال فبعضها قد تكون معدة لنا من قبل وهي المقدمات التي تسمى في هذا الكتاب مواضع؛ وهي المقدمات التي من بيانها أجزاء قياس بالقوة أو بالفعل، وبعضها لا تكون معدة بل المعد فيها أصول وقوانين إذا علمناها استخرجنا منها وقت المحاورة مقدمات خطابية. وتلك القوانين تسمّى في هذا الكتاب أنواعًا فلا تزال تتوصل من نتيجة مستمرة على طريق الاستدراج إلى حصول الغرض؛ مثاله إذا كان قد تيسر لنا عند تحصيل هذه القوة كيف ينتقل الحكم من ضد إلى ضد على سبيل الإقناع. ثم خاطبنا مشيرين فعلنا إن كان زيد الذي هو استوجب إساءتك إليه فعمرو الذي هو صديقك استوجب إحسانك إليه؛ كنا قد استخرجنا هذا من قانون معد لنا. والتصديقات الصناعية التي يختال لها بالكلام، ويكون ذلك الكلام مقنعاً لوضع أو شرع هي ثلاثة أصناف: [1] أحدها العمود الذي يسمى تثبيتا في هذا الكتاب. [ب] والثاني في كيفية المتكلم تأديته الكلام في سمته. [ت] والثالث استدراج السامعين؛ وهذا الذي هو عمود وتثبيت فإنه قد يكون نحو الغرض نفسه، وقد يكون نحو تقرير شيء من الأبواب الأخر. فيكون عمودًا وتثبيتا في ذلك الباب كما يبين المرء فضيلة نفسه أو خسيسة خصمه، أو يبين وجوب الرحمة عليه؛ فهذا يدخل في القسم الأول غير أن سمت القائل في أكثر الأمر إنما يعني في المحاورات التي تكون في أمور وقعت كما يكون في الشكاية والاعتذار، وكما يكون في المدح والذم. وأما إذا حاول إقناعا في ممكن استقبالي فنفيس سمته وصلاحه لا يدل على صدقه؛ لأن السمت فضيلة ما غير فضيلة العلم؛ وليس إذا حسن سمته ودل على فضيلة دل ذلك على إصابة رأيه في الأمور الغائبة، فاعتبر بما عرفت في سائر الأمور.
And it is a faculty for persuasion of the possible, but persuasion for the physician is accidental and insofar as he performs the act of the orator, yet he is not thereby an orator. How so? And he does not have a faculty to persuade in everything; rather the orator is an orator by this faculty, not by its acts that issue from it in specific matters. Then the rhetorical assents may be artificial, and may not be; and these are not like issues due to the existence of that which calls to it. As for the artificial ones, they are either from our thought and choice, so we derive the rhetorical topics and types, and we know the arrangement of the rhetorical syllogism and what pertains to it, this from the side of principle. As for the side of use, some of them may be prepared for us beforehand, and they are the premises that are called in this book 'topics'; they are the premises from whose expression the parts of a syllogism in potency or act are made. And some are not prepared, but in them are prepared principles and rules which, if we know them, we extract from them at the time of discussion rhetorical premises. And those rules are called in this book 'types'. Thus one continues to proceed from a continuous conclusion in a gradual way until obtaining the goal. Example: if it was easy for us when acquiring this power how the judgment transfers from one opposite to the opposite by way of persuasion. Then we addressed by indicating, we did that if Zayd, who deserves your ill-treatment, then Amr, who is your friend, deserves your kindness; we had extracted this from a rule prepared for us. And the artificial assents for which one speaks, and that speech is persuasive due to a situation or a custom, are three types: [1] one of them is the pillar which is called 'tathbit' in this book. [2] The second is concerning how the speaker delivers the speech in his demeanor. [3] The third is the gradual drawing of the listeners. And this which is the pillar and confirmation may be towards the goal itself, or it may be towards the establishment of something from other chapters. So it becomes a pillar and confirmation in that chapter, as when a person shows his own virtue or the baseness of his opponent, or shows the obligation of mercy upon him; this enters the first division, except that the speaker's demeanor in most cases is only significant in discussions that occur in past matters, as in complaint and apology, and as in praise and blame. But if he attempts persuasion concerning a future possibility, then his fine demeanor and goodness do not indicate his truthfulness; because demeanor is a virtue other than the virtue of knowledge; and if his demeanor is good and indicates virtue, that does not indicate the correctness of his opinion in absent matters. So consider what you know in other matters.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of rhetorical discourse and its types. It begins by distinguishing the orator's faculty: it is a power for persuasion in possible matters, but a physician may perform the act of an orator accidentally without being one. The orator is defined by the faculty, not by particular acts. Then it classifies rhetorical assents (tasdiqat khatabiyya) into artificial (sina'i) and non-artificial. Artificial assents come from our own thought: we derive topics (mawadi') and types (anwa') which are the premises and rules for constructing rhetorical syllogisms. Some premises are pre-prepared (called 'topics'), while others are generated from principles at the time of discussion (called 'types'). The author gives an example: if one knows how to transfer a judgment from one opposite to another persuasively, and uses a rule like 'if Zayd deserves ill, then his friend 'Amr deserves good', this is an extracted premise. Then the three types of artificial persuasive speech are enumerated: (1) 'al-'amud' or 'tathbit' (the pillar/confirmation) – addressing the goal itself or establishing something else; (2) the speaker's demeanor (samat) – how he delivers the speech; (3) drawing the listener gradually (istidraj). The passage explains that the first type (tathbit) includes showing one's own virtue or an opponent's vice, or establishing a claim like mercy. The second type (demeanor) is primarily used in past matters (complaint, apology, praise, blame). For future possibilities, a good demeanor does not guarantee truthfulness because demeanor is a separate virtue from knowledge.

Tathbit and Its Subcategories in Rhetorical Logic
Author defines tathbit and its subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tathbit and distinguish its four subcategories (damir, i'tibar, burhan, tafkir) by their aspects.

Core Idea
Tathbit is the umbrella term for rhetorical arguments; its subcategories differ by structure and perspective.

This section lays out the terminology of rhetorical argument in Islamic logic. The author introduces tathbit as the pillar of persuasion and then distinguishes between damir (enthymeme), i'tibar (analogy), burhan (quick inference), and tafkir (thought-oriented enthymeme). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analyzing how Islamic scholars crafted persuasive arguments in khutbahs and debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching term
Find 'tathbit' in the Arabic and note its definition: a statement intended to persuade assent to the desired claim itself. This is the genus of all rhetorical arguments.
Tathbit is not a specific argument type but a category encompassing all persuasive statements.
2
List the four subcategories
Extract the terms: damir (syllogism with missing premise), i'tibar (analogy), burhan (a quick i'tibar), and tafkir (damir considered from the perspective of the middle term requiring thought).
Burhan here is not the certain demonstration (burhan haqiqi) but a specialized usage in this text.
3
Differentiate damir and i'tibar
Damir is a qiyas (syllogism) that is incomplete; i'tibar is tamthil (analogy). Note that damir relies on deduction while i'tibar relies on similarity.
This parallels the distinction between syllogism and induction in dialectic.
4
Understand burhan and tafkir
Burhan is defined as an i'tibar that achieves the goal quickly. Tafkir and damir are the same in subject matter but differ in aspect: tafkir emphasizes the middle term requiring thought, damir emphasizes the missing premise.
Tafkir and damir are two perspectives on the same incomplete syllogism.
5
Summarize the relationships
All four are types of tathbit. Damir and tafkir are two faces of enthymeme; i'tibar and burhan relate to analogy. This classification is not exhaustive but tailored to rhetoric.
The author compares rhetoric to dialectic: just as dialectic has syllogism and induction, rhetoric has damir (enthymeme) and i'tibar (analogy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في العمود وهو التثبيت وأقسامه فنقول يقال تثبيت وتفكر وضمير واعتبار وبرهان وبينها فرق، فالتثبيت هو قول يراد به إقناع التصديق بالمطلوب نفسه وهو يعم جميع ذلك لكن الضمير هو ما كان منه قياسًا، والاعتبار ما كان تمثيلا. واسم البرهان في هذا الكتاب يقع على اعتبار يتم به المقصود سريعا. والتفكير هو الضمير بعينه في الموضوع ولكن من حيث إن يعتبر بالحد الأوسط وهو أن يؤخذ فيه وسط يقتضيه الفكر هو تفكير، ومن حيث فيه نقصان مقدمة وهو ضمير؛ فيكون التفكير والضمير واحدًا بالموضوع. وكما أن الجدل مقولة على قياس واستقراء كذلك الخطابة مقولها على ضمير وتمثيل. وكل ذلك إما أصلي وإما مظنون. وقد يكون في البيانات البرهانية ضمائر قد حذفت كبرياتها، وتكون تلك الضمائر البرهانية في قوة القياسات فإن كبرياتها إنما تحذف لوضوحها، وعلى سبيل الاختصار. وكذلك في الجدل الذي ليس على سبيل المغالطات. وأما الخطابة فإنما تحذف الكبريات فيها لأنه لو صرّح بها لزال الإقناع لأن تلك الأحكام إذا كانت حاضرت بالكلية علم كذبها وخصوصا في المستويات.
Section: On the pillar, which is tathbit, and its divisions. We say: tathbit, tafkir, damir, i‘tibar, and burhan are said, and between them there is a difference. Tathbit is a statement by which persuasion to assent to the desired thing itself is intended, and it encompasses all that. But damir is what is a syllogism from it, and i‘tibar is what is an analogy. The name burhan in this book applies to an i‘tibar by which the purpose is quickly achieved. Tafkir is the very same as damir in subject matter, but insofar as one considers the middle term—that is, a middle term is taken that demands thought—it is tafkir, and insofar as it is a premiss missing, it is damir. Thus tafkir and damir are one in subject matter. And just as jadal is predicated of syllogism and induction, likewise rhetoric is predicated of damir and analogy. And all of that is either original or conjectural. And there may occur in demonstrative clarifications damirs whose major premises have been omitted, and those demonstrative damirs are in the strength of syllogisms, for their major premises are omitted only due to their clarity and for brevity. Similarly in jadal that is not of the sophistical kind. As for rhetoric, the major premises are omitted therein because if they were stated explicitly, persuasion would cease, because those judgments, if entirely present, their falsity would be known, especially in the accepted premises (al-mustawiyat).
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This section is a definitional passage that clarifies the terminology around 'tathbit' (confirmation), the pillar of rhetorical argument. The author explains that several terms—tathbit, tafkir, damir, i‘tibar, burhan—are used, but they have distinct meanings. Tathbit is the overarching concept: any statement intended to persuade assent to the desired claim itself. Under it, damir refers to a syllogism (qiyas) that is incomplete; i‘tibar refers to analogy (tamthil). Burhan in this book is defined as an i‘tibar (analogy or inference) that achieves the goal quickly. Tafkir and damir are the same in subject matter but differ in aspect: tafkir focuses on the middle term as requiring thought, while damir focuses on the missing premise. The author then compares rhetoric to dialectic: just as dialectic (jadal) includes syllogism and induction, rhetoric includes damir and analogy. All these may be certain (asli) or conjectural (maznun). Finally, the text discusses the omission of major premises: in demonstrative (burhani) contexts, major premises are omitted for clarity and brevity, and the resulting damirs are equivalent to full syllogisms. In dialectic, similarly, omission occurs when not fallacious. In rhetoric, however, major premises are omitted because stating them explicitly would reveal their falsity and destroy persuasion, especially in the case of widely accepted premises (mustawiyat).

ضمير
اعتبار
برهان
تفكير

C) Tafkir is used only in dialectic, while damir is used only in rhetoric.
D) Tafkir is always certain, while damir is always conjectural.

Key Takeaway
Tathbit is the generic term for rhetorical persuasion; its subspecies are distinguished by whether they are syllogistic (damir/tafkir) or analogical (i'tibar/burhan).

Why Major Premises Are Omitted: Rhetoric, Dialectic, and Demonstration
Author discusses omission of major premises in different argumentative contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why major premises are omitted in demonstrative, dialectical, and rhetorical arguments, and identify the rhetorical reason.

Core Idea
In rhetoric, stating the major premise explicitly would reveal its weakness and destroy persuasion.

This passage delves into the practical reasons behind omitting major premises in different types of arguments. The author distinguishes three contexts: demonstrative (burhani), where omission is for brevity; dialectical (jadali), where omission avoids sophistry; and rhetorical (khatabi), where omission is necessary because stating the premise would expose its falsity, especially in widely accepted premises (mustawiyat). Understanding this helps us appreciate the craft of persuasion in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three contexts
Find in the Arabic the three contexts: demonstrative (برهانية), dialectical (جدل), and rhetorical (خطابة). Note that the passage mentions 'في البيانات البرهانية' and 'في الجدل' and 'الخطابة'.
The contexts are not named explicitly but are implied by the types of arguments.
2
Understand the reasons for omission in each
For demonstrative: major premises are omitted due to clarity and brevity (لوضوحها، وعلى سبيل الاختصار). For dialectical: omission occurs when the argument is not sophistical. For rhetorical: omission is necessary because stating the premise would make the conclusion lose persuasiveness (لو صرّح بها لزال الإقناع), since those premises are known to be false.
The last reason is the core of rhetorical strategy: hiding weak premises.
3
Distinguish the epistemic status
Demonstrative damirs are 'في قوة القياسات' (equivalent to full syllogisms) because the omitted premise is clear and true. Rhetorical damirs, by contrast, rely on premises that if fully stated would be seen as false.
This shows that omission does not necessarily weaken the argument in demonstration but does in rhetoric.
4
Relate to the concept of mustawiyat
The passage ends with a reference to 'المستويات' (al-mustawiyat), widely accepted premises. These are premises that are acknowledged but if articulated might reveal their lack of certainty.
This term may correspond to 'al-mashhurat' (received opinions) in other works.

Source Text

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are major premises omitted in rhetorical arguments?
A) Because the audience already knows them and they are self-evident.
B) Because stating them would reveal their falsity and undo persuasion.
C) Because the speaker wishes to be brief and concise.
D) Because the argument is meant to be a dialectical exercise.

Key Takeaway
Major premises are omitted in rhetoric precisely to hide their weakness, a strategy distinct from demonstrative or dialectical omission.

The Limits of Character-Based Persuasion
Author discusses using personal character to persuade in past-event dialogues and warns against relying on it for future claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a speaker's character (samt) can legitimately persuade and when it cannot, based on whether the claim concerns past events or future possibilities.

Core Idea
Character alone does not prove truth about unseen future matters.

In this passage, the author explains that while a speaker's virtuous demeanor can be a persuasive tool when discussing events that have already occurred, it is not a reliable indicator of truth when making claims about future or unseen matters. This lesson helps you recognize the proper scope of ethos in argumentation, especially within Islamic rhetorical traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two persuasive contexts
Find where the author distinguishes between dialogues about past events (e.g., complaint, praise) and attempts to persuade about future possibilities. Note that character is said to be effective only in the first context.
Past events: 'shikaya wa i'tidhar' (complaint and apology), 'madḥ wa dhamm' (praise and blame). Future: 'mumkin istiqbali' (future possibility).
2
Extract the reason for the limitation
Identify the author's argument: character (al-samt) is a virtue distinct from the virtue of knowledge ('ilm). Therefore, good character does not imply correct opinion ('iṣābat ra'y) about unseen matters (al-umur al-gha'iba).
Key phrase: 'al-samt faḍīla mā ghayr faḍīlat al-ʿilm' (character is a virtue other than the virtue of knowledge).
3
Apply the principle to a new case
Given a scenario from Islamic scholarly discourse, determine whether appealing to a speaker's character is sufficient to support a claim. For example, does a scholar's piety guarantee the correctness of his ijtihad on a future ruling? No, because character does not ensure knowledge.

Source Text

من الأبواب الأخر. فيكون عمودًا وتثبيتا في ذلك الباب كما يبين المرء فضيلة نفسه أو خسيسة خصمه، أو يبين وجوب الرحمة عليه؛ فهذا يدخل في القسم الأول غير أن سمت القائل في أكثر الأمر إنما يعني في المحاورات التي تكون في أمور وقعت كما يكون في الشكاية والاعتذار، وكما يكون في المدح والذم. وأما إذا حاول إقناعا في ممكن استقبالي فنفس سمته وصلاحه لا يدل على صدقه؛ لأن السمت فضيلة ما غير فضيلة العلم؛ وليس إذا حسن سمته ودل على فضيلة دل ذلك على إصابة رأيه في الأمور الغائبة، فاعتبر بما عرفت في سائر الأمور.
From the other sections. So it becomes a pillar and confirmation in that chapter, just as a person shows his own virtue or the baseness of his opponent, or shows the obligation of mercy upon him; this falls into the first division, except that the character of the speaker in most cases is only effective in dialogues that concern matters that have occurred, as in complaint and apology, and as in praise and blame. But if he attempts persuasion regarding a future possibility, then his character and integrity do not indicate his truthfulness, because character is a virtue other than the virtue of knowledge; and just because his character is good and indicates virtue does not indicate the correctness of his opinion about unseen matters. So consider by what you know in other matters.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage discusses the persuasive use of personal character (samt) in argumentation. The author explains that character can be effective when discussing past events, such as complaints, excuses, praise, or blame. However, when trying to persuade about future possibilities, good character alone does not guarantee the truth of one's claim. The reason given is that character is a virtue distinct from knowledge. Therefore, one should not assume that a person with good character holds correct opinions about unseen matters. This is a caution against relying on ethos rather than evidence.

C) Only in future possibilities
D) Only in scientific demonstrations

Key Takeaway
Character is a useful but limited persuasive tool; it applies to past events but does not guarantee truth about future possibilities.

Conditions for Using Praiseworthy Premises in Dialectic vs Rhetoric
Distinguishes dialectical vs rhetorical conditions for premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the conditions for using praiseworthy premises in dialectic and rhetoric and identify which condition is required for each.

Core Idea
Dialectic demands logical rigor; rhetoric only needs apparent praise.

The passage contrasts how the dialectician and the orator handle the same type of premises—praiseworthy premises—but under vastly different standards. The dialectician must satisfy all syllogistic conditions, while the orator only needs the premises to seem praiseworthy to the audience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arts contrasted
Look for the terms 'al-jadal' (dialectic) and 'al-khataba' (rhetoric) in the passage. They are discussed in relation to how they use praiseworthy premises.
Dialectic is also contrasted with the sciences ('al-ulum').
2
Extract conditions for dialectic
From the phrase 'على شرط المنطق' (upon the condition of logic) and subsequent details, list the requirements: premises must be true in predication, correct in composition, and arranged in syllogistic order actually or potentially.
These conditions ensure the argument is logically valid.
3
Extract conditions for rhetoric
Rhetoric only requires that the premises be 'praiseworthy in appearance' (محمودة في الظاهر), i.e., accepted by people according to their beliefs, either as obligatory or as considered.
No logical structure is mandatory; customary conventions suffice.
4
Contrast the two approaches
Summarize: the dialectician works under logical conditions; the orator works under customary conventions. This distinction affects how arguments are constructed and received.
This explains why an orator might avoid explicit logical terms.

And it may also be omitted so that the statement is not logical, because the orator, if he attributes to his speech (logical) or (theological), gives the impression that his proficiency in another craft always prevails due to the excellence of his power in logic, not due to the excellence of his correctness. So it is better that he addresses with a common speech. And just as the case of rhetoric in using the implicit premise is opposite to that of dialectic and the sciences, so too its benefit from using consideration; and syllogism is opposite to the case of dialectic and the sciences: for syllogism is more compelling in dialectic and more certain in the sciences than consideration and induction, but consideration in rhetoric is closer to convincing the public than the implicit premise, because the implicit premise and what runs the course of syllogism may tolerate much review in asking what the premises were. And such a question does not occur in matters that are apparently conceded. And the difference between example and induction has already passed; and example and implicit premise share that each of them yields persuasion, i.e., makes something not persuasive by itself persuasive. For every persuasive thing is either persuasive in itself, or persuasive in something else, but as long as it is not persuasive in itself, it is not persuasive in something else. And the persuasive in itself is the praiseworthy; and the praiseworthy may be according to some, and may be according to all people, and opinions differ according to inclinations. And such praiseworthy premises, even if they are suitable for use in many rhetorical syllogisms, they are not suitable to be made a pillar in the craft; for their states are infinite, and praiseworthy premises are needed in dialectic upon the condition of logic, meaning that a syllogism be composed from them with conditions, namely that the premises be true in predication and correct in composition, and upon the order of a syllogism either actually or potentially. As for rhetoric, it suffices in it that the premises be praiseworthy in appearance, i.e., that people see them not in a proper way but according to their own belief, either as obligatory or as considered. So among them are genuine praiseworthy premises, which are the praiseworthy at first glance, so the orator uses the genuine praiseworthy and the praiseworthy according to opinion. And the praiseworthy according to opinion are those that the public habitually accept before examination and scrutiny of them. The dialectician takes them as praiseworthy only because they are praiseworthy among the public, and the demonstrator also sees them as praiseworthy, not doubting that they are praiseworthy, but they only doubt whether they are true. And the opinionative ones, if scrutinized, their praiseworthiness disappears, not only due to the appearance of falsehood but for example due to the Law. So the orator, even if he uses the genuine praiseworthy, he uses it only from the aspect that it is praiseworthy in appearance. So it is clear that the dialectician deals with praiseworthy premises according to the conditions of logic, and the orator deals with them according to customary conventions, rather the customary conventions in the matter of the syllogism and its form at that time. And it is not necessary that the pondering and implicit premises be about possibilities equally; rather they may be about necessities and about the more frequent. And the implicit premises present in each of them may be from the true ones, i.e., from the genuine praiseworthy, and may be from proofs. An example of what is from the predicates in necessities is our saying: Zayd is a scholar with a pure soul, and the scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife. And this praiseworthy premise may be omitted and its force used. And it is only expressed as a universal premise; lest the universal predication qua universal predication be explicitly stated. And this is among what is composed on the pattern of the first figure. And an example of what is from the proof by specific division is our saying: This woman gave birth, so she is the one who weaned. So he made giving birth a proof for weaning, and it is a true proof; then it follows that it is with it or more specific than it, and therefore it is upon the force of the first figure. As for the sign, it is a judgment either that the predicate is necessary for it and it is not necessary for the subject, or it is necessary for the subject and the predicate is not necessary for it. For if the predicate is necessary for it and it is necessary for the subject, it is a proof, so the first figure is formed. The first of these two signs is clarified by the third figure, like our saying: The jurist is virtuous, because Zayd the jurist is virtuous. And truth in this...
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage examines the distinctions between rhetoric (khataba), dialectic (jadal), and demonstration (burhan) in their use of premises, particularly focusing on what are called 'praiseworthy premises' (al-mahmudat). The author begins by noting that an orator may avoid explicit logical terminology to prevent the audience from thinking his power comes from logic rather than correctness of his argument. He then contrasts rhetoric with dialectic and the sciences: in rhetoric, the implicit premise (damir) is used inversely, and 'consideration' (i'tibar) is more effective for convincing the public than the implicit premise, because the latter invites scrutiny of the premises. The difference between example (mithal) and induction (istiqra') was already covered. Example and implicit premise share the feature of making something persuasive that was not persuasive in itself. The persuasive in itself is the praiseworthy, which may be praiseworthy to some or to all. Such praiseworthy premises, though usable in rhetoric, cannot be foundational in the craft because their states are infinite. In dialectic, praiseworthy premises must meet logical conditions: true predication, correct composition, and syllogistic form actually or potentially. In rhetoric, it suffices that premises appear praiseworthy, either as obligatory or as considered. Some praiseworthy premises are genuine (at first glance) and some are opinionative (accepted without scrutiny by the public). The dialectician uses them because they are publicly esteemed; the demonstrator also finds them praiseworthy but doubts their truth. Opinionative ones lose their praiseworthiness upon scrutiny, sometimes due to conflict with Sharia. The orator uses genuine praiseworthy only as apparently praiseworthy. Thus, the dialectician works under logical conditions, the orator under customary conventions. Implicit premises may be about necessities or frequents, and may be from truths or proofs. Two examples: one from necessities: 'Zayd is a scholar with a pure soul; the scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife' — this forms a first-figure syllogism where the universal premise may be omitted to avoid explicit universal predication. The second example is from proof by specific division: 'This woman gave birth, so she is the one who weaned' — making birth a proof for weaning, which follows the force of the first figure. Then the author discusses the 'sign' ('alama): either the predicate is necessary for it and it not necessary for the subject, or it is necessary for the subject and the predicate not necessary for it. If both necessities hold, it is a proof forming the first figure. The first type of sign is clarified by the third figure, e.g., 'The jurist is virtuous because Zayd the jurist is virtuous.' The passage ends mid-sentence, discussing truth in this context.

Key Terms
المحمودات
الجدل
الخطابة
الضمير
العلامة

D) To save time and effort.

Key Takeaway
Dialectic requires praiseworthy premises to meet logical conditions; rhetoric only requires apparent praiseworthiness.

Types of Praiseworthy Premises: Genuine vs Opinionative
Classifies praiseworthy premises into genuine and opinionative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between genuine and opinionative praiseworthy premises and explain how each is treated by the dialectician and the orator.

Core Idea
Genuine premises are praiseworthy at first glance; opinionative premises lose their praise upon scrutiny.

Not all praiseworthy premises are equal. The passage identifies two kinds: genuine (mahnuda haqiqiyya) and opinionative (al-mahmudat bi-hasb al-zann). The dialectician uses them as publicly esteemed; the demonstrator doubts their truth; the orator uses them as apparent.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types
Locate in the passage: 'محمودات حقيقية وهي المحمودات في بادي الرأي' (genuine praiseworthy at first glance) and 'المحمودات بحسب الظن' (opinionative praiseworthy accepted without scrutiny).
The opinionative are those the public habitually accept before examination.
2
Identify how each is used by different practitioners
The dialectician takes them as praiseworthy because they are publicly esteemed. The demonstrator also sees them as praiseworthy but doubts their truth. The orator uses the genuine only as apparent praiseworthiness.
The opinionative ones lose their praiseworthiness upon scrutiny, sometimes due to Sharia.
3
Explain the reason for the difference
Opinionative premises may be based on custom or unexamined belief; genuine ones are immediately evident. The dialectician and demonstrator have different relationships to truth.
The passage notes that scrutiny can reveal falsehood or conflict with divine law.
4
Apply the distinction to a real case
Consider a premise like 'The sun rises every day' – it is genuine praiseworthy because it is universally accepted at first glance. A premise like 'Prayer must be performed in Arabic' might be opinionative for some groups who accept it without logical proof.
This distinction affects how arguments are built in rhetoric vs dialectic.
