بواجب، / وإما باعتبار. فمن ذلك محمودات حقيقية وهي المحمودات في بادي الرأي فالخطابي يستعمل المحمود الحقيقي والمحمود بحسب الظن. والمحمودات بحسب الظنِّ هي التي من شأن الجمهور أن يكون تسلموها قبل النظر فيها والتعقب لها. والجدلي إنما يأخذها محمودة لأنها عند الجمهور محمودة والبرهاني أيضًا يرونها محمودة لا يشكون في أنها محمودة لكنهم إنما يشكون في أنها صادقة والمظنونة منها ما إذا تعقبت زال حمدها لا لأجل ظهور الكذب فقط بل لأجل الشريعة مثلاً. فيكون الخطابي وإن استعمل المحمود الحقيقي فإنما يستعمله من جهة أنه محمود في الظاهر؛ فقد بان إذن أن الجدلي يتصرف في المحمودات على شروط المنطق، والخطابي يتصرف فيها على الرسوم المعتادة بل الرسوم المعتادة في مادة القياس وفي صورته حينئذ. ولا يلزم أن تكون التفكيرات والضمائر عن ممكنات بالتساوي بل قد تكون عن ضروريات وعن أكثريات، والضمائر الموجودة في كل واحد منهما قد تكون من الصادقات أي من المحمودات الحقيقية، وقد تكون من الدلائل: فمثال ما يكون من المحمولات في الضروريات قولنا زيد عالم زكي النفس، والعالم الزكي النفس سعيد في الآخرة؛ وهذه المقدمة المحمودة قد تحذف وتستعمل قوتها. وإنما تصرّح بها مهملة؛ لئلا يكون المقول على الكل من حيث هو مقول على الكل مصرَّحًا به ؛ وهذا من جملة ما يكون تأليفه على نهج الشكل الأول ومثال ما هو من الدليل بالقسمة الخاصة قولنا هذه المرأة ولدت فهي المقتصة؛ فجعل الولادة دليلا على الاقتصاص وهو دليل صدق؛ فيلزم أن يكون معه أو أخص منه، ولذلك يكون على قوة الشكل الأول. وأما العلامة فهي حكم إما أن يكون المحمول يلزمه وهو لا يلزم الموضوع أو هو يلزم الموضوع، والمحمول لا يلزمه. فإنه إذا لزمه المحمول ويلزم هو الموضوع كان دليلا فانعقد الشكل الأول. فالعلامة الأولى منهما تتبين بالشكل الثالث كقولنا الفقيه عفيف لأن زيدًا الفقيه عفيف؛ والصدق في هذا
And it may also be omitted so that the statement is not logical, because the orator, if he attributes to his speech (logical) or (theological), gives the impression that his proficiency in another craft always prevails due to the excellence of his power in logic, not due to the excellence of his correctness. So it is better that he addresses with a common speech. And just as the case of rhetoric in using the implicit premise is opposite to that of dialectic and the sciences, so too its benefit from using consideration; and syllogism is opposite to the case of dialectic and the sciences: for syllogism is more compelling in dialectic and more certain in the sciences than consideration and induction, but consideration in rhetoric is closer to convincing the public than the implicit premise, because the implicit premise and what runs the course of syllogism may tolerate much review in asking what the premises were. And such a question does not occur in matters that are apparently conceded. And the difference between example and induction has already passed; and example and implicit premise share that each of them yields persuasion, i.e., makes something not persuasive by itself persuasive. For every persuasive thing is either persuasive in itself, or persuasive in something else, but as long as it is not persuasive in itself, it is not persuasive in something else. And the persuasive in itself is the praiseworthy; and the praiseworthy may be according to some, and may be according to all people, and opinions differ according to inclinations. And such praiseworthy premises, even if they are suitable for use in many rhetorical syllogisms, they are not suitable to be made a pillar in the craft; for their states are infinite, and praiseworthy premises are needed in dialectic upon the condition of logic, meaning that a syllogism be composed from them with conditions, namely that the premises be true in predication and correct in composition, and upon the order of a syllogism either actually or potentially. As for rhetoric, it suffices in it that the premises be praiseworthy in appearance, i.e., that people see them not in a proper way but according to their own belief, either as obligatory or as considered. So among them are genuine praiseworthy premises, which are the praiseworthy at first glance, so the orator uses the genuine praiseworthy and the praiseworthy according to opinion. And the praiseworthy according to opinion are those that the public habitually accept before examination and scrutiny of them. The dialectician takes them as praiseworthy only because they are praiseworthy among the public, and the demonstrator also sees them as praiseworthy, not doubting that they are praiseworthy, but they only doubt whether they are true. And the opinionative ones, if scrutinized, their praiseworthiness disappears, not only due to the appearance of falsehood but for example due to the Law. So the orator, even if he uses the genuine praiseworthy, he uses it only from the aspect that it is praiseworthy in appearance. So it is clear that the dialectician deals with praiseworthy premises according to the conditions of logic, and the orator deals with them according to customary conventions, rather the customary conventions in the matter of the syllogism and its form at that time. And it is not necessary that the pondering and implicit premises be about possibilities equally; rather they may be about necessities and about the more frequent. And the implicit premises present in each of them may be from the true ones, i.e., from the genuine praiseworthy, and may be from proofs. An example of what is from the predicates in necessities is our saying: Zayd is a scholar with a pure soul, and the scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife. And this praiseworthy premise may be omitted and its force used. And it is only expressed as a universal premise; lest the universal predication qua universal predication be explicitly stated. And this is among what is composed on the pattern of the first figure. And an example of what is from the proof by specific division is our saying: This woman gave birth, so she is the one who weaned. So he made giving birth a proof for weaning, and it is a true proof; then it follows that it is with it or more specific than it, and therefore it is upon the force of the first figure. As for the sign, it is a judgment either that the predicate is necessary for it and it is not necessary for the subject, or it is necessary for the subject and the predicate is not necessary for it. For if the predicate is necessary for it and it is necessary for the subject, it is a proof, so the first figure is formed. The first of these two signs is clarified by the third figure, like our saying: The jurist is virtuous, because Zayd the jurist is virtuous. And truth in this...
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage examines the distinctions between rhetoric (khataba), dialectic (jadal), and demonstration (burhan) in their use of premises, particularly focusing on what are called 'praiseworthy premises' (al-mahmudat). The author begins by noting that an orator may avoid explicit logical terminology to prevent the audience from thinking his power comes from logic rather than correctness of his argument. He then contrasts rhetoric with dialectic and the sciences: in rhetoric, the implicit premise (damir) is used inversely, and 'consideration' (i'tibar) is more effective for convincing the public than the implicit premise, because the latter invites scrutiny of the premises. The difference between example (mithal) and induction (istiqra') was already covered. Example and implicit premise share the feature of making something persuasive that was not persuasive in itself. The persuasive in itself is the praiseworthy, which may be praiseworthy to some or to all. Such praiseworthy premises, though usable in rhetoric, cannot be foundational in the craft because their states are infinite. In dialectic, praiseworthy premises must meet logical conditions: true predication, correct composition, and syllogistic form actually or potentially. In rhetoric, it suffices that premises appear praiseworthy, either as obligatory or as considered. Some praiseworthy premises are genuine (at first glance) and some are opinionative (accepted without scrutiny by the public). The dialectician uses them because they are publicly esteemed; the demonstrator also finds them praiseworthy but doubts their truth. Opinionative ones lose their praiseworthiness upon scrutiny, sometimes due to conflict with Sharia. The orator uses genuine praiseworthy only as apparently praiseworthy. Thus, the dialectician works under logical conditions, the orator under customary conventions. Implicit premises may be about necessities or frequents, and may be from truths or proofs. Two examples: one from necessities: 'Zayd is a scholar with a pure soul; the scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife' — this forms a first-figure syllogism where the universal premise may be omitted to avoid explicit universal predication. The second example is from proof by specific division: 'This woman gave birth, so she is the one who weaned' — making birth a proof for weaning, which follows the force of the first figure. Then the author discusses the 'sign' ('alama): either the predicate is necessary for it and it not necessary for the subject, or it is necessary for the subject and the predicate not necessary for it. If both necessities hold, it is a proof forming the first figure. The first type of sign is clarified by the third figure, e.g., 'The jurist is virtuous because Zayd the jurist is virtuous.' The passage ends mid-sentence, discussing truth in this context.

Key Terms

الجدلي
البرهاني
الخطابي

Checkpoint
What happens to opinionative praiseworthy premises when scrutinized?
A) They become more persuasive.
B) Their praiseworthiness disappears, possibly due to falsehood or conflict with Sharia.
C) They remain praiseworthy because the public continues to accept them.
D) They are replaced by genuine premises.

Key Takeaway
Praiseworthy premises are either genuine (self-evident) or opinionative (accepted without scrutiny), and they serve different roles in rhetoric and dialectic.

Rhetoric -- Khitaba

Syllogisms in Rhetoric: First Figure and Sign
Provides syllogistic examples for rhetoric, including first figure and sign

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of first-figure syllogisms and sign arguments used in rhetoric, and reconstruct the implicit premises in the examples provided in the passage.

Core Idea
Rhetoric uses first-figure syllogisms with omitted universal premises to avoid explicit logical form.

The passage provides concrete examples of how syllogisms are employed in rhetoric, often with implicit premises. Two examples illustrate the first figure: one about a scholar and the afterlife, another about a woman who gave birth. The author also discusses the sign (‘alama) and how it can form a third-figure argument.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the first example: the scholar
Find the example: 'زيد عالم زكي النفس، والعالم الزكي النفس سعيد في الآخرة' (Zayd is a scholar with a pure soul; the scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife). The conclusion is implied: Zayd is happy in the afterlife.
The universal premise 'Every scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife' is omitted but its force is used.
2
Identify the figure and mood
This is a first-figure syllogism (Barbara): middle term = 'scholar with pure soul'; major term = 'happy in afterlife'; minor term = 'Zayd'. The omitted premise is the major premise.
The passage says it is 'على نهج الشكل الأول' (on the pattern of the first figure).
3
Analyze the second example: the woman giving birth
Find: 'هذه المرأة ولدت فهي المقتصة' (This woman gave birth, so she is the one who weaned). The omitted premise is something like 'Every woman who gave birth is the one who weaned' or 'All women who give birth wean their children'.
This is a proof (dail) that also follows the force of the first figure.
4
Understand the sign and the third figure
The sign (‘alama) is defined: either the predicate is necessary for it and it not necessary for the subject, or the reverse. If both necessities hold, it forms a first-figure proof. The first type is clarified by the third figure: e.g., 'The jurist is virtuous because Zayd the jurist is virtuous' – a third-figure syllogism (the middle term 'Zayd' is subject of both premises).
This example shows how a particular instance can serve as a sign for a universal claim.
5
Reconstruct the third-figure syllogism
The example: 'Zayd is a jurist, Zayd is virtuous, therefore some jurist (or all jurists?) are virtuous?' Actually, the passage says 'الفقه عفيف لأن زيدًا الفقيه عفيف' – the conclusion seems to be that the jurist is virtuous based on Zayd. In third figure, the minor premise is particular, so the conclusion is particular: 'Some jurist is virtuous' but the example uses a singular term. This requires careful analysis.
The passage is incomplete, but it shows how sign reasoning operates.

Commentary
This passage examines the distinctions between rhetoric (khataba), dialectic (jadal), and demonstration (burhan) in their use of premises, particularly focusing on what are called 'praiseworthy premises' (al-mahmudat). The author begins by noting that an orator may avoid explicit logical terminology to prevent the audience from thinking his power comes from logic rather than correctness of his argument. He then contrasts rhetoric with dialectic and the sciences: in rhetoric, the implicit premise (damir) is used inversely, and 'consideration' (i'tibar) is more effective for convincing the public than the implicit premise, because the latter invites scrutiny of the premises. The difference between example (mithal) and induction (istiqra') was already covered. Example and implicit premise share the feature of making something persuasive that was not persuasive in itself. The persuasive in itself is the praiseworthy, which may be praiseworthy to some or to all. Such praiseworthy premises, though usable in rhetoric, cannot be foundational in the craft because their states are infinite. In dialectic, praiseworthy premises must meet logical conditions: true predication, correct composition, and syllogistic form actually or potentially. In rhetoric, it suffices that premises appear praiseworthy, either as obligatory or as considered. Some praiseworthy premises are genuine (at first glance) and some are opinionative (accepted without scrutiny by the public). The dialectician uses them because they are publicly esteemed; the demonstrator also finds them praiseworthy but doubts their truth. Opinionative ones lose their praiseworthiness upon scrutiny, sometimes due to conflict with Sharia. The orator uses genuine praiseworthy only as apparently praiseworthy. Thus, the dialectician works under logical conditions, the orator under customary conventions. Implicit premises may be about necessities or frequents, and may be from truths or proofs. Two examples: one from necessities: 'Zayd is a scholar with a pure soul; the scholar with a pure soul is happy in the afterlife' — this forms a first-figure syllogism where the universal premise may be omitted to avoid explicit universal predication. The second example is from proof by specific division: 'This woman gave birth, so she is the one who weaned' — making birth a proof for weaning, which follows the force of the first figure. Then the author discusses the 'sign' ('alama): either the predicate is necessary for it and it not necessary for the subject, or it is necessary for the subject and the predicate not necessary for it. If both necessities hold, it is a proof forming the first figure. The first type of sign is clarified by the third figure, e.g., 'The jurist is virtuous because Zayd the jurist is virtuous.' The passage ends mid-sentence, discussing truth in this context.

Deliberative Rhetoric (Mashwara)
Defines deliberative rhetoric and explains its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define deliberative rhetoric (mashwara) and identify its key characteristics: temporal focus on the future, purpose of persuading about benefit or harm, and its connection to possibilities.

Core Idea
Deliberative rhetoric moves the will towards future possibilities, persuading to act or avoid for benefit or harm.

In this passage, the author distinguishes three types of rhetorical discourse. Here we focus on the first: deliberative rhetoric (mashwara). It is directed towards future actions and aims to persuade an audience whether something should be done for its benefit or avoided for its harm. The passage also clarifies that the persuasion does not require actual benefit—only apparent benefit is sufficient. This type of rhetoric is concerned with possibilities, not necessities, and it serves as a prelude to voluntary action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term for deliberative rhetoric in Arabic
Find the word 'مشورة' (mashwara) in the passage and note its root meaning of consultation or deliberation.
2
Extract the purpose
Read the definition: 'مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في أن كذا ينبغي أن يفعل لنفعه أو أن لا يفعل لضره' – a discourse intended to persuade that something should be done for its benefit or not done for its harm.
The purpose is future-oriented, focusing on benefit (نفع) and harm (ضر).
3
Note the qualification about real vs. apparent benefit
Observe that the passage says deliberation remains deliberation even if the benefit is not real, as long as it appears beneficial. This distinguishes it from a mere factual statement.
The orator can persuade about apparent benefit.
4
Understand the prerequisite of possibility
The passage states deliberation is only about possible matters, not necessary ones: 'المشورة يتوجه نحو الممكنات'. This is crucial for distinguishing it from other discourses.
Deliberation is a prelude to voluntary action.
5
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Consider a khutbah that encourages charity by arguing it brings blessings; classify it as deliberative rhetoric because it persuades about future benefit.
Use the tool to analyze real Islamic oratory.

فصل: فيما يتعلّق به الخطابة من الأعراض الأولية وغير ذلك. فنقول: إن المنازعة في كون الشيء ولا لكونه منازعة عامة لجميع الأنواع الخطابية؛ فإذا رجع إلى التفصيل والتخصيص فأكثر أصناف المحاورات العامية في الأمور الجزئية يرجع إلى ما فيه خير أو شر. والجزئيات إما مستقبلة وإما واقعة؛ ويبعد أن يقع للجمهور منازعة في جزئي مستقبل واقع بالطبع والاتفاق هل هو خير أو شر؛ فإن هذا النحو من النظر بأهل العلوم أولى بل إن تنازعوا في هذا تنازعوا. وهم يشيرون بالتحرز عنه إن كان شرا، أو التوقع إن كان خيرا. وأما الأمور الواقعة من الخير والشر فإما أن يراد إثبات وجود هذا الخير للإنسان، أو هذا الشر له فقط؛ وهذه هي المفاوضة التي يمدح فيها أو يدم. ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام في المعدوم من الخير والشر مما نقل؛ وإنما يمدح ويذم في أكثر الأمر ما هو موجود خاص فيكون أولى لأزمنة لموضوعات هذه المفاوضة هو الزمان الحاضر. وأما أن وجود هذا أو ذلك من إنسان آخر بإرادته؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون للخطيب تفاوض إنسانًا في أن خيرًا وصل إليه منه، أو من إنسان آخر. وليس مع الاعتراف تشاجر وتنازع البتة؛ فقصارى ذلك محاورة في الشكر. وإن كان هناك تنازع فذلك إما من باب المدح والذم، وإما من باب الإثبات والإبطال؛ وهذه منازعة عامة. فإذا جعل بدل الخير شر كانت للمفاوضة جارية على سبيل شكاية واعتذار ؛ وذلك في أمر ماض لا محالة. فإذن المفاوضات الخطابية ثلاثة: [أ] مشاورية، [ب] ومنافرية، جميع [ت] ومشاجرية؛ وكذلك السامعون ثلاثة : [1] خصم [ب] وحاكم بإقناع أحدهما، [ت] ونظار . أما الحاكم في الأمور المستقبلة فهو الرئيس المدبر لأمر الجماعة، وفي الواقعة يكون كالمتوسط الموثوق / تفحصه. وأما النظار فينظرون في قوة أحدهما وضعف الآخر ليس إليهم غير ذلك. ثم لكل واحد من هذه المخاطبات غرض خاص؛ [۱] أما المشورة فهي مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في أن كذا ينبغي أن يفعل لنفعه أو أن لا يفعل لضره. [٢] وأما المنافرة فهي مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في مدح شيء بفضله أو ذمه بنقيضه. [٣] وأما المشاجرة فهي مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في شكاية ظلم أو اعتذار بأنه لا ظلم وربَّما لم تقع منازعته كون الأمر نفسه ولكن في كونه نافعا أو غير نافع أو ظلمًا أو غير ظلم مثلا . والمشورة ليست مشورة بسبب إقناعها في أمر هو نافع بالحقيقة فإنه ربَّما لم يكن نافعا بالحقيقة ولا عند المشير لكنه إن ظهر أنه نافع حاول الإقناع فيه؛ فيكون المخاطبة مع ذلك مشورة. وربَّما كانت المشورة بالجميل الذي نفعه من جهة أخرى لا بالنافع. وكذلك المدح والذم بل ربَّما كان المدح مدحا بالضار كما يكون في اقتحام الضرر والأذى للذكر الجميل. ولما كان القياس الخطابي في جميع هذه الوجوه يقتضي تقدم قضية وهي مأخوذة المحمودات أو دليل أو علامة فكل واحد من هذه متقدمة وهي بعينها مكان القياس؛ فالقياس المطلق مما يكون بالإطلاق والمقيد نحو التفكير مما له تقييد بوجه خاص ولما كان الضروري من جملة ما لا يطلب ولا يهرب فلا مشورة فيه بل المشورة يتوجه نحو الممكنات. فالخطيب المشير ينبغي أن يكون عنده مقدمات في إثبات أن الأمر ممكن أو غير ممكن، وفي أنه هل يكون أو لا يكون؛ إذ المشورة قول يراد به التحريك الإرادي نحو ما يكتسب بالإرادة من الخير والشر وهي مقدمة للعمل الإرادي لما أنها مخصوصة بالممكنات؛ فهذا هو الأمر العام لما ينحو المشورة. ثم هنا أنواع خاصة لا بد من الإشارة إليها فنقول: إن الأمور التي هي أقسام المشورة الخطيرة جدا دون الجزئيات خمسة؛ [أ] العدة والحرب والسلم، [ب]
Chapter: On what pertains to rhetoric from primary accidents and other things. We say: The dispute about whether something is or is not a thing is a general dispute for all rhetorical types. When returning to detail and specification, most types of common dialogues in particular matters revert to what contains good or evil. Particulars are either future or occurring. It is unlikely that the public would dispute about a particular future thing that occurs naturally and by chance whether it is good or evil, for this kind of investigation is more appropriate for the people of the sciences. Rather, if they dispute about this, they dispute. They indicate caution if it is evil, or expectation if it is good. As for occurring matters of good and evil, either it is intended to prove the existence of this good for a person, or this evil for him alone; and this is the dialogue in which one praises or blames. There is no speech about it, rather speech is about non-existent good and evil that is reported. One praises and blames in most cases what is specific and existent, so the time most appropriate for the subjects of this dialogue is the present time. As for the existence of this or that from another person by his will, then either the orator dialogues with a person about a good that reached him from him, or from another person. With acknowledgment, there is no quarrel or dispute at all; the most that is is a dialogue of thanks. If there is a dispute, then it is either of the type of praise and blame, or of the type of affirmation and negation; and this is a general dispute. If instead of good one puts evil, then the dialogue proceeds in the way of complaint and apology; and that is about a past matter necessarily. So rhetorical dialogues are three: [1] deliberative, [2] epideictic, and [3] forensic; likewise the listeners are three: [1] opponent, [2] judge by persuasion of one of them, and [3] spectators. As for the judge in future matters, he is the leader who manages the affair of the community, and in occurring matters he is like a trusted mediator/investigator. As for the spectators, they look at the strength of one and the weakness of the other; nothing else is up to them. Then each of these discourses has a specific purpose: [1] Deliberation is a discourse intended to persuade that such and such should be done for its benefit or not done for its harm. [2] Epideictic is a discourse intended to persuade in praising something for its virtue or blaming it for its opposite. [3] Forensic is a discourse intended to persuade in a complaint of injustice or an apology that there is no injustice; and perhaps the dispute is not about the matter itself but about whether it is beneficial or not beneficial, or an injustice or not an injustice, for example. Deliberation is not deliberation because it persuades about something that is beneficial in reality, for perhaps it is not beneficial in reality nor with the advisor, but if it appears beneficial he tries to persuade about it; the discourse is nevertheless deliberation. And perhaps deliberation is about the beautiful whose benefit comes from another aspect, not the beneficial. Likewise praise and blame, rather perhaps praise is praise of the harmful, as occurs in encountering harm and injury for the sake of good reputation. Since rhetorical syllogism in all these respects requires a prior proposition taken from praiseworthy things or an indicator or sign, each of these is prior and is itself in place of the syllogism. The absolute syllogism sometimes is absolute and sometimes restricted towards thinking with a specific restriction. Since the necessary is among what is not sought nor fled from, there is no deliberation about it; rather deliberation is directed towards possibilities. So the advising orator should have premises proving that the matter is possible or impossible, and whether it will happen or not; for deliberation is a speech intended to move the will towards what is acquired by will of good and evil, and it is a prelude to voluntary action because it is specific to possibilities. This is the general principle regarding deliberation. Then there are special types that must be mentioned: we say that the matters that are the divisions of very serious deliberation (not particulars) are five: [1] armament, war, and peace,
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is a classification of rhetorical discourses into three types based on their temporal focus and purpose. The author begins by noting that all rhetorical types involve a general dispute about whether something is or is not. When specifying, common dialogues in particular matters concern good or evil. Particulars are either future or occurring. The author then explains that disputes about future particular goods/evils are rare among the public; they rather deal with present matters for praise/blame or past matters for complaint/apology. This leads to the central classification: rhetorical dialogues are three: deliberative (mashawiriyya) dealing with future benefit/harm, epideictic (munafariyya) dealing with present praise/blame, and forensic (mushajariyya) dealing with past accusation/defense. The listeners are also three: the opponent, the judge (who decides based on persuasion), and the spectators (who merely observe the strength of arguments). The author then elaborates on each type: deliberation aims to persuade to do or avoid an action for its benefit/harm; epideictic aims to praise or blame a thing for its virtue or vice; forensic aims to persuade in a complaint of injustice or an apology denying injustice. He clarifies that deliberation does not require actual benefit—only apparent benefit is enough for persuasion. Similarly, praise can be for something harmful if it brings a good reputation. The author then ties this to rhetorical syllogism, which requires a prior proposition from praiseworthy things or an indicator. He emphasizes that deliberation only concerns possibilities, not necessities. Finally, he outlines five important topics for serious deliberation: armament, war, peace, etc. (the list is cut off in the text).

Deliberative rhetoric focuses on future possibilities, aiming to persuade about benefit or harm, and does not require actual benefit, only apparent benefit.

Rhetoric -- Khitaba

Epideictic Rhetoric (Munafara)
Defines epideictic rhetoric and explains its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define epideictic rhetoric (munafara) and identify its focus on present praise or blame based on virtue or vice.

Core Idea
Epideictic rhetoric praises the virtuous and blames the vicious, even if the praise is for something harmful.

Epideictic rhetoric (munafara) is one of the three types of rhetorical discourse. It is concerned with present or timeless matters, aiming to persuade an audience to praise something for its virtue or blame it for its vice. The passage notes that even harmful actions can be praised if they bring a good reputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term for epideictic rhetoric in Arabic
Find the word 'منافرة' (munafara) in the passage and note its root meaning of contest or rivalry in praise/blame.
2
Extract the purpose
Read the definition: 'مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في مدح شيء بفضله أو ذمه بنقيضه' – a discourse intended to persuade in praising something for its virtue or blaming it for its vice.
The focus is on present or inherent qualities (virtue/vice).
3
Note the qualification about harmful praise
Observe that the passage says praise can be for harmful actions if they bring a good reputation: 'ربما كان المدح مدحا بالضار كما يكون في اقتحام الضرر والأذى للذكر الجميل'.
Epideictic rhetoric is not limited to objective virtue.
4
Distinguish from deliberative rhetoric
Contrast: epideictic is about present qualities, while deliberative is about future actions. Epideictic does not argue about future benefit/harm but about inherent praiseworthiness or blameworthiness.
This distinction is crucial for classification.
5
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Consider a mawlid poem praising the Prophet's character; classify it as epideictic rhetoric because it praises virtues.
Use the tool to analyze a real Islamic praise text.

The author then elaborates on each type: deliberation aims to persuade to do or avoid an action for its benefit/harm; epideictic aims to praise or blame a thing for its virtue or vice; forensic aims to persuade in a complaint of injustice or an apology denying injustice. He clarifies that deliberation does not require actual benefit—only apparent benefit is enough for persuasion. Similarly, praise can be for something harmful if it brings a good reputation. The author then ties this to rhetorical syllogism, which requires a prior proposition from praiseworthy things or an indicator. He emphasizes that deliberation only concerns possibilities, not necessities. Finally, he outlines five important topics for serious deliberation: armament, war, peace, etc. (the list is cut off in the text).

Forensic Rhetoric (Mushajara)
Defines forensic rhetoric and explains its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define forensic rhetoric (mushajara) and identify its focus on past actions, involving complaint of injustice or defense against accusation.

Core Idea
Forensic rhetoric settles disputes about past injustices through accusation and apology.

Forensic rhetoric (mushajara) is the third type of rhetorical discourse. It deals with past events, aiming to persuade about whether an injustice occurred, either through a complaint (shikaya) of injustice or an apology (i'tidhar) denying injustice. The passage also notes that the dispute may be about the classification of the act rather than the act itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term for forensic rhetoric in Arabic
Find the word 'مشاجرة' (mushajara) in the passage and note its root meaning of quarrel or dispute.
2
Extract the purpose
Read the definition: 'مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في شكاية ظلم أو اعتذار بأنه لا ظلم' – a discourse intended to persuade in a complaint of injustice or an apology that there is no injustice.
The focus is on past events, either accusing or defending.
3
Note the possible dispute about classification
The passage adds that the dispute may not be about the act itself but about whether it is beneficial/harmful or just/unjust: 'ربما لم تقع منازعته كون الأمر نفسه ولكن في كونه نافعا أو غير نافع أو ظلما أو غير ظلم مثلا'.
This shows the rhetorical nature of forensic discourse.
4
Distinguish from other types
Contrast: forensic is about past events (injustice), deliberative about future actions (benefit/harm), epideictic about present qualities (virtue/vice).
Temporal focus is key.
5
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
In a hadd punishment case, the prosecutor's argument is forensic, accusing the defendant of theft; the defense apologizes. Classify this exchange as forensic rhetoric.
Use the tool to analyze real Islamic legal proceedings.

فصل: فيما يتعلّق به الخطابة من الأعراض الأولية وغير ذلك. فنقول: إن المنازعة في كون الشيء ولا لكونه منازعة عامة لجميع الأنواع الخطابية؛ فإذا رجع إلى التفصيل والتخصيص فأكثر أصناف المحاورات العامية في الأمور الجزئية يرجع إلى ما فيه خير أو شر. والجزئيات إما مستقبلة وإما واقعة؛ ويبعد أن يقع للجمهور منازعة في جزئي مستقبل واقع بالطبع والاتفاق هل هو خير أو شر؛ فإن هذا النحو من النظر بأهل العلوم أولى بل إن تنازعوا في هذا تنازعوا. وهم يشيرون بالتحرز عنه إن كان شرا، أو التوقع إن كان خيرا. وأما الأمور الواقعة من الخير والشر فإما أن يراد إثبات وجود هذا الخير للإنسان، أو هذا الشر له فقط؛ وهذه هي المفاوضة التي يمدح فيها أو يدم. ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام في المعدوم من الخير والشر مما نقل؛ وإنما يمدح ويذم في أكثر الأمر ما هو موجود خاص فيكون أولى لأزمنة لموضوعات هذه المفاوضة هو الزمان الحاضر. وأما أن وجود هذا أو ذلك من إنسان آخر بإرادته؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون للخطيب تفاوض إنسانًا في أن خيرًا وصل إليه منه، أو من إنسان آخر. وليس مع الاعتراف تشاجر وتنازع البتة؛ فقصارى ذلك محاورة في الشكر. وإن كان هناك تنازع فذلك إما من باب المدح والذم، وإما من باب الإثبات والإبطال؛ وهذه منازعة عامة. فإذا جعل بدل الخير شر كانت للمفاوضة جارية على سبيل شكاية واعتذار ؛ وذلك في أمر ماض لا محالة. فإذن المفاوضات الخطابية ثلاثة: [أ] مشاورية، [ب] ومنافرية، جميع [ت] ومشاجرية؛ وكذلك السامعون ثلاثة : [1] خصم [ب] وحاكم بإقناع أحدهما، [ت] ونظار . أما الحاكم في الأمور المستقبلة فهو الرئيس المدبر لأمر الجماعة، وفي الواقعة يكون كالمتوسط الموثوق / تفحصه. وأما النظار فينظرون في قوة أحدهما وضعف الآخر ليس إليهم غير ذلك. ثم لكل واحد من هذه المخاطبات غرض خاص؛ [۱] أما المشورة فهي مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في أن كذا ينبغي أن يفعل لنفعه أو أن لا يفعل لضره. [٢] وأما المنافرة فهي مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في مدح شيء بفضله أو ذمه بنقيضه. [٣] وأما المشاجرة فهي مخاطبة يراد بها الإقناع في شكاية ظلم أو اعتذار بأنه لا ظلم وربَّما لم تقع منازعته كون الأمر نفسه ولكن في كونه نافعا أو غير نافع أو ظلمًا أو غير ظلم مثلا . والمشورة ليست مشورة بسبب إقناعها في أمر هو نافع بالحقيقة فإنه ربَّما لم يكن نافعا بالحقيقة ولا عند المشير لكنه إن ظهر أنه نافع حاول الإقناع فيه؛ فيكون المخاطبة مع ذلك مشورة. وربَّما كانت المشورة بالجميل الذي نفعه من جهة أخرى لا بالنافع. وكذلك المدح والذم بل ربَّما كان المدح مدحا بالضار كما يكون في اقتحام الضرر والأذى للذكر الجميل. ولما كان القياس الخطابي في جميع هذه الوجوه يقتضي تقدم قضية وهي مأخوذة المحمودات أو دليل أو علامة فكل واحد من هذه متقدمة وهي بعينها مكان القياس؛ فالقياس المطلق مما يكون بالإطلاق والمقيد نحو التفكير مما له تقييد بوجه خاص ولما كان الضروري من جملة ما لا يطلب ولا يهرب فلا مشورة فيه بل المشورة يتوجه نحو الممكنات. فالخطيب المشير ينبغي أن يكون عنده مقدمات في إثبات أن الأمر ممكن أو غير ممكن، وفي أنه هل يكون أو لا يكون؛ إذ المشورة قول يراد به التحريك الإرادي نحو ما يكتسب بالإرادة من الخير والشر وهي مقدمة للعمل الإرادي لما أنها مخصوصة بالممكنات؛ فهذا هو الأمر العام لما ينحو المشورة. ثم هنا أنواع خاصة لا بد من الإشارة إليها فنقول: إن الأمور التي هي أقسام المشورة الخطيرة جدا دون الجزئيات خمسة؛ [أ] العدة والحرب والسلم، [ب]
Chapter: On what pertains to rhetoric from primary accidents and other things. We say: The dispute about whether something is or is not a thing is a general dispute for all rhetorical types. When returning to detail and specification, most types of common dialogues in particular matters revert to what contains good or evil. Particulars are either future or occurring. It is unlikely that the public would dispute about a particular future thing that occurs naturally and by chance whether it is good or evil, for this kind of investigation is more appropriate for the people of the sciences. Rather, if they dispute about this, they dispute. They indicate caution if it is evil, or expectation if it is good. As for occurring matters of good and evil, either it is intended to prove the existence of this good for a person, or this evil for him alone; and this is the dialogue in which one praises or blames. There is no speech about it, rather speech is about non-existent good and evil that is reported. One praises and blames in most cases what is specific and existent, so the time most appropriate for the subjects of this dialogue is the present time. As for the existence of this or that from another person by his will, then either the orator dialogues with a person about a good that reached him from him, or from another person. With acknowledgment, there is no quarrel or dispute at all; the most that is is a dialogue of thanks. If there is a dispute, then it is either of the type of praise and blame, or of the type of affirmation and negation; and this is a general dispute. If instead of good one puts evil, then the dialogue proceeds in the way of complaint and apology; and that is about a past matter necessarily. So rhetorical dialogues are three: [1] deliberative, [2] epideictic, and [3] forensic; likewise the listeners are three: [1] opponent, [2] judge by persuasion of one of them, and [3] spectators. As for the judge in future matters, he is the leader who manages the affair of the community, and in occurring matters he is like a trusted mediator/investigator. As for the spectators, they look at the strength of one and the weakness of the other; nothing else is up to them. Then each of these discourses has a specific purpose: [1] Deliberation is a discourse intended to persuade that such and such should be done for its benefit or not done for its harm. [2] Epideictic is a discourse intended to persuade in praising something for its virtue or blaming it for its opposite. [3] Forensic is a discourse intended to persuade in a complaint of injustice or an apology that there is no injustice; and perhaps the dispute is not about the matter itself but about whether it is beneficial or not beneficial, or an injustice or not an injustice, for example. Deliberation is not deliberation because it persuades about something that is beneficial in reality, for perhaps it is not beneficial in reality nor with the advisor, but if it appears beneficial he tries to persuade about it; the discourse is nevertheless deliberation. And perhaps deliberation is about the beautiful whose benefit comes from another aspect, not the beneficial. Likewise praise and blame, rather perhaps praise is praise of the harmful, as occurs in encountering harm and injury for the sake of good reputation. Since rhetorical syllogism in all these respects requires a prior proposition taken from praiseworthy things or an indicator or sign, each of these is prior and is itself in place of the syllogism. The absolute syllogism sometimes is absolute and sometimes restricted towards thinking with a specific restriction. Since the necessary is among what is not sought nor fled from, there is no deliberation about it; rather deliberation is directed towards possibilities. So the advising orator should have premises proving that the matter is possible or impossible, and whether it will happen or not; for deliberation is a speech intended to move the will towards what is acquired by will of good and evil, and it is a prelude to voluntary action because it is specific to possibilities. This is the general principle regarding deliberation. Then there are special types that must be mentioned: we say that the matters that are the divisions of very serious deliberation (not particulars) are five: [1] armament, war, and peace,
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is a classification of rhetorical discourses into three types based on their temporal focus and purpose. The author begins by noting that all rhetorical types involve a general dispute about whether something is or is not. When specifying, common dialogues in particular matters concern good or evil. Particulars are either future or occurring. The author then explains that disputes about future particular goods/evils are rare among the public; they rather deal with present matters for praise/blame or past matters for complaint/apology. This leads to the central classification: rhetorical dialogues are three: deliberative (mashawiriyya) dealing with future benefit/harm, epideictic (munafariyya) dealing with present praise/blame, and forensic (mushajariyya) dealing with past accusation/defense. The listeners are also three: the opponent, the judge (who decides based on persuasion), and the spectators (who merely observe the strength of arguments). The author then elaborates on each type: deliberation aims to persuade to do or avoid an action for its benefit/harm; epideictic aims to praise or blame a thing for its virtue or vice; forensic aims to persuade in a complaint of injustice or an apology denying injustice. He clarifies that deliberation does not require actual benefit—only apparent benefit is enough for persuasion. Similarly, praise can be for something harmful if it brings a good reputation. The author then ties this to rhetorical syllogism, which requires a prior proposition from praiseworthy things or an indicator. He emphasizes that deliberation only concerns possibilities, not necessities. Finally, he outlines five important topics for serious deliberation: armament, war, peace, etc. (the list is cut off in the text).

Types of Advisors in Islamic Rhetoric: Defense, War, Protection
Describes required knowledge for advisors in city defense, warfare, and protection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three types of advisors in Islamic rhetoric and list the knowledge each must possess.

Core Idea
The advisor must master strategy, terrain, and human needs beyond mere speech.

This passage from the rhetorical tradition defines the qualities required for three distinct types of counselors. Understanding these roles helps us see how rhetoric serves practical governance and defense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of advisors
Read the source Arabic and find where the text explicitly names the three counselors: advisor in matters of equipment (al-khatib al-mushīr fī amr al-ʿidda), advisor in war and peace (al-mushīr fī amr al-ḥarb wa-l-ṣulḥ), and advisor in city protection (al-mushīr fī ḥimāyat al-madīna).
The transition phrases 'وأما المشير في أمر الحرب والصلح' and 'وأما المشير في حماية المدينة' signal the shifts.
2
Extract required knowledge for each advisor
For each advisor, list the specific areas of expertise mentioned. For the equipment advisor, note: expertise in types of 'elevations' (genera and species), expenditures, reports, and experiences. For the war/peace advisor: first, the power of the orator, then enemy numbers, strategy, and consequences. For the protection advisor: types of defenses for different terrains, positions of water sources, and knowledge of people's needs.
Focus on the verbs 'ينبغي أن يكون خبيرًا' and 'ينبغي أن يحيط علمًا' to locate requirements.
3
Understand the common thread in rhetoric
Notice that all three advisors rely on rhetorical skill (the orator's power) and practical knowledge. The passage ties these roles together under the broader science of rhetoric. Reflect on how each advisor's knowledge complements the others.
The passage begins with general duties (protection, finance, laws) and then specifies each advisor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحماية المدينة [ت] ومراعاة أمر الدخل والخارج، [ث] وتفريع الشرائع [ج] ووضع المصالح والخطيب المشير في أمر العدة ينبغي أن يكون خبيرًا بارتفاعات الناجية من أي الأجناس هي وكم هي، وأصناف المصارف وكيفية أحوالهم. وينبغي أيضًا أن يحيط علمًا بجزئيات الأخبار وبفوائد التجارب؛ فإنها تذاكير وأمثال . وأما المشير في أمر الحرب والصلح فأول ما ينبغي أن يلحظه قوة الخطيب الباعث على القتال. وبعد ذلك ينبغي أن يحيط بمقابلة مدينة والمقابلة المحاصرين عدهم وعددهم و درتهم بالحرب وبالهم علمًا، ويحيط بحال العسكر كما ينبغي من طريق الحيلة والاستمداد. وبالجملة يجب أن يكون له نظر إلى بعض أنواع الحروب والتعالي وكيفية المقابلة السالفة في مدينة وما يليها ورسومهم ومذاهبهم، وأن يكون خبيرًا بعواقبها المحمودة والمذمومة. وأما المشير في حماية المدينة فينبغي أن يعرف أنواع الحفظ لأنواع البلاد المختلفة سهلًا وحبلًا برًا وبحرًا، وأن يعرف مواضع المسائح وأنها كيف ينبغي أن يكون في قربها وبعدها، وكيف وجه الاستظهار في ترتيبها. والناس يختلفون في الحاجة إلى ما لا بد لكل واحد؛ فينبغي أن يكون المشير بصيرًا بمقدار حاجة كل إلى كل، وبأحوال أهل القضاء وأهل الثروة منهم. فيشير بما ينبغي أن يستعان به فيه لأهل الفضل وغيرهم.
And the protection of the city and the observation of matters of income and expenses, and the branching of laws and the establishment of interests, and the orator-counselor regarding the matter of equipment should be expert in the elevations of the salvations of which genera they are and how many they are, and the types of expenditures and how their conditions are. And he should also encompass knowledge of particulars of reports and benefits of experiences; for they are reminders and examples. And as for the counselor concerning war and peace, the first thing he should observe is the power of the orator that incites fighting. And after that, he should encompass knowledge of the confrontation of the city and the confrontation of the besieged: their number, their count, and their expertise in war and their condition. And he should encompass the condition of the army as necessary through the way of strategy and reinforcement. And in summary, he must have a view to some types of wars and heights and how the past confrontation in the city and its surroundings and their customs and doctrines, and he should be expert in their praiseworthy and blameworthy consequences. And as for the counselor in the protection of the city, he must know the types of protection for the different types of lands, plain and mountainous, land and sea, and he must know the positions of the water sources and how they should be in their proximity and distance, and how the aspect of precaution is in their arrangement. And the people differ in the need for what is necessary for each one; so the counselor should be insightful about the extent of each one's need for everything, and about the conditions of the people of judgment and the people of wealth among them. So he advises what should be sought help for the people of virtue and others.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage defines the qualities required for a counselor in various domains, within the context of rhetoric (al-khataba). The author first mentions general aspects: protection of the city, management of revenue and expenditure, and legislation. Then he specifies three types of counselors. First, the counselor in matters of equipment (al-khatib al-mushīr fī amr al-ʿidda) must be expert in the types of 'elevations' (likely weaponry or resources), their genera and species, and the types of expenditures and conditions of people. He must also know particular reports and experiences. Second, the counselor in war and peace (al-mushīr fī amr al-ḥarb wa-l-ṣulḥ) must first consider the power of the orator inciting combat, then know the enemy's numbers and condition, and strategies for reinforcement. Third, the counselor in protecting the city (al-mushīr fī ḥimāyat al-madīna) must know types of defenses for different terrains, both land and sea, and the positioning of water sources (al-masāʾiḥ). Finally, a general point: since people differ in needs, the counselor must understand each person's requirements and the conditions of the judiciary and wealthy, so he can advise accordingly. This passage is definitional, outlining the knowledge base for each type of advisor.

Key Terms
الخطيب المشير (al-khaṭīb al-mushīr): The orator-counselor who advises on matters of equipment or war
المشير في أمر الحرب والصلح (al-mushīr fī amr al-ḥarb wa-l-ṣulḥ): The counselor in matters of war and peace, who must gauge rhetorical power and strategies
المشير في حماية المدينة (al-mushīr fī ḥimāyat al-madīna): The counselor in city protection, who knows types of defenses and terrain

Key Takeaway
The rhetorical tradition classifies advisors by domain, emphasizing specialized knowledge in equipment, warfare, or protection.

The Six Political Systems for the Rhetorical Counselor
Enumerates six types of political systems and their combinations as required knowledge for the counselor on laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the six political systems described for advising on laws in Islamic rhetorical theory.

Core Idea
The counselor on laws must master six political systems to give sound advice.

This passage from a logic-rhetoric text outlines the classifications of political systems that a counselor must know when advising on laws. The author emphasizes that this knowledge is crucial for the most dangerous and rhetoric-intensive aspect of counsel. We will walk through each system and its combinations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of this classification
The passage states that consultation on laws requires knowledge of the types of 'civil associations' (ishtirākāt madaniyya) and the policies that preserve them. Recognize that this is a tool for the orator-counselor to tailor advice.
2
List the six base policies
The passage enumerates six: Unity (al-waḥdāniyya), Domination (al-taghlubiyya), Honor (al-karāma), Poverty (al-fuqriyya), Communal cooperation (al-jamāʿiyya), and later mentions subdivisions. Extract the Arabic terms and their brief descriptions.
The text numbers them [1] to [6].
3
Understand the combinations
The passage shows how certain policies combine to form new ones: e.g., Domination + Honor = Unity; Domination + something = Baseness; etc. Note the hierarchical structure.
The combinations are named separately.
4
Identify the final classification
The passage concludes with six named policies: Domination, Honor, Policy of the few (oligarchy), Democracy, Policy of good, and Kingship, with 'Socratic policy' encompassing the last two. Understand that these are the types the counselor must be insightful about.
The text says 'فأول هذه السياسات تسمى التغلبية...'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشورة في أمر السنن فإنها من أعظم الأبواب خطرًا وأمسها إلى أفضل قوة في الخطابة حاجة؛ فأول ما ينبغي أن يتحققه حال عدد الأنواع والاشتراكات المدنية، والمناسبة بينها، والأسباب الحافظة لكل واحد من الأمم، وأضدادها، وأصناف السياسات التي تحفظ هذه الاشتراكات؛ أربعة يشعب إلى ستة منها البتة : [١] الوحدانية؛ إذ لا رضى السائس فيها بالشريك. [٢] والسياسة التغلبية وهي أن يكون المطاع المنتهى إلى رسمه المتدبر بتدبيره. [٣] والغلبة إما بفضل ذات اليد، وإما بفضل قوة أخرى، [٤] وسياسة الكرامة وهي أن يكون الرئيس يراعي مصالح الرعية لا لشيء يستقبضه إلا الكرامة والتعظيم. [٥] والسياسة الفقرية وهي أن يكون المطاع هو المؤثر يرأس ويقدم لثروته من غير مغالبة لولاه قبل. [٦] والسياسة الجماعية وهي أن يكون أهل المدينة متشاركين على طلب السعادة العاجلية والآجلية كل له مقام محمود بحسب فضيلة في نوع صناعته وجنسها؛ فهو دون من فوقه وفوق من دونه إن كان وكل له عمل يعود بصلاح المشاركة وفيهم رئيس واحد أو رؤساء لنفس واحدة يذعن له، أو لهم الآخرُ وتطوعًا لا عن اختيار . ثم يشعب تحته رئاسات بحسب الصناعات إلى آخر الناس لا نزاع بينهم ولا اختلاف؛ فإن كان الرئيس فيها حكيما، وكان له مع الفضيلة المدنية فضيلة نظرية كان بالحري أن يكمل هذه السياسة: فأول هذه السياسات تسمى التغلبية. والثانية سياسة الكرامة؛ وإذا أخذت مع التغلبية سُمي الأمر الذي يعمهما الوحدانية. والثالثة تسمى سياسة القلة. وإذا أخذت مع التغلبية سمي الأمر الذي يعمهما سياسة الخسة. والرابعة تسمى سياسة الحرية والديمقراطية. والخامسة سياسة الخير والسادسة سياسة الملك؛ ويعمهما اسم سياسة السقراطية؛ فينبغي أن يكون المشير بصيرا بهذه السياسات، وما يعرض لكل واحد منها وما يؤول إليه حال كل واحد منها. وأما فساد السنن من جهة الأضداد فليس يحتاج إليه الخطيب من جهة ما يشير إليه في وضع السنن بل من جهة حفظ البلاد. وقد قيل في ذلك وقوع عنه على أن استقصاء الأمر في هذا العلم للسياسة لا لصناعة الخطابة.
And as for consultation regarding the matters of laws, it is among the greatest of chapters in danger and the most in need of the best power in rhetoric. So the first thing he should ascertain is the condition of the number of types and civil associations, and the suitability between them, and the preserving causes for each one of the nations, and their opposites, and the types of policies that preserve these associations; four that branch into six absolutely: [1] Unity; for the ruler does not accept a partner in it. [2] The policy of domination, which is that the obeyed one whose final authority is his own command, the one who is managed by his management. [3] And domination is either by excellence of wealth, or by excellence of another strength. [4] And the policy of honor, which is that the ruler takes care of the interests of the subjects not for something reciprocated except honor and veneration. [5] And the policy of poverty, which is that the obeyed one is the influential one, he becomes chief and is advanced for his wealth without domineering, had he not been so before. [6] And the policy of communal cooperation, which is that the people of the city are co-participants in seeking immediate and future happiness, each has a praised station according to a virtue in the type of his craft and its genus; so he is below him above him and above him below him if there is, and each has a work that returns the welfare of the partnership, and among them is one leader or leaders for one self to whom the other yields willingly and voluntarily, not by choice. Then under it branch leaderships according to the crafts to the end of the people, with no dispute nor disagreement among them. If the leader among them is wise, and with civil virtue he possesses theoretical virtue, then he is more worthy to complete this policy. So the first of these policies is called domination. The second: policy of honor. And if taken with domination, the matter that encompasses both is called unity. The third is called policy of the few. And if taken with domination, the matter that encompasses both is called policy of baseness. The fourth is called policy of freedom and democracy. The fifth: policy of good. The sixth: policy of kingship. And encompassing both is the name Socratic policy. So the counselor should be insightful about these policies, and what occurs for each one of them and what each one of them culminates in. And as for the corruption of laws from the aspect of opposites, the orator does not need it from the aspect of what he advises in establishing laws, but from the aspect of preserving countries. And it has been said in that, an occurrence about it, that the thorough investigation of this science belongs to politics, not to the craft of rhetoric.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage discusses the most important domain for the counselor: laws (al-sunan). It says that consultation on laws is the most dangerous and in greatest need of rhetorical power. The counselor must ascertain the number of 'civil associations' (ishtirakāt madaniyya) and the political systems that preserve them. There are four that branch into six. The author then enumerates six policies: 1) al-waḥdāniyya (Unity): the ruler accepts no partner. 2) al-siyāsa al-taghlubiyya (Domination): the ruler is obeyed absolutely, and his rule is by management. 3) A subtype of domination: either by wealth or other strength. 4) siyāsat al-karāma (Honor): the ruler cares for subjects only for honor. 5) al-siyāsa al-fuqriyya (Poverty): the ruler is wealthy and leads without force. 6) al-siyāsa al-jamāʿiyya (Communal): people cooperate for happiness, with a single leader or multiple leaders. Then the author branches further: under these, there are other policies named based on combinations. He lists: domination; honor; combination called unity; then policy of the few (oligarchy); combination with domination called policy of baseness; then policy of freedom and democracy; policy of good; policy of kingship; and the combination called Socratic policy. The counselor must be expert in these and understand their outcomes. Finally, the author notes that the orator does not need to study corruption of laws from opposites for legislation but for preserving countries, and that this science belongs to politics, not rhetoric. This passage defines political classifications within the rhetorical art.

Key Terms
اشتراكات مدنية
السياسة التغلبية
ديمقراطية
سقراطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Six Political Regimes for the Rhetorician
Classification of six political regimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six main political regimes described in this passage and name their combinations.

Core Idea
The rhetorician must understand regimes to advise rulers effectively.

In this passage from a logic-rhetoric textbook, the author classifies six types of political regimes, each based on a different principle: force, honor, wealth, cooperation, goodness, or kingship. These regimes can also combine to form hybrid categories. As an advisor (الخطيب), knowing these regimes and their outcomes is essential for crafting persuasive counsel.

Guided Path
1
List the six regimes
Read the Arabic carefully and extract the names of all six types. Note the basis of each: force (تغلب), honor (كرامة), wealth (فقر), partnership (جماعة), good (خير), and kingship (ملك).
The policy of the few (سياسة القلة) is listed as the third, though it is not separately named in the earlier enumeration? Actually, the third is called 'سياسة القلة' in the later summary.
2
Distinguish the regimes
Compare the domineering policy (التغلبية) with the policy of dignity (سياسة الكرامة). The first is based on force or superiority, the second on a leader's desire for honor, not material gain. Identify what drives each ruler.
3
Identify the combinations
The author gives three composite terms: الوحدانية (unity), الخسة (base policy), and السقراطية (Socratic policy). For each, determine which two basic regimes are combined. For example, unity = domineering + dignity.
The text also mentions 'سياسة الحرية والديمقراطية' as the fourth, but does not combine it.
4
Understand the audience
The author concludes that the rhetorician only needs the political aspect for preserving the country, not the details of corruption of norms. Why is this distinction made?
This limits the scope of rhetoric to practical advice, not theoretical political science.

[٣] والغلبة إما بفضل ذات اليد، وإما بفضل قوة أخرى، [٤] وسياسة الكرامة وهي أن يكون الرئيس يراعي مصالح الرعية لا لشيء يستقبضه إلا الكرامة والتعظيم. [٥] والسياسة الفقرية وهي أن يكون المطاع هو المؤثر يرأس ويقدم لثروته من غير مغالبة لولاه قبل. [٦] والسياسة الجماعية وهي أن يكون أهل المدينة متشاركين على طلب السعادة العاجلية والآجلية كل له مقام محمود بحسب فضيلة في نوع صناعته وجنسها ؛ فهو دون من فوقه وفوق من دونه إن كان وكل له عمل يعود بصلاح المشاركة وفيهم رئيس واحد أو رؤساء لنفس واحدة يذعن له، أو لهم الآخرُ وتطوعًا لا عن اختيار . ثم يشعب تحته رئاسات بحسب الصناعات إلى آخر الناس لا نزاع بينهم ولا اختلاف؛ فإن كان الرئيس فيها حكيما، وكان له مع الفضيلة المدنية فضيلة نظرية كان بالحري أن يكمل هذه السياسة: فأول هذه السياسات تسمى التغلبية. والثانية سياسة الكرامة؛ وإذا أخذت مع التغلبية سُمي الأمر الذي يعمهما الوحدانية. والثالثة تسمى سياسة القلة. وإذا أخذت مع التغلبية سمي الأمر الذي يعمهما سياسة الخسة. والرابعة تسمى سياسة الحرية والديمقراطية. والخامسة سياسة الخير والسادسة سياسة الملك؛ ويعمهما اسم سياسة السقراطية؛ فينبغي أن يكون المشير بصيرا بهذه السياسات، وما يعرض لكل واحد منها وما يؤول إليه حال كل واحد منها. وأما فساد السنن من جهة الأضداد فليس يحتاج إليه الخطيب من جهة ما يشير إليه في وضع السنن بل من جهة حفظ البلاد. وقد قيل في ذلك وقوع عنه على أن استقصاء الأمر في هذا العلم للسياسة لا لصناعة الخطابة.
[2] The domineering policy: it is that the obeyed one ends at his own plan, organized by his management. [3] Domination is either by superiority in wealth or by superiority in another power. [4] The policy of dignity: it is that the leader looks after the interests of the subjects not for something he takes except dignity and honor. [5] The poor policy: it is that the obeyed one is the influential one who leads and advances due to his wealth without domination, had it not been before. [6] The collective policy: it is that the people of the city are partners in seeking immediate and delayed happiness, each having a praised station according to virtue in the type and genus of his craft; so he is below those above him and above those below him, if he is entrusted with work that returns to the good of partnership, and among them is one leader or leaders for one soul to whom others submit voluntarily, not by choice. Then beneath it branches leaderships according to the crafts down to the rest of people, with no dispute or disagreement among them. If the leader is wise and has theoretical virtue along with civic virtue, it is fitting that he perfect this policy. So the first of these policies is called domineering; the second is policy of dignity; if taken with domineering, the concept encompassing them is called unity. The third is called policy of the few; if taken with domineering, the concept encompassing them is called base policy. The fourth is called policy of freedom and democracy. The fifth is policy of good and the sixth is policy of kingship; they are encompassed by the name Socratic policy. So the advisor should be insightful about these policies, what occurs in each, and what each leads to. As for corruption of norms from the side of opposites, the rhetorician does not need it insofar as he alludes to establishing norms, but rather from the side of preserving the country. It has been said about that, that investigating the matter in this science is for politics, not for the craft of rhetoric.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage classifies six types of political regimes, primarily for the benefit of the rhetorician (الخطيب) in advisory contexts. The author defines each type: (1) Domineering policy (السياسة التغلبية) based on force, (2) Policy of dignity (سياسة الكرامة) where the leader seeks honor, (3) Poor policy (السياسة الفقرية) based on wealth, (4) Collective policy (السياسة الجماعية) where citizens cooperate for happiness, (5) Policy of good (سياسة الخير), and (6) Policy of kingship (سياسة الملك). Combinations are given names: domineering + dignity = unity (الوحدانية); domineering + policy of the few = base policy (سياسة الخسة); good + kingship = Socratic policy (السياسة السقراطية). The author stresses that the advisor must know these regimes and their outcomes. The passage concludes that rhetoric needs only the political aspect for preserving the country, not the details of norm corruption. Throughout, the examples are from political science, not standard logic, reflecting this work's rhetorical focus.

Defining Welfare and Its Parts for Rhetorical Advice
Author defines welfare and enumerates its parts for rhetorical advice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize the components of ṣalāḥ al-ḥāl as presented in this passage and apply them to construct advisory premises.

Core Idea
Welfare in rhetoric is beautiful action with love, dignity, and virtue—known by general opinion.

This passage shifts from great advisory matters to particular ones, focusing on the concept of ṣalāḥ al-ḥāl (welfare). The author synthesizes a definition and enumerates its parts, providing a toolkit for the advisor. We will dissect the definition, list its components, and understand how they serve as premises in rhetorical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: ṣalāḥ al-ḥāl (صلاح الحال). Notice the author states 'فنقول: إن صلاح الحال هو...' (So we say: welfare is...).
2
Extract the definition's core
The definition: 'beautiful action (الفعال الجميل) due to its virtue, fuller and more constructive of life, accompanied by love of hearts, and provides dignity from people, defense, good life, protection, and abundance of wealth and contracts.' Note that it combines moral, social, and material aspects.
The author emphasizes 'according to general opinion' (بحسب الظن العام) — it's probabilistic, not certain.
3
Enumerate the parts (ajzāʾ)
List the components: sharpness of love, abundance of brothers/children/wealth, beneficence, good reputation, health, beauty, glory, good fortune, virtues like strength, sound intellect, lineage, chastity, righteousness.
The list ends with 'والبطش' (strength) but text may be incomplete.
4
Categorize the parts
The author classifies: 'some are physical (بدنية), some are psychological (نفسانية).' Identify which items fit each category (e.g., health is physical; love is psychological).
5
Connect to rhetorical use
Understand that these parts serve as 'advisory topics' (مواضع مشورية) for the advisor to construct persuasive premises. For instance, to persuade someone to act, you can appeal to the 'love of hearts' or 'dignity' components.
The author quotes Aristotle criticizing earlier rhetoricians for not understanding the causes; this definition provides those causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأمور المشورية الجزئية الغير العظام
قد مرَّ من قبل ذكر المواضع التي ينتزع منها المقدمات المشورية في الأمور العظام فيلزمنا التعرُّض بالغير وهو المواضع المشورية النافعة في الأمور التي بحسب الأشخاص وهي في أنفسها غير معدودة إلا أن جميعها تشترك في حكم [٣٥٦] المشورة ينحو نحو صلاح الحال بالحقيقة؛ إذ كان بالظن؛ فيجب أن يعرف صلاح الحال. وإن تعددت (۱) الأمور التي هي أنواع لصلاح الحال أو أجزاء له باجتماعها تصلح الحال حتى تكون للمشير فيما يشيره مواضع تجعلها مقدمات مشورية. قال المعلم الأول : «ولا نقتصر على ما كان عند الخطباء في ذلك فيما سلف من ذكر وجوب التهويل والتكثير أو التهوين أو التحقير». والحث عليها واجتناب ما يفسد النظام ويزيل الإقناع من غير أن عرفوا بماذا يكون كذا، وفي ماذا يكون ، وما الذي يُفسد غرض الخطيب وينتقص إقناعه. فنقول: إن صلاح الحال هو الفعال الجميل عن فضله وأملأ وأنشأ العمر مشفوعا بمحبة القلوب ويوفر الكرامة من الناس، وفي دفاعه، وطيب عيش ووقائه، وسعة ذات اليد في المال، والعقد. ويمكن من استدامة هذه الأقوال والاستمداد إليها؛ فإن صلاح الحال بحسب الظن العام هو ما ذكرنا أو ما يجري مجراه.
وأما أجزاؤه فذكاء المحبة، ووفور الإخوان والأولاد واليسار والإنعام وبلوغ السنة الحسنة لوقارها وأحوالها، والصحة والجمال والخساسة ذلك فالمجد والجلالة وسعادة البخت وأنواع الفضائل مثل
والبطش. ومع أصالة العقل والنسالة والعفاف والبر؛ فبعض هذه بدنية، وبعضها نفسانية،
(۱) النسخة تعدد وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
A chapter on particular advisory matters that are not great. Previously we mentioned the topics from which advisory premises are taken in great matters, so we need to address the other, which are the advisory topics useful in matters concerning individuals. They are in themselves uncountable, but all share in the judgment [356] that advice tends toward actual welfare, since it is probable. So one must know welfare. Even if the things that are types of welfare or parts of it are multiple, by their coming together welfare is achieved, so that the advisor has topics that he makes as advisory premises. The First Teacher said: 'We are not limited to what the rhetoricians had in the past regarding the mention of the necessity of exaggeration and increase or of minimization or belittlement.' And urging them and avoiding what corrupts order and removes persuasion, without them knowing by what it is so, and in what it is, and what corrupts the rhetorician's aim and diminishes his persuasion. So we say: Welfare is beautiful action due to its virtue, and fuller and more constructive of life, accompanied by love of hearts, and provides dignity from people, and defense, good life and protection, and abundance of hands in wealth and contracts. And it is possible to sustain these statements and draw from them; for welfare according to general opinion is what we mentioned or what runs in its course. As for its parts: sharpness of love, abundance of brothers, children, wealth, beneficence, attainment of good reputation for its dignity and states, health, beauty, and baseness, then glory and majesty, good fortune, and types of virtues like strength. And with soundness of intellect, lineage, chastity, and righteousness; some of these are physical, some are psychological. (1) The copy has 'تعدد' and what we stated is correct, as appears from context.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the concept of صلاح الحال (welfare) for use in rhetorical advice. The author begins by situating this discussion: previously he covered advisory premises for great matters (umur 'izam); now he turns to particular matters (juz'iyya) concerning individuals. The key point is that all such matters aim at صلاح الحال (welfare), which is known by probability (zann). He then quotes Aristotle (المعلم الأول) criticizing earlier rhetoricians for only focusing on exaggeration or minimization without understanding the causes. The author defines صلاح الحال as beautiful action (فعال جميل) accompanied by love, dignity, good life, wealth, etc. He then enumerates its parts: sharpness of love, abundance of brothers, children, wealth, beneficence, good reputation, health, beauty, glory, good fortune, virtues like strength, sound intellect, lineage, chastity, and righteousness. These are categorized into physical and psychological. The footnote indicates a textual variant. Throughout, the author provides a taxonomy of welfare components that the rhetorician can use as premises for persuasive arguments, keeping all examples within the Islamic/ethical framework.

Key Terms
صلاح الحال
المقدمات المشورية
المعلم الأول

Key Takeaway
Welfare (ṣalāḥ al-ḥāl) is defined as beautiful action accompanied by love, dignity, and material well-being, with physical and psychological components that serve as advisory premises in rhetoric.

?

Defining Well-Being for Deliberative Rhetoric
Definition of well-being and enumeration of its components for deliberative rhetoric.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of well-being as defined for deliberative rhetoric and classify them as bodily, psychological, or external.

Core Idea
Well-being is a composite of commonly perceived goods, not just material wealth.

In deliberative rhetoric, a speaker must know what the audience considers good or desirable. This passage gives a comprehensive definition of well-being (صلاح الحال) as the ultimate goal of deliberation. By breaking down its components, you learn how to construct persuasive premises that appeal to shared values.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'صلاح الحال' in the Arabic. Note that the passage begins by stating the need to know well-being for deliberative matters.
2
Extract the core definition
Find the sentence starting with 'فنقول: إن صلاح الحال...'. List the elements mentioned: beautiful action, filling and growing life, love of hearts, honor, defense, pleasant living, wealth.
This is the author's explicit definition.
3
Enumerate the components (أجزاء)
After the definition, the passage lists components: love, brothers, children, wealth, favor, good reputation, health, beauty, glory, majesty, luck, virtues like strength, soundness of mind, lineage, chastity, piety.
Some terms like 'الخساسة' may be corrupt; focus on clear ones.
4
Classify according to the author's categories
Note the author's own classification: bodily, psychological, and external. Place each component into one of these three groups.
External includes lineage, brothers, wealth, honor.
5
Understand the rhetorical purpose
Realize that these components serve as 'deliberative premises' – they provide ready-made appeals to common opinion for persuasive discourse.
The advisor (مشير) uses these to construct arguments.

Source Text

فصل في الأمور المشورية الجزئية الغير العظام. قد مر من قبل ذكر المواضع التي ينتزع منها المقدمات المشورية في الأمور العظام فيلزمنا التعرض بالغير وهو المواضع المشورية النافعة في الأمور التي بحسب الأشخاص وهي في أنفسها غير معدودة إلا أن جميعها تشترك في حكم المشورة ينحو نحو صلاح الحال بالحقيقة؛ إذ كان بالظن؛ فيجب أن يعرف صلاح الحال. وإن تعددت الأمور التي هي أنواع لصلاح الحال أو أجزاء له باجتماعها تصلح الحال حتى تكون للمشير فيما يشيره مواضع تجعلها مقدمات مشورية. قال المعلم الأول : «ولا نقتصر على ما كان عند الخطباء في ذلك فيما سلف من ذكر وجوب التهويل والتكثير أو التهوين أو التحقير». والحث عليها واجتناب ما يفسد النظام ويزيل الإقناع من غير أن عرفوا بماذا يكون كذا، وفي ماذا يكون، وما الذي يفسد غرض الخطيب وينتقص إقناعه. فنقول: إن صلاح الحال هو الفعال الجميل عن فضله وأملأ وأنشأ العمر مشفوعا بمحبة القلوب ويوفر الكرامة من الناس، وفي دفاعه، وطيب عيش ووقائه، وسعة ذات اليد في المال، والعقد. ويمكن من استدامة هذه الأقوال والاستمداد إليها؛ فإن صلاح الحال بحسب الظن العام هو ما ذكرنا أو ما يجري مجراه. وأما أجزاؤه فذكاء المحبة، ووفور الإخوان والأولاد واليسار والإنعام وبلوغ السنة الحسنة لوقارها وأحوالها، والصحة والجمال والخساسة ذلك فالمجد والجلالة وسعادة البخت وأنواع الفضائل مثل البطش. ومع أصالة العقل والنسالة والعفاف والبر؛ فبعض هذه بدنية، وبعضها نفسانية، وبعضها خارجة نحو الحسب والإخوان والمال والكرامة. ومن كان حاله هكذا في حالة الحياة وبعدها فهو السعيد عند الجمهور.
Chapter on particular deliberative matters that are not great. We have previously mentioned the topics from which deliberative premises are derived concerning great matters, so we must now address the other matters, which are the deliberative topics useful in matters concerning individuals. They are in themselves not countable, but all share in the judgment of deliberation, tending towards well-being in reality, since it is by opinion. So it is necessary to know well-being. Even if the matters that are types of well-being or its parts are numerous, by their combination the state becomes good, so that the advisor has topics which he makes as deliberative premises. The First Teacher said: 'We should not limit ourselves to what the orators have in their past mention of the necessity of exaggeration and multiplication or belittling or disparagement.' And urging them and avoiding what corrupts order and removes persuasion without their knowing how it is so, and in what it is, and what corrupts the goal of the orator and diminishes his persuasion. So we say: Well-being is beautiful action from its virtue, and filling and growing life accompanied by love of hearts, and providing honor from people, and defense, and pleasant living and preservation, and abundance of wealth and contracts. And it is possible to sustain these states and seek help from them. For well-being according to common opinion is what we mentioned or what proceeds in its path. As for its components: sharpness of love, abundance of brothers, children, wealth, favor, attaining a good reputation for its dignity and states, health, beauty, and lowliness?—then glory, majesty, luck, and types of virtues such as strength. With soundness of mind, lineage, chastity, and piety. Some of these are bodily, some are psychological, and some are external such as lineage, brothers, wealth, and honor. Whoever's state is like this during life and after it, he is happy according to the common people.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the concept of 'well-being' (صلاح الحال) as it pertains to particular deliberative matters in rhetoric. The author first establishes the necessity of discussing these matters after having covered the great deliberative topics. He then quotes Aristotle (the First Teacher) to justify going beyond the limited techniques of orators. The core definition of well-being is given as a composite of virtuous action, love, honor, defense, pleasant living, and wealth. The author then enumerates its components—subjective (love, brothers, children), material (wealth, favor), and virtuous (health, beauty, glory, luck, strength, intellect, lineage, chastity, piety)—classifying them as bodily, psychological, or external. The passage concludes that one who possesses these in life and after death is considered happy by the common people. This sets the foundation for building deliberative arguments, where premises are drawn from these components.

Key Terms
صلاح الحال (salah al-hal): Well-being defined as composite of virtuous action, love, honor, defense, pleasant living, and wealth; the ultimate goal of deliberation. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable in Arabic rhetoric and philosophy.]
المشورة (al-mashwara): Deliberation; the art of advising on practical matters, relying on opinion-based premises. [Note: Standard term in Aristotelian rhetoric, but in Islamic context often linked to shura (consultation) in governance.]
السعادة (al-sa'ada): Happiness as judged by common opinion, consisting in possession of well-being components. [Note: In falsafah, often eudaimonia; here it is popular, not philosophical.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Soundness of mind

Key Takeaway
Well-being, in deliberative rhetoric, is a composite of commonly perceived goods – virtuous action, love, honor, defense, pleasant living, and wealth – and its components are classified into bodily, psychological, and external categories.
