Subdividing Well-Being: Glory, Wealth, and Gender Virtues
Author further subdivides components of well-being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the subcomponents of well-being as classified by the author, and explain the logical principle of division used.

Core Idea
Well-being is not monolithic; its parts branch into lineage, wealth, and gender-specific virtues, each with unique subparts.

In this passage from a logic textbook on rhetoric, the author demonstrates how to expand a concept by dividing it into its constituent parts. Using 'well-being' as the example, he shows that each component—glory, wealth, and even personal virtues—can be subdivided further. This method of division is essential for constructing persuasive arguments that appeal to the audience's values, a key skill in Islamic oratory and da'wah. Let's walk through the subdivisions step by step to see how the author builds a comprehensive map of well-being.

Guided Path
1
Locate the primary components
The passage begins by stating 'As for the parts of these parts'—meaning it will subdivide components already introduced. Identify the major components being subdivided: glory (al-mujtad), children's traits (beauty, masculinity, femininity), and wealth (silent properties and other assets).
2
Analyze the subdivision for glory
Glory branches to paternal and maternal uncles, indicating lineage includes both sides. The condition is that ancestors and descendants possess inherited virtues (beauty, chastity, lineage). Ask: Why does the author include both sides? How does this expand the concept?
The term 'al-mujtad' may be a variant of 'al-majd' (glory).
3
Examine the subdivision for children and gender
Each child is distinguished by a unique appealing trait: some by beauty, some by masculinity, some by femininity. The passage then highlights a special virtue for females: beauty and love of husband. Consider the rhetorical purpose: what value is being emphasized for each gender?
This subdivision shows sensitivity to audience values—a key in deliberative rhetoric.
4
Deconstruct the wealth component
Wealth is divided into 'silent properties' (non-liquid assets like land) and others (including honor from gifts, money, presents). The conclusion is a maxim: sufficiency lies in enjoyment, not hoarding. Analyze how this maxim ties the subdivisions together.
'Silent properties' is a legal term; here used to classify types of wealth.
5
Assess the logical consistency
Check if the subdivisions are exhaustive and mutually exclusive. Does the author's method follow a clear principle? Compare to Aristotelian categories or Islamic divisions (e.g., in fiqh). Suggest how a speaker could use this classification to argue for generosity or family honor.
The passage itself does not explicitly state a principle, but the division implies a means-end structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أجزاء هذه الأجزاء فكذلك فإن المجتد يتفرع إلى طرف الأعمام والأخوال جميعا إذا كان فيهم ما عددناه موروثًا عن أسلافهم وموجودا في المشايخ من الخلف وفي الأحداث منهم كما في الأولاد من الحامة والجمال والعفاف والنسالة. ثم يخص كل واحد في ولده شهوة منهم من بشره جماله ومنهم من بشره ذكورته ومنهم من بشره أنوثته؛ وللإناث فضيلة خاصة وهي الجمال وحب الزوج مثلا. وعلى هذا في اليسار وغيره فإن أجزاءه من الأموال الصامتة وغيرها، وفي الغير نحو الكرامة مثلا من المال والهدايا والتحف وغير ذلك، وبالجملة فإن الاستغناء في الاستمتاع لا في الادخار.
As for the parts of these parts, likewise: for glory branches to the side of paternal uncles and maternal uncles all together if among them is what we counted inherited from their forefathers and present in the elders from the descendants and in the youth among them, as among the children of beauty and chastity and lineage. Then each one is distinguished in his child by a desire: some of them are gladdened by his beauty, some by his masculinity, some by his femininity. And for females there is a special virtue: beauty and love of husband, for example. Similarly in wealth and other things: its parts consist of silent properties and others, and in others such as honor, for example, from money, gifts, presents, etc. And in summary: sufficiency is in enjoyment, not in hoarding.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage continues the definitional expansion by examining the parts of the previously enumerated components of well-being. Using the example of 'glory' (or 'diligence'), the author shows that it subdivides into ancestors on both the paternal and maternal sides, provided they possess inherited virtues. This illustrates how lineage is a composite good. The passage then notes that within children, each individual has unique traits that bring joy—beauty, masculinity, femininity—and highlights a special virtue for females: beauty and love of husband. Turning to wealth, it distinguishes between silent properties (e.g., land, cash) and other assets, and remarks that honor comes from gifts and presents. The conclusion is a pragmatic maxim: sufficiency lies in using goods for enjoyment, not mere accumulation. This reinforces the rhetorical principle that deliberative arguments should appeal to what people actually value: enjoyment and use, not hoarding.

Key Terms
المجتد
الأموال الصامتة
الاستغناء في الاستمتاع

Key Takeaway
The author's method of dividing well-being into parts and subparts shows that effective rhetorical argument requires precise classification of what is valuable to the audience.

Distinguishing Al-Khayr from Al-Nafi'
Author defines prominence and distinguishes good from useful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-khayr (the good) and al-nafi' (the useful) using the criterion of whether something is desired for itself or for its consequences, and apply this distinction to evaluate ethical claims in Islamic discourse.

Core Idea
The good is desired for itself; the useful is desired for its consequences.

This passage from a classical logic/rhetoric text draws a critical ethical distinction: between what is good in itself (al-khayr) and what is merely useful (al-nafi'). While both are valued, their logical roles differ. Understanding this distinction helps you avoid conflating means with ends in Islamic ethical reasoning, whether in fiqh, kalam, or daily life. The author provides a crisp criterion: al-khayr is desired both for itself and for other things, while al-nafi' is desired only for the sake of something else.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitional sentence
Find the sentence that contrasts al-khayr and al-nafi'. It appears near the end of the passage: 'والفرق بين النافع والخير أن الخير يراد لأجله وغيره، والنافع يراد لأجل غيره'.
2
Parse the criterion
The criterion is whether the thing is desired 'for itself' (لأجله) or 'for something else' (لأجل غيره). Al-khayr has both: it is desired for itself and also for other things. Al-nafi' is desired only for its external benefit.
This does not mean al-khayr cannot be useful; it can be, but its value is not exhausted by utility.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Think of 'salah' (prayer): is it khayr or nafi'? According to the criterion, salah is khayr because it is desired for itself (obedience to Allah) and also for its benefits (spiritual purification). In contrast, a medicine is nafi' because it is taken only for the sake of health, not for its own sake.
Health itself can be considered khayr because it is desired for itself (well-being) and for enabling other goods (e.g., worship).
4
Reflect on overlapping categories
Note that some things can be both khayr and nafi' (e.g., health). The distinction is about primary intention: the good is valued intrinsically, the useful instrumentally. This prevents conflating ends and means in ethical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النباهة فهي الشهرة بأصالة الرأي وجميل الفعل وهي الفضيلة عند الجمهور، وأما فضائل الجد فالصحة الغريزية التي لا يشوبها سقامته مع اقتدار على استعمال الأعضاء الآلية كلها. وفرق بين من لا أعضاء له وبين من له أعضاء معطلة، والضمان المرفوض في حكم من لا عضو له غير لسان به ينطق وأسنان بها يمضغ . وأما سعادة الجد فمعلوم أنها من صلاح الحال، وكم من نعمة كملت بالبخت لا عن اكتساب صناعي ولا عن فعل طبيعي. وإن كان في الخيرات ما تفيدها الصناعة حتى أن الصحة كثيرًا ما تفيدها الصناعة. وأما الجمال والحامة الغريزية فعن الطبيعة لا محالة. وأما الفضيلة فسنعد أجزاءها بحسب الظن في باب المدح، فهذه هي التي تشار بما يشار على واحد واحد من الناس. وقد تفيد النوافع المشتركة وهي التي يشار بها لا لها. والفرق بين النافع والخير أن الخير يراد لأجله وغيره، والنافع يراد لأجل غيره. والمعتبر
As for prominence: it is fame for soundness of opinion and beautiful action, and it is virtue according to the common people. As for the virtues of effort: innate health that is not mixed with illness, along with ability to use all instrumental organs. And there is a difference between one who has no organs and one who has disabled organs, and the removed guarantee is in the judgment of one who has no organ except a tongue with which he speaks and teeth with which he chews. As for the happiness of effort: it is known that it is from well-being. How many a blessing is completed by luck, not by artificial acquisition nor by natural action? Although among goods there is what artificial skill produces, such that health is often produced by skill. As for beauty and innate virtue: they are from nature inevitably. As for virtue: we will enumerate its parts according to opinion in the chapter on praise. So these are the things that are indicated with what is indicated about each individual person. And common benefits may be beneficial, and they are what are indicated by them, not for them. The difference between the useful (al-nafi') and the good (al-khayr) is that the good is desired for itself and for other than itself, while the useful is desired for the sake of something else. And the considered...
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage defines three key concepts for deliberative rhetoric: prominence (النباهة), virtues of effort (فضائل الجد), and happiness of effort (سعادة الجد). Prominence is identified as fame for sound opinion and beautiful action – a virtue in the eyes of the public. Virtues of effort include innate health and full bodily function; the author distinguishes between having no organs and having disabled organs, and notes that someone with only a tongue and teeth is effectively lacking organs. Happiness of effort is subsumed under well-being; luck often contributes to blessings beyond natural or artificial means. However, health can be artificial. Beauty and innate virtue are purely natural. The passage promises a later enumeration of virtue's parts. Finally, a crucial distinction is made between the good (الخير) and the useful (النافع): the good is desired for itself and also for other things, while the useful is desired only for its consequences. This is a standard Aristotelian distinction that informs ethical reasoning: e.g., health is good (desired for itself) and useful (for work), while medicine is merely useful. The text ends abruptly with 'والمعتبر' (and the considered...), suggesting continuation.

Key Terms
النباهة
الخير
النافع

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The good is temporary; the useful is eternal

Key Takeaway
The good is an end in itself; the useful is a means to an end. This distinction is foundational for ethical reasoning in Islamic thought.

Defining Happiness and Its External Components
Author defines happiness and divides its external parts including lineage, wealth, honor, and nobility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the external components of happiness according to the majority and explain how they are subdivided in the text.

Core Idea
Popular happiness is having lineage, wealth, honor, and brothers—not just spiritual perfection.

This passage from a rhetorical primer divides happiness (sa'ada) into its parts as understood by common people. The author lists external goods and then expands each into subcategories. Understanding this classification equips you to analyze how concepts are defined by enumeration in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of happiness
Find the sentence that defines the happy person (السعيد). Notice it includes both life and afterlife.
2
List the four external components
The text explicitly mentions lineage (حسب), brothers (إخوان), wealth (مال), and honor (كرامة). These are the primary parts.
3
Examine the sub‑parts of each component
For lineage, the author divides clan into paternal and maternal sides, then mentions inherited qualities. For wealth, he distinguishes liquid and non‑liquid assets. For honor, he includes gifts and presents.
4
Distinguish between nobility and virtue
Nobility (النباهة) is defined as fame for sound opinion and good action. Virtue (الفضيلة) is promised for a later chapter. Note that the text treats them as separate concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضها خارجة نحو الحسب والإخوان والمال والكرامة. ومن كان حاله هكذا في حالة الحياة وبعدها فهو السعيد عند الجمهور. وأما أجزاء هذه الأجزاء فكذلك فإن المجتد يتفرع إلى طرف الأعمام والأخوال جميعا إذا كان فيهم ما عددناه موروثًا عن أسلافهم وموجودا في المشايخ من الخلف وفي الأحداث منهم كما في الأولاد من الحامة والجمال والعفاف والنسالة. ثم يخص كل واحد في ولده شهوة منهم من بشره جماله ومنهم من بشره ذكورته ومنهم من بشره أنوثته؛ وللإناث فضيلة خاصة وهي الجمال وحبُّ الزوج مثلا. وعلى هذا في اليسار وغيره فإن أجزاءه من الأموال الصامتة وغيرها، وفي الغير نحو الكرامة مثلا من المال والهدايا والتحف وغير ذلك، وبالجملة فإن الاستغناء في الاستمتاع لا في الادخار.
وأما النباهة فهي الشهرة بأصالة الرأي وجميل الفعل وهي الفضيلة عند الجمهور، وأما فضائل الجد فالصحة الغريزية التي لا يشوبها سقامته مع اقتدار على استعمال الأعضاء الآلية كلها. وفرق بين من لا أعضاء له وبين من له أعضاء معطّلة، والضمان المرفوض في حكم من لا عضو له غير لسان به ينطق وأسنان بها يمضغ . وأما سعادة الجد فمعلوم أنها من صلاح الحال، وكم من نعمة كملت بالبخت لا عن اكتساب صناعي ولا عن فعل طبيعي. وإن كان في الخيرات ما تفيدها الصناعة حتى أن الصحة كثيرًا ما تفيدها الصناعة. وأما الجمال والحامة الغريزية فعن الطبيعة لا محالة. وأما الفضيلة فسنعد أجزاءها بحسب الظن في باب المدح، فهذه هي التي تشار بما يشار على واحد واحد من الناس.
Some of them are external, such as lineage, brothers, wealth, and honor. Whoever's state is like this in life and after it is the happy one according to the majority. As for the parts of these parts, likewise: for the clan branches into the sides of paternal uncles and maternal uncles all together if among them there is what we enumerated inherited from their ancestors and present in the elders of the followers and in the youth among them as in the children of the in-laws and beauty and chastity and offspring. Then each one specializes in his child a desire: some of them are given glad tidings of his beauty, some of them are given glad tidings of his masculinity, and some of them are given glad tidings of his femininity; and for females there is a special virtue which is beauty and love of the husband for example. And similarly concerning wealth and other things, for its parts are from non-liquid assets and others, and concerning other things such as honor for example from money, gifts, presents and so forth. In short, self-sufficiency is in the enjoyment, not in hoarding. As for nobility, it is fame for soundness of opinion and beautiful action, and it is virtue according to the majority. As for the virtues of earnestness, they are innate health that is not tainted by his illness together with ability to use all instrumental limbs. And there is a difference between one who has no limbs and one who has disabled limbs, and the rejected guarantee is in the ruling of one who has no limb except a tongue with which he speaks and teeth with which he chews. As for the happiness of earnestness, it is known that it comes from rightness of condition. How many a blessing has been perfected by luck, not through acquisition by art nor by natural action. And among goods there are those that art brings about, to the extent that health is often brought about by art. As for beauty and innate splendor they come from nature inevitably. As for virtue, we will enumerate its parts according to opinion in the chapter on praise. These are the things that are indicated by the indicator for each individual person.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the components of happiness (sa'ada) as understood by the common people (al-jumhur). The author begins by listing external goods: lineage (hasab), brothers (ikhwan), wealth (mal), and honor (karama). Whoever possesses these during life and after death is considered happy. Then he explains the sub-parts of these: for example, clan (al-mujtad) branches into paternal and maternal sides, each inheriting qualities like beauty, chastity, and offspring. Similarly, wealth is divided into liquid and non-liquid assets. The key point is that self-sufficiency consists in enjoyment, not hoarding. Nobility (nabaaha) is defined as fame for sound opinion and beautiful action, which is virtue according to the majority. The virtues of earnestness (al-jadd) are innate health and ability to use all limbs. The author contrasts those with no limbs and those with disabled limbs, and notes that the 'rejected guarantee' applies to one who has only a tongue and teeth. Happiness from earnestness comes from good condition, often by luck rather than skill. Health can be aided by art (industry), but beauty comes from nature. Finally, virtue (fadila) will be enumerated in the chapter on praise. This classification provides the orator with topics (mawadi') for praise and blame.

Key Terms
السعيد
النباهة
الفضيلة
الجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Nobility

Key Takeaway
Happiness for the majority is defined by possessing external goods (lineage, brothers, wealth, honor) in life and after death, and these goods have further subdivisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept of 'ilm (knowledge) as defined in the Shafi'i school. List its external components (e.g., teachers, books, students) using the same method of subdivision seen in the passage.
?
Identify the definition of 'al-istiqama' (uprightness) in a hadith commentary and break it down into internal (spiritual) and external (social) parts.

Distinguishing Benefit from Good in Rhetoric
Author distinguishes benefit from good and explains how orators use necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate benefit (nafi') from good (khayr) based on their ends and identify how orators use necessary concomitants to argue for benefit.

Core Idea
Good is desired for itself; benefit is desired for something else – the orator exploits this distinction.

In Islamic deliberative rhetoric (khataba), orators often appeal to benefit and good. But these terms are not synonymous. The author here sharpens the distinction: good is sought intrinsically, benefit extrinsically. Understanding this is key to analysing Islamic persuasive arguments from moral exhortation to political counsel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate where the author defines khayr and nafi'. Note that khayr is 'desired for itself and for other than itself', while nafi' is 'desired for the sake of something else'.
The Arabic: الخير يراد لأجله وغيره، والنافع يراد لأجل غيره
2
Compare the ends
The essential difference is the end: good is both intrinsic and instrumental, benefit is purely instrumental. This distinction determines how an orator presents something.
3
Recognise the arbiter of good
The passage states that good is what the 'people of opinion' (ahl al-ra'y) incline to. Ignorant choices are not counted as good by the majority. This is an epistemic criterion.
This ties to the concept of ijma' in usul al-fiqh.
4
Analyse the necessary concomitant example
The orator may not directly say 'this is beneficial' but uses a necessary concomitant: 'Exert yourself, you will be healthy.' Exertion is a lazim of exercise, which causes health. The orator implies benefit indirectly.
This is a rhetorical strategy to persuade without stating the obvious.
5
Identify the fallacy warning
The author warns that if an action is taken as absolutely harmful without specifying the aspect, it becomes a fallacy (mughalata). For example, courage is harmful when against the enemy, but not absolutely.
This is the fallacy of secundum quid (ignoring the condition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تفيد النوافع المشتركة وهي التي يشار بها لا لها. والفرق بين النافع والخير أن الخير يراد لأجله وغيره، والنافع يراد لأجل غيره. والمعتبر من الخير ما يميل إليه أهل الرأي، وإذا وصلوا إليه سكتوا عن المطلب حتى إن الجاهل إذا اختار شيئًا من جهله لا يعده الجمهور خيرا ولا يظنونه؛ والخطيب ينتفع بالاحتجاج بذلك. والمقصود والمحتاج إليه الذي هو نفس الحاجة قد يشارك النافع الذي يفعل الخاصة ويوجدها، أو الذي يحفظها ويديمها في أن المشير يشير نحوه. فإنه يشير نحو الخير ونحو النافع لكن يشير إلى أحدهما لنفسه وإلى الآخر لأجل غيره. وربَّما أشار بلازم النافع كما يقال «اتعب تصح؛ إذ التعب من لوازم الحركة الرياضية وهي علة الصحة. ثم النافع كما يوجد في إيصال الخير فكذلك في التخليص من الشر وإنه من الخيرات النافعة لا من الخيرات الحقيقية. وقد يعانيها بهذه الجهة فإن أضداد النافعة شرور فتجعل الأفعال النافعة ضارة وشرورًا؛ وأضدادها خيرات ونوافع؛ إذ الشجاعة ضارة إذا كان بالعدو. وكذلك العمل إذا كان له؛ فإذا أخذت ضارة مطلقة ولم تضف إلى الوجه الذي ينبغي أن يضاف إليه كانت مغالطة. فاعتبر بما عرفت من الفضائل نحو الإحسان ومكافأته للإنسان والجزاء وقت الحاجة إليه والرغبة فيه وغير ذلك. فإن لكل واحد منها موقع لذيذ بل عظيم كريم، ولكل خاص أشار؛ فلقوم ما يعينهم في العلم، ولقوم ما يعينهم في الكرامة، ولقوم ما يعينهم في اليسار وهلم جرا.
And common benefits may be useful, and they are those that are indicated by but not for themselves. The difference between benefit and good is that good is desired for itself and for other than itself, while benefit is desired for the sake of something else. What is considered good is what the people of opinion incline to, and when they attain it they are silent about the request, to the extent that if an ignorant person chooses something out of ignorance, the majority do not consider it good nor do they think so; and the orator benefits by arguing with that. The intended and needed thing which is the very need may share with the benefit that produces the particularity and brings it into existence, or that preserves it and perpetuates it, in that the advisor indicates towards it. For he indicates towards good and towards benefit, but he indicates towards one for itself and towards the other for something else. And perhaps he indicates by the necessary concomitant of benefit, as is said: 'Exert yourself, you will be healthy'; for exertion is among the necessary concomitants of athletic movement, and it is the cause of health. Then benefit, as it is found in delivering good, similarly it is found in delivering from evil, and it is among beneficial goods, not among real goods. And it may be considered in this regard, for the opposites of beneficial things are evils, so beneficial acts become harmful and evil; and their opposites are goods and benefits; for courage is harmful when against the enemy. And similarly action if it is for him; so if it is taken as absolutely harmful without adding the aspect to which it should be added, it becomes a fallacy. So take account of what you have known of virtues such as beneficence and its recompense to a person, and reward at the time of need and desire for it and so on. For each one has a delightful position, indeed great and noble, and each has a particular indicator; for some people, what helps them in knowledge, for some people what helps them in honor, for some people what helps them in wealth, and so forth.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage clarifies the conceptual distinction between benefit (nafi') and good (khayr) in the context of deliberative rhetoric. The author states that good is desired for its own sake even if it also yields other things, whereas benefit is desired solely for the sake of something else. The people of sound opinion (ahl al-ra'y) are the reference for what is considered good; if an ignorant person chooses something due to ignorance, the public does not deem it good. The orator (khatib) can use this in argument. The intended thing (al-maqsud) that is a need itself may share features with benefit. When advising, one may point to good for itself or to benefit for its result. Sometimes the orator indicates benefit by its necessary concomitant (lazim), as in saying 'Exert yourself, you will be healthy' – exertion is a necessary concomitant of exercise, which is the cause of health. Benefit can consist in delivering good or removing evil; it is a beneficial good, not a real good (al-khayrat al-haqiqiyya). The author warns that the opposites of beneficial things are evils, so courage might be harmful when used against an enemy. If an action is taken as absolutely harmful without specifying the proper aspect, a fallacy occurs. The passage concludes by noting that every virtue (like beneficence, reward) has a delightful position and a particular indicator that helps different people in knowledge, honor, or wealth.

الخير
اللازم

Key Takeaway
The distinction between intrinsic good and instrumental benefit is fundamental in Islamic rhetoric; orators use necessary concomitants to argue for benefit without directly mentioning it.

Criteria for Comparing Goods in Deliberative Rhetoric
Criteria for comparing goods in deliberative rhetoric

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four criteria for determining the better of two goods in deliberative rhetoric and apply them to Islamic ethical scenarios.

Core Idea
The better good is the more general, lasting, multi-beneficial, and self-intended.

In Islamic deliberative rhetoric (al-umur al-mashwuriyya), an advisor often needs to compare goods to recommend the best course of action. This passage from the logic tradition provides four concrete criteria for ranking goods. Understanding these criteria helps you construct persuasive ethical arguments in Islamic contexts, such as weighing the merits of different acts of worship or charitable deeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of assents about good and evil
The author states that assents (tasdiqat) regarding good and evil are acquired from 'the mentioned places'—likely the topics of nobility, health, etc., discussed earlier. Recognize that this is the epistemological foundation: our knowledge of good and evil comes from established topics.
This sets the stage for comparative judgments in deliberative rhetoric.
2
Locate the comparative premises in deliberative matters
The author notes that in deliberative matters, one often needs premises like 'this good is better' (afdal) or 'this benefit is more beneficial' (anfa'). Find these in the Arabic: 'أن هذا الخير أفضل، أو هذا النافع أنفع'.
These premises are the building blocks of persuasive recommendations.
3
Extract the four criteria for the better of two goods
The passage gives four criteria: (1) more general (a'amm), (2) more lasting (adwam), (3) more numerous in aspects of benefit (akthar jihat naf'), and (4) more worthy of being intended for itself (awla an yakun maqsudan li nafsihi). List them in order.
These criteria are used to compare any two goods.
4
Apply the criteria to an Islamic example
Take a concrete Islamic example: comparing the act of giving charity (sadaqah) to the act of praying extra voluntary prayers (nafl). Which is 'better'? Use the criteria: charity benefits others (more general), has lasting effects (more lasting), provides multiple benefits (physical, spiritual), and is often intended for Allah's sake (self-intended). Discuss how the criteria guide the comparison.
The criteria help resolve ethical priorities in Islamic practice.

Commentary
This passage explains how assents (tasdiqat) concerning good and evil are obtained from the topics (mawadi') previously mentioned (such as nobility, health, etc.). In deliberative rhetoric (al-umur al-mashwuriyya), which deals with future actions, one often needs comparative premises: 'this good is better' (afdal) or 'this benefit is more beneficial' (anfa'). The author provides criteria for the better of two goods: it is the more general (a'amm), more lasting (adwam), more numerous in aspects of benefit (akthar jihat naf'), and more primary in being intended for itself (awla an yakun maqsudan li nafsihi). The passage begins to consider a case where a single good is better than several goods, but the text is cut off. This is crucial for constructing persuasive arguments that rank goods and benefits, a key skill for the orator in deliberative settings.

Good vs Beneficial in Deliberative Rhetoric
Defines and distinguishes khayr and nafi' for the orator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khayr (good) and nafi' (beneficial) as the orator uses them, and identify how each functions in deliberative arguments.

Core Idea
The orator points to good for its own sake, but to benefit for the sake of something else.

In deliberative rhetoric, the advisor must know when to appeal to what is inherently good versus what is merely beneficial. This lesson clarifies the distinction and shows how the orator uses each concept to persuade an audience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of good
Find where the author distinguishes the good that people of opinion incline to from the beneficial that is sought for something else. Notice that the beneficial can produce or preserve a property.
The beneficial is not a true good but a means.
2
Examine how the orator points to each
The advisor points to good for its own sake and to benefit for the sake of something else. Sometimes he uses a necessary concomitant (lazim) of benefit, like exertion for health.
The orator's argument strategy relies on this distinction.
3
Analyze the fallacy warning
The author warns against taking an action as absolutely harmful without context. For example, courage is harmful if directed against the enemy, but good otherwise. This is a fallacy if context is ignored.
Context determines whether an action is truly beneficial or harmful.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider virtues like beneficence and recompense. Each has a delightful station; the orator must know which aspect to emphasize for different audiences (knowledge, honor, wealth).
This shows the practical use of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الخير ما يميل إليه أهل الرأي، وإذا وصلوا إليه سكتوا عن المطلب حتى إن الجاهل إذا اختار شيئًا من جهله لا يعده الجمهور خيرا ولا يظنونه؛ والخطيب ينتفع بالاحتجاج بذلك. والمقصود والمحتاج إليه الذي هو نفس الحاجة قد يشارك النافع الذي يفعل الخاصة ويوجدها، أو الذي يحفظها ويديمها في أن المشير يشير نحوه. فإنه يشير نحو الخير ونحو النافع لكن يشير إلى أحدهما لنفسه وإلى الآخر لأجل غيره. وربَّما أشار بلازم النافع كما يقال «اتعب تصح؛ إذ التعب من لوازم الحركة الرياضية وهي علة الصحة. ثم النافع كما يوجد في إيصال الخير فكذلك في التخليص من الشر وإنه من الخيرات النافعة لا من الخيرات الحقيقية. وقد يعانيها بهذه الجهة فإن أضداد النافعة شرور فتجعل الأفعال النافعة ضارة وشرورًا؛ وأضدادها خيرات ونوافع؛ إذ الشجاعة ضارة إذا كان بالعدو. وكذلك العمل إذا كان له؛ فإذا أخذت ضارة مطلقة ولم تضف إلى الوجه الذي ينبغي أن يضاف إليه كانت مغالطة. فاعتبر بما عرفت من الفضائل نحو الإحسان ومكافأته للإنسان والجزاء وقت الحاجة إليه والرغبة فيه وغير ذلك. فإن لكل واحد منها موقع لذيذ بل عظيم كريم، ولكل خاص أشار ؛ فلقوم ما يعينهم في العلم، ولقوم ما يعينهم في الكرامة، ولقوم ما يعينهم في اليسار وهلم جرا. والتصديقات في باب الخير والشر إنما تكتسب من هذه المواضع المذكورة ومن جملة ما يعتبر في الأمور المشورية هو ما يكون من باب الأشد والأضعف فإن المشير قد يحتاج إلى مقدمات هي أن هذا الخير أفضل، أو هذا النافع أنفع ; فأفضل الخيرين أعمهما وأدومهما وأكثرهما جهات نفع وأولاها بأن يكون مقصودًا لنفسه. وإذا كان الواحد من باب خير ما أفضل من عدةٍ من خير آخر إلا أن يكثر عدا فهو أفضل، والخير الذي عظيمة أفضل من عظيم خير آخر فهو أفضل مثل أن العظيم من الحكمة هو معرفة الله تعالى، والعظيم من العبادة هو المثابرة على الصواب؛ ومعرفة الله تعالى أفضل من المثابرة فالحكمة أفضل من العبادة. وما كان أيضًا نفسه أفضل فعظيم أفضل نحو القرآن فإنه أفضل من خطب النبي عليه السلام ففصيح القرآن أفضل من فصيح الخطب. وإذا كان أحد الخيرين يستتبع الآخر كالسلطان والكرامة مثلًا، والآخر لا يستتبعه دائما فالمستتبع أفضل. وربَّما كان بعض ما هو نافع آثر من بعض ما هو خير. والذي يؤثر لنفسه وإن لم يصحبه الآخر هو آثر وأفضل من الآخر إذا كان لا يؤثر إن لم يصحبه الأول مثل الصحة والجمال، فإن الصحة بلا جمال مؤثرة، والجمال بلا صحة / غير مؤثرة. والذي هو تام الوجود ويراد بنفسه فهو آثر من الذي يفعل أحدهما أو كلاهما؛ إما كاللذة فإنها في طريق التكون تطلب لذاتها، وإما كالرياضة فإنها في طريق التكون ومع ذلك فإنها تطلب لغيرها. وكذلك إذا كان أحدهما يفتقر إلى الآخر، والآخر لا يفتقر البتة فإن ذلك آثر نحو اليسار والتجارة؛ إذ اليسار أفضل. وكذلك اختيار الأفضل ومحبته فإنه أفضل من اختيار غيره. ويمكن أن يكون أحدهما أفضل من الآخر بجهة، والآخر أفضل منه بجهة أخرى نحو الذهب والحديد؛ فإن الذهب أفضل من الحديد لكونه أعزّ في الوجود، والحديد أفضل منه لكونه أعم في النفع. وكذلك الماء فإن دهن التلبسان وإن كان أعزّ وجودًا منه فهو أفضل لعموم نفعه. وأيضًا فالذي ضدُّه أعظم ضررًا فهو أعظم نفعًا. ثم الفضائل أنفسها وهي المبادئ فإنها أفضل من الأفعال الفاضلة وما اشتهاره أفضل فهو أفضل، وبالعكس. وأفضل العلمين فهو الأفضل أثرًا نحو
Of good, there is that which the people of opinion incline to, and when they reach it they become silent about the demand, so that if the ignorant chooses something out of his ignorance, the public does not count it as good nor do they suspect it. And the orator benefits by arguing with that. The intended and needed which is the very need may share with the beneficial that brings about the property and produces it, or that preserves and perpetuates it, in that the advisor points towards it. For he points towards good and towards beneficial, but he points to one for its own sake and to the other for the sake of something else. And sometimes he points by the necessary concomitant of the beneficial, as it is said, 'Exert yourself and you will be healthy'; for exertion is a necessary concomitant of physical movement, which is the cause of health. Then the beneficial, just as it exists in conveying good, likewise exists in deliverance from evil, and it is from beneficial goods, not from true goods. And it may be afflicted in this respect, for the opposites of beneficial things are evils, so they make beneficial acts harmful and evil; and their opposites are goods and beneficials; for courage is harmful if it is against the enemy. And likewise action if it is for him; so if it is taken as harmful absolutely and not added to the aspect to which it should be added, it is a fallacy. So consider what you know of virtues such as beneficence and its recompense to a person, and recompense at the time of need for it and desire for it, and so on. For each of them has a delightful, nay, great and noble station, and each has a special indication; so for some people, what helps them in knowledge, for some what helps them in honor, for some what helps them in wealth, and so on. As for assents in the chapter of good and evil, they are acquired only from these aforementioned topics, and among what is considered in deliberative matters is that which is from the category of stronger and weaker; for the advisor may need premises that this good is better, or this beneficial is more beneficial. So the better of two goods is the more general, the more enduring, the more numerous in aspects of benefit, and the most deserving of being intended for itself. And if one from the category of a certain good is better than several from another good, unless it be numerous in number, then it is better. And the good whose great is better than the great of another good is better; for example, the great of wisdom is knowledge of God Most High, and the great of worship is perseverance in what is correct; and knowledge of God Most High is better than perseverance, so wisdom is better than worship. Also, what is itself better, its great is better, like the Qur'an, for it is better than the sermons of the Prophet (peace be upon him), so the eloquent part of the Qur'an is better than the eloquent part of sermons. And if one of the two goods entails the other, like sultanate and honor for example, and the other does not always entail it, then the entailer is better. And sometimes some beneficial is more preferable than some good. And that which is preferred for itself even if the other does not accompany it is more preferable and better than the other if the latter is not preferred unless the former accompanies it; like health and beauty: health without beauty is preferable, while beauty without health is not preferable. And that which is perfect in existence and is desired for its own sake is more preferable than that which achieves one or both of them; either like pleasure, for it in the process of generation is sought for itself, or like exercise, for it in the process of generation and with that, it is sought for something else. Likewise, if one of them is dependent on the other, and the other is not dependent at all, then that is more preferable; like wealth and trade: wealth is better. Also, choosing the better and loving it is better than choosing other than it. And it is possible that one of them is better than the other in one respect, and the other is better than it in another respect, like gold and iron: gold is better than iron because it is rarer in existence, and iron is better than it because it is more general in benefit. Likewise water: the oil of the telbisān even if it is rarer in existence than it, is better due to the generality of its benefit. Also, that whose opposite is greater in harm is greater in benefit. Then the virtues themselves, which are the principles, are better than the virtuous acts; and what is more famous is better, and vice versa. And the better of the two sciences is the one that is better in effect, like
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rhetorical analysis of how to compare goods (khayr) and benefits (nafi') in deliberative contexts (al-umur al-shuriyya). It begins by distinguishing between two types of good: that which people of opinion naturally incline to (and even if an ignorant person chooses something, the public does not consider it good), and the beneficial which is sought for something else. The orator (khatib) uses arguments based on these. The passage then clarifies that the beneficial can be either what produces a property or what preserves it, and the advisor may point to good for its own sake or to beneficial for the sake of something else. Sometimes he points to a necessary concomitant (lazim) of benefit, like saying 'exert yourself and you will be healthy'—exertion is a concomitant of movement, which causes health. The beneficial exists both in conveying good and in removing evil; it is a beneficial good, not a true good in itself. The author warns against fallacies: opposites of beneficial things are evils, but if you take an action as absolutely harmful without considering its proper context, it becomes a fallacy (e.g., courage is harmful if used against an enemy, but good otherwise). The passage then moves to the topic of assents (tasdiqat) concerning good and evil, which are acquired from these topics. In deliberative matters, the advisor needs premises about which good is better or which beneficial is more beneficial. The criteria for the better of two goods are: it is more general (a'amm), more enduring (adwam), more numerous in aspects of benefit, and more deserving of being intended for itself. If one good from a category is better than several from another unless the latter are numerous, then it is better. If the 'great' (i.e., highest instance) of one good is better than the 'great' of another, then the first good is better. Example: the great of wisdom is knowledge of God, and the great of worship is perseverance; since knowledge of God is superior, wisdom is better than worship. Similarly, if a thing itself is better, its great is better: the Qur'an is better than the Prophet's sermons, so the eloquent part of the Qur'an is better than eloquent sermons. If one good entails the other (like sultanate entails honor), the entailing good is better. Sometimes a beneficial thing is more preferable than a good thing. That which is preferred for itself even without the other is more preferable than that which is not preferred without the former—e.g., health without beauty is preferable, but beauty without health is not. That which is perfect in existence and desired for itself is more preferable than that which achieves one or both; pleasure is sought for itself, while exercise is sought for something else. If one depends on the other, the independent one is more preferable (wealth vs trade). Choosing and loving the better is itself better than choosing other than it. Sometimes two things can each be better in different respects: gold is better for rarity, iron for general benefit; similarly water is more beneficial than a rare oil because of its general utility. That whose opposite is more harmful is itself more beneficial. Finally, the virtues themselves (the principles) are better than virtuous acts; what is more famous (in goodness) is better, and vice versa. The passage concludes by stating that the better of two sciences is that which is better in effect (athar).

Key Terms
الخير
النافع
المشير
الخطيب
الخاصة
لازم النافع

A) Because courage is always harmful.
B) Because the context makes it harmful.
C) Because the enemy is stronger.
D) Because courage is not a virtue.

Key Takeaway
The orator must distinguish between khayr (good in itself) and nafi' (beneficial as a means) and avoid fallacies by considering context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a contemporary Islamic issue where an orator might appeal to khayr versus nafi'. Describe how the orator would phrase each appeal.

Criteria for the Better of Two Goods
Lists criteria for comparing goods and benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four criteria (general, enduring, more beneficial aspects, intended for itself) to determine which of two Islamic goods is better, and use additional sub-criteria.

Core Idea
The better good is more general, more enduring, more multifaceted in benefit, and more desired for its own sake.

In deliberative rhetoric, the advisor often needs to argue that one good is better than another. The author provides concrete criteria for such comparisons, with examples from Islamic sources like wisdom versus worship, and the Qur'an versus sermons.

Guided Path
1
List the four main criteria
Find the sentence that states 'the better of two goods is the more general, the more enduring, the more numerous in aspects of benefit, and the most deserving of being intended for itself.'
These are the primary measures.
2
Apply to the wisdom vs worship example
The author says the great of wisdom (knowledge of God) is better than the great of worship (perseverance). Check if knowledge of God is more general, enduring, etc. than perseverance.
This shows how criteria work in practice.
3
Apply to the Qur'an vs sermons example
The Qur'an itself is better than the Prophet's sermons, so its eloquent part is better. This uses the criterion that if the thing itself is better, its great is better.
Notice the rule about the 'great' (c al-azim).
4
Consider entailment
If one good entails another (like sultanate entails honor), the entailer is better. This is an additional sub-criterion.
Entailment indicates superiority.
5
Evaluate other sub-criteria
Look for rules like: one good better than several of another unless numerous, beneficial can be more preferable than good, etc.
These refine the comparative judgments.

من الخير ما يميل إليه أهل الرأي، وإذا وصلوا إليه سكتوا عن المطلب حتى إن الجاهل إذا اختار شيئًا من جهله لا يعده الجمهور خيرا ولا يظنونه؛ والخطيب ينتفع بالاحتجاج بذلك. والمقصود والمحتاج إليه الذي هو نفس الحاجة قد يشارك النافع الذي يفعل الخاصة ويوجدها، أو الذي يحفظها ويديمها في أن المشير يشير نحوه. فإنه يشير نحو الخير ونحو النافع لكن يشير إلى أحدهما لنفسه وإلى الآخر لأجل غيره. وربَّما أشار بلازم النافع كما يقال «اتعب تصح؛ إذ التعب من لوازم الحركة الرياضية وهي علة الصحة. ثم النافع كما يوجد في إيصال الخير فكذلك في التخليص من الشر وإنه من الخيرات النافعة لا من الخيرات الحقيقية. وقد يعانيها بهذه الجهة فإن أضداد النافعة شرور فتجعل الأفعال النافعة ضارة وشرورًا؛ وأضدادها خيرات ونوافع؛ إذ الشجاعة ضارة إذا كان بالعدو. وكذلك العمل إذا كان له؛ فإذا أخذت ضارة مطلقة ولم تضف إلى الوجه الذي ينبغي أن يضاف إليه كانت مغالطة. فاعتبر بما عرفت من الفضائل نحو الإحسان ومكافأته للإنسان والجزاء وقت الحاجة إليه والرغبة فيه وغير ذلك. فإن لكل واحد منها موقع لذيذ بل عظيم كريم، ولكل خاص أشار ؛ فلقوم ما يعينهم في العلم، ولقوم ما يعينهم في الكرامة، ولقوم ما يعينهم في اليسار وهلم جرا. والتصديقات في باب الخير والشر إنما تكتسب من هذه المواضع المذكورة ومن جملة ما يعتبر في الأمور المشورية هو ما يكون من باب الأشد والأضعف فإن المشير قد يحتاج إلى مقدمات هي أن هذا الخير أفضل، أو هذا النافع أنفع ; فأفضل الخيرين أعمهما وأدومهما وأكثرهما جهات نفع وأولاها بأن يكون مقصودًا لنفسه. وإذا كان الواحد من باب خير ما أفضل من عدةٍ من خير آخر إلا أن يكثر عدا فهو أفضل، والخير الذي عظيمة أفضل من عظيم خير آخر فهو أفضل مثل أن العظيم من الحكمة هو معرفة الله تعالى، والعظيم من العبادة هو المثابرة على الصواب؛ ومعرفة الله تعالى أفضل من المثابرة فالحكمة أفضل من العبادة. وما كان أيضًا نفسه أفضل فعظيم أفضل نحو القرآن فإنه أفضل من خطب النبي عليه السلام ففصيح القرآن أفضل من فصيح الخطب. وإذا كان أحد الخيرين يستتبع الآخر كالسلطان والكرامة مثلًا، والآخر لا يستتبعه دائما فالمستتبع أفضل. وربَّما كان بعض ما هو نافع آثر من بعض ما هو خير. والذي يؤثر لنفسه وإن لم يصحبه الآخر هو آثر وأفضل من الآخر إذا كان لا يؤثر إن لم يصحبه الأول مثل الصحة والجمال، فإن الصحة بلا جمال مؤثرة، والجمال بلا صحة / غير مؤثرة. والذي هو تام الوجود ويراد بنفسه فهو آثر من الذي يفعل أحدهما أو كلاهما؛ إما كاللذة فإنها في طريق التكون تطلب لذاتها، وإما كالرياضة فإنها في طريق التكون ومع ذلك فإنها تطلب لغيرها. وكذلك إذا كان أحدهما يفتقر إلى الآخر، والآخر لا يفتقر البتة فإن ذلك آثر نحو اليسار والتجارة؛ إذ اليسار أفضل. وكذلك اختيار الأفضل ومحبته فإنه أفضل من اختيار غيره. ويمكن أن يكون أحدهما أفضل من الآخر بجهة، والآخر أفضل منه بجهة أخرى نحو الذهب والحديد؛ فإن الذهب أفضل من الحديد لكونه أعزّ في الوجود، والحديد أفضل منه لكونه أعم في النفع. وكذلك الماء فإن دهن التلبسان وإن كان أعزّ وجودًا منه فهو أفضل لعموم نفعه. وأيضًا فالذي ضدُّه أعظم ضررًا فهو أعظم نفعًا. ثم الفضائل أنفسها وهي المبادئ فإنها أفضل من الأفعال الفاضلة وما اشتهاره أفضل فهو أفضل، وبالعكس. وأفضل العلمين فهو الأفضل أثرًا نحو
Of good, there is that which the people of opinion incline to, and when they reach it they become silent about the demand, so that if the ignorant chooses something out of his ignorance, the public does not count it as good nor do they suspect it. And the orator benefits by arguing with that. The intended and needed which is the very need may share with the beneficial that brings about the property and produces it, or that preserves and perpetuates it, in that the advisor points towards it. For he points towards good and towards beneficial, but he points to one for its own sake and to the other for the sake of something else. And sometimes he points by the necessary concomitant of the beneficial, as it is said, 'Exert yourself and you will be healthy'; for exertion is a necessary concomitant of physical movement, which is the cause of health. Then the beneficial, just as it exists in conveying good, likewise exists in deliverance from evil, and it is from beneficial goods, not from true goods. And it may be afflicted in this respect, for the opposites of beneficial things are evils, so they make beneficial acts harmful and evil; and their opposites are goods and beneficials; for courage is harmful if it is against the enemy. And likewise action if it is for him; so if it is taken as harmful absolutely and not added to the aspect to which it should be added, it is a fallacy. So consider what you know of virtues such as beneficence and its recompense to a person, and recompense at the time of need for it and desire for it, and so on. For each of them has a delightful, nay, great and noble station, and each has a special indication; so for some people, what helps them in knowledge, for some what helps them in honor, for some what helps them in wealth, and so on. As for assents in the chapter of good and evil, they are acquired only from these aforementioned topics, and among what is considered in deliberative matters is that which is from the category of stronger and weaker; for the advisor may need premises that this good is better, or this beneficial is more beneficial. So the better of two goods is the more general, the more enduring, the more numerous in aspects of benefit, and the most deserving of being intended for itself. And if one from the category of a certain good is better than several from another good, unless it be numerous in number, then it is better. And the good whose great is better than the great of another good is better; for example, the great of wisdom is knowledge of God Most High, and the great of worship is perseverance in what is correct; and knowledge of God Most High is better than perseverance, so wisdom is better than worship. Also, what is itself better, its great is better, like the Qur'an, for it is better than the sermons of the Prophet (peace be upon him), so the eloquent part of the Qur'an is better than the eloquent part of sermons. And if one of the two goods entails the other, like sultanate and honor for example, and the other does not always entail it, then the entailer is better. And sometimes some beneficial is more preferable than some good. And that which is preferred for itself even if the other does not accompany it is more preferable and better than the other if the latter is not preferred unless the former accompanies it; like health and beauty: health without beauty is preferable, while beauty without health is not preferable. And that which is perfect in existence and is desired for its own sake is more preferable than that which achieves one or both of them; either like pleasure, for it in the process of generation is sought for itself, or like exercise, for it in the process of generation and with that, it is sought for something else. Likewise, if one of them is dependent on the other, and the other is not dependent at all, then that is more preferable; like wealth and trade: wealth is better. Also, choosing the better and loving it is better than choosing other than it. And it is possible that one of them is better than the other in one respect, and the other is better than it in another respect, like gold and iron: gold is better than iron because it is rarer in existence, and iron is better than it because it is more general in benefit. Likewise water: the oil of the telbisān even if it is rarer in existence than it, is better due to the generality of its benefit. Also, that whose opposite is greater in harm is greater in benefit. Then the virtues themselves, which are the principles, are better than the virtuous acts; and what is more famous is better, and vice versa. And the better of the two sciences is the one that is better in effect, like
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rhetorical analysis of how to compare goods (khayr) and benefits (nafi') in deliberative contexts (al-umur al-shuriyya). It begins by distinguishing between two types of good: that which people of opinion naturally incline to (and even if an ignorant person chooses something, the public does not consider it good), and the beneficial which is sought for something else. The orator (khatib) uses arguments based on these. The passage then clarifies that the beneficial can be either what produces a property or what preserves it, and the advisor may point to good for its own sake or to beneficial for the sake of something else. Sometimes he points to a necessary concomitant (lazim) of benefit, like saying 'exert yourself and you will be healthy'—exertion is a concomitant of movement, which causes health. The beneficial exists both in conveying good and in removing evil; it is a beneficial good, not a true good in itself. The author warns against fallacies: opposites of beneficial things are evils, but if you take an action as absolutely harmful without considering its proper context, it becomes a fallacy (e.g., courage is harmful if used against an enemy, but good otherwise). The passage then moves to the topic of assents (tasdiqat) concerning good and evil, which are acquired from these topics. In deliberative matters, the advisor needs premises about which good is better or which beneficial is more beneficial. The criteria for the better of two goods are: it is more general (a'amm), more enduring (adwam), more numerous in aspects of benefit, and more deserving of being intended for itself. If one good from a category is better than several from another unless the latter are numerous, then it is better. If the 'great' (i.e., highest instance) of one good is better than the 'great' of another, then the first good is better. Example: the great of wisdom is knowledge of God, and the great of worship is perseverance; since knowledge of God is superior, wisdom is better than worship. Similarly, if a thing itself is better, its great is better: the Qur'an is better than the Prophet's sermons, so the eloquent part of the Qur'an is better than eloquent sermons. If one good entails the other (like sultanate entails honor), the entailing good is better. Sometimes a beneficial thing is more preferable than a good thing. That which is preferred for itself even without the other is more preferable than that which is not preferred without the former—e.g., health without beauty is preferable, but beauty without health is not. That which is perfect in existence and desired for itself is more preferable than that which achieves one or both; pleasure is sought for itself, while exercise is sought for something else. If one depends on the other, the independent one is more preferable (wealth vs trade). Choosing and loving the better is itself better than choosing other than it. Sometimes two things can each be better in different respects: gold is better for rarity, iron for general benefit; similarly water is more beneficial than a rare oil because of its general utility. That whose opposite is more harmful is itself more beneficial. Finally, the virtues themselves (the principles) are better than virtuous acts; what is more famous (in goodness) is better, and vice versa. The passage concludes by stating that the better of two sciences is that which is better in effect (athar).

According to the passage, why is wisdom (al-hikma) better than worship (al-ibada)?

Key Takeaway
The better good is identified by its generality, endurance, multiplicity of benefit, and being intended for itself, along with sub-criteria like entailment and greatness.

Preferability and Context: When Beneficial Outweighs Good
Discusses when beneficial things are more preferable than good things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of preferability (afdaliyya) in cases where a beneficial thing is more preferable than a good, using context-dependent examples from the passage.

Core Idea
Sometimes a beneficial thing is more preferable than a good, especially when the beneficial is needed for existence or has greater general benefit.

The passage provides interesting cases where what is beneficial (nafi') can be more preferable than what is good (khayr). Understanding these exceptions is crucial for nuanced rhetorical and ethical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Find the statement that beneficial can be more preferable than good
Locate the line: 'sometimes some beneficial is more preferable than some good.' This is the key principle.
This contradicts a simplistic hierarchy.
2
Examine the health vs beauty example
Health without beauty is preferable, but beauty without health is not. Health is beneficial and also good; beauty is good but dependent on health. So the beneficial (health) outweighs the good (beauty) when the good depends on it.
This shows the principle of independence.
3
Analyze the gold vs iron and water vs oil examples
Gold is better by rarity, iron by general benefit; water is better than rare oil due to general utility. Here, the more beneficial (general benefit) trumps the rare good.
Context determines which aspect is prioritized.
4
Apply the principle of opposite harm
That whose opposite is greater in harm is greater in benefit. For example, if the opposite of a beneficial thing is a great evil, that beneficial thing becomes highly preferable.
This is another context-dependent rule.
5
Consider pleasure vs exercise
Pleasure is sought for itself (perfect existence), exercise is sought for something else. Pleasure is more preferable as it is desired for its own sake, even though both are beneficial.
Being intended for itself increases preferability.

Of good, there is that which the people of opinion incline to, and when they reach it they become silent about the demand, so that if the ignorant chooses something out of his ignorance, the public does not count it as good nor do they suspect it. And the orator benefits by arguing with that. The intended and needed which is the very need may share with the beneficial that brings about the property and produces it, or that preserves and perpetuates it, in that the advisor points towards it. For he points towards good and towards beneficial, but he points to one for its own sake and to the other for the sake of something else. And sometimes he points by the necessary concomitant of the beneficial, as it is said, 'Exert yourself and you will be healthy'; for exertion is a necessary concomitant of physical movement, which is the cause of health. Then the beneficial, just as it exists in conveying good, likewise exists in deliverance from evil, and it is from beneficial goods, not from true goods. And it may be afflicted in this respect, for the opposites of beneficial things are evils, so they make beneficial acts harmful and evil; and their opposites are goods and beneficials; for courage is harmful if it is against the enemy. And likewise action if it is for him; so if it is taken as harmful absolutely and not added to the aspect to which it should be added, it is a fallacy. So consider what you know of virtues such as beneficence and its recompense to a person, and recompense at the time of need for it and desire for it, and so on. For each of them has a delightful, nay, great and noble station, and each has a special indication; so for some people, what helps them in knowledge, for some what helps them in honor, for some what helps them in wealth, and so on. As for assents in the chapter of good and evil, they are acquired only from these aforementioned topics, and among what is considered in deliberative matters is that which is from the category of stronger and weaker; for the advisor may need premises that this good is better, or this beneficial is more beneficial. So the better of two goods is the more general, the more enduring, the more numerous in aspects of benefit, and the most deserving of being intended for itself. And if one from the category of a certain good is better than several from another good, unless it be numerous in number, then it is better. And the good whose great is better than the great of another good is better; for example, the great of wisdom is knowledge of God Most High, and the great of worship is perseverance in what is correct; and knowledge of God Most High is better than perseverance, so wisdom is better than worship. Also, what is itself better, its great is better, like the Qur'an, for it is better than the sermons of the Prophet (peace be upon him), so the eloquent part of the Qur'an is better than the eloquent part of sermons. And if one of the two goods entails the other, like sultanate and honor for example, and the other does not always entail it, then the entailer is better. And sometimes some beneficial is more preferable than some good. And that which is preferred for itself even if the other does not accompany it is more preferable and better than the other if the latter is not preferred unless the former accompanies it; like health and beauty: health without beauty is preferable, while beauty without health is not preferable. And that which is perfect in existence and is desired for its own sake is more preferable than that which achieves one or both of them; either like pleasure, for it in the process of generation is sought for itself, or like exercise, for it in the process of generation and with that, it is sought for something else. Likewise, if one of them is dependent on the other, and the other is not dependent at all, then that is more preferable; like wealth and trade: wealth is better. Also, choosing the better and loving it is better than choosing other than it. And it is possible that one of them is better than the other in one respect, and the other is better than it in another respect, like gold and iron: gold is better than iron because it is rarer in existence, and iron is better than it because it is more general in benefit. Likewise water: the oil of the telbisān even if it is rarer in existence than it, is better due to the generality of its benefit. Also, that whose opposite is greater in harm is greater in benefit. Then the virtues themselves, which are the principles, are better than the virtuous acts; and what is more famous is better, and vice versa. And the better of the two sciences is the one that is better in effect, like
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rhetorical analysis of how to compare goods (khayr) and benefits (nafi') in deliberative contexts (al-umur al-shuriyya). It begins by distinguishing between two types of good: that which people of opinion naturally incline to (and even if an ignorant person chooses something, the public does not consider it good), and the beneficial which is sought for something else. The orator (khatib) uses arguments based on these. The passage then clarifies that the beneficial can be either what produces a property or what preserves it, and the advisor may point to good for its own sake or to beneficial for the sake of something else. Sometimes he points to a necessary concomitant (lazim) of benefit, like saying 'exert yourself and you will be healthy'—exertion is a concomitant of movement, which causes health. The beneficial exists both in conveying good and in removing evil; it is a beneficial good, not a true good in itself. The author warns against fallacies: opposites of beneficial things are evils, but if you take an action as absolutely harmful without considering its proper context, it becomes a fallacy (e.g., courage is harmful if used against an enemy, but good otherwise). The passage then moves to the topic of assents (tasdiqat) concerning good and evil, which are acquired from these topics. In deliberative matters, the advisor needs premises about which good is better or which beneficial is more beneficial. The criteria for the better of two goods are: it is more general (a'amm), more enduring (adwam), more numerous in aspects of benefit, and more deserving of being intended for itself. If one good from a category is better than several from another unless the latter are numerous, then it is better. If the 'great' (i.e., highest instance) of one good is better than the 'great' of another, then the first good is better. Example: the great of wisdom is knowledge of God, and the great of worship is perseverance; since knowledge of God is superior, wisdom is better than worship. Similarly, if a thing itself is better, its great is better: the Qur'an is better than the Prophet's sermons, so the eloquent part of the Qur'an is better than eloquent sermons. If one good entails the other (like sultanate entails honor), the entailing good is better. Sometimes a beneficial thing is more preferable than a good thing. That which is preferred for itself even without the other is more preferable than that which is not preferred without the former—e.g., health without beauty is preferable, but beauty without health is not. That which is perfect in existence and desired for itself is more preferable than that which achieves one or both; pleasure is sought for itself, while exercise is sought for something else. If one depends on the other, the independent one is more preferable (wealth vs trade). Choosing and loving the better is itself better than choosing other than it. Sometimes two things can each be better in different respects: gold is better for rarity, iron for general benefit; similarly water is more beneficial than a rare oil because of its general utility. That whose opposite is more harmful is itself more beneficial. Finally, the virtues themselves (the principles) are better than virtuous acts; what is more famous (in goodness) is better, and vice versa. The passage concludes by stating that the better of two sciences is that which is better in effect (athar).

جهة

Definition of Virtue and Its Parts in Islamic Rhetoric
Author defines the beautiful, the praised, virtue, and lists its constituent parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definitions of al-jamīl, al-maḥmūd, and al-faḍīlah as presented in this passage, and list the eight parts of virtue with their Shariah-based definitions.

Core Idea
Virtue is a faculty for acquiring good, and its parts are measured by Shariah.

In this passage, the author sets out the key definitions for the rhetoric chapter on praise and blame. He defines the beautiful (al-jamīl), the praised (al-maḥmūd), and virtue (al-faḍīlah), then enumerates its eight parts. Understanding these definitions is essential for the orator who seeks to win acceptance through moral credibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key terms defined
The passage defines al-jamīl, al-maḥmūd, and al-faḍīlah. Find the Arabic sentences that contain these definitions.
Look for phrases like 'الجميل هو المختار لأجل نفسه'.
2
Analyze the definition of al-jamīl
Al-jamīl is 'al-mukhtār li-ajli nafsihi' (chosen for its own sake). This defines it by its purpose.
It is a definition by goal (ghāyah).
3
Analyze the definition of al-maḥmūd
Al-maḥmūd is chosen not for another thing but for its goodness, making it beautiful in that respect. It is a subset of al-jamīl.
The author links maḥmūd to jamīl through the concept of goodness.
4
Analyze the definition of al-faḍīlah
Virtue is a power/faculty (quwwa/malaka) of good deliberation to acquire what is good or seen as good, and it performs and preserves great religious matters. It is a type (naw') of al-jamīl.
Note the two aspects: power of deliberation and action/preservation of religious matters.
5
List and understand the eight parts of virtue
Extract the eight parts: al-birr, al-shajā'ah, al-murū'ah, kibr al-himmah, al-sakhā', al-ḥilm, al-layn, al-ḥikmah. Then note the definitions of three: al-birr (just virtue measured by Shariah), al-shajā'ah (enables jihad), al-sakhā' (enables beautiful acts).
The author emphasizes that some virtues extend benefit to others.

Source Text

Chapter on Topics of Contrast, which is the chapter on praise and blame. As for the useful types in praise and blame related to virtue and vice and what runs in their course, indeed they are useful in praise and blame and may also be useful in preparing the orator for belief in his statement even if it is in a topic other than contrast. That is because when he displays his own virtue, he makes himself worthy of people accepting his statement, and likewise when he blames his opponent, he exposes him to people rejecting his statement. And the praises attributed to virtue, and what follows virtue of beauty, goodness, and other praises that may by praising them extend from people and angels to other persons who are praised by them. The parts of virtue are: righteousness, courage, chivalry, high aspiration, generosity, forbearance, gentleness, and wisdom. So inevitably among virtues are those whose good extends to others besides the virtuous, like righteousness, courage, and generosity. For righteousness is a just virtue, measured according to the measure of the Shariah for everything it deserves. Courage is a virtue by which a person performs acts of jihad as commanded by the Shariah in support of it. Generosity is a virtue by which a person performs beautiful acts.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section within the chapter on 'al-munāfariyāt' (topics of contrast), which deals with praise and blame in rhetoric. The author begins by stating that the useful types in praise and blame related to virtue and vice also help the orator gain acceptance for his speech, even outside this specific chapter. By displaying his own virtue, the orator makes himself credible, and by blaming his opponent, he discredits him. The praises attributed to virtue and its accompaniments (beauty, goodness) can extend from praising people and angels to praising others. The author then defines key terms: 'al-jamīl' (beautiful) is what is chosen for its own sake; 'al-maḥmūd' (praised) is what is chosen for its goodness, making it beautiful in that respect. Virtue is a type of the beautiful because it is a power (quwwa) or faculty (malaka) of good deliberation to acquire what is good or perceived as good. It performs and preserves great religious matters. The author then lists the parts of virtue: righteousness (al-birr), courage (al-shajā'ah), chivalry (al-murū'ah), high aspiration (kibr al-himmah), generosity (al-sakhā'), forbearance (al-ḥilm), gentleness (al-layn), and wisdom (al-ḥikmah). He notes that some virtues extend their benefit to others, such as righteousness, courage, and generosity. He defines each briefly: righteousness is a just virtue measured by the Shariah; courage enables performing jihad as commanded; generosity enables performing beautiful acts. This passage uses Islamic concepts like Shariah, jihad, and praiseworthy qualities central to Islamic ethics.

Rhetorical Definitions of Virtues: Rasm vs Hadd
Author defines virtues as apparent descriptions for rhetoric.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the elements of a rhetorical description (rasm) in the definition of a virtue and explain how it differs from a true logical definition (hadd).

Core Idea
Rusum describe virtues by apparent actions; hadd captures essential reality.

In classical Islamic logic, a true definition (hadd) penetrates to the essence of a thing. However, in the rhetorical art, orators use descriptions (rusum) based on observable features to praise or blame. In this passage, the author defines several virtues by the faculties they govern and the actions they produce. These are not complete logical definitions but persuasive sketches. Understanding this difference is crucial for evaluating arguments in the five arts: burhan, jadal, khitaba, shi'r, and mughalata.

Guided Path
1
Identify the virtue being defined
Locate each virtue in the Arabic text. The passage mentions chastity (al-‘iffa), manliness (al-muruwwa), high aspiration (kibar al-himma), effort and gentleness (al-juhd wa al-layn), and associated vices.
The virtues are defined in succession.
2
Extract the faculty and action
For each virtue, note which soul faculty it regulates (e.g., concupiscent for chastity) and what specific action or measure is described (e.g., spending wealth to the extent permitted by Sharia).
These elements form the genus (faculty) and differentia (action) of the rasm.
3
Determine if the definition could be a hadd
A true hadd captures the essence (quiddity) of the virtue. Ask: does this description reveal the internal state of the soul, or only outward behavior? The passage states these are 'rusum bi-hasab al-zawahir'—according to appearances.
The author explicitly denies these are hadd.
4
Distinguish rasm from hadd
Compare with a true hadd example from logic, e.g., 'human is a rational animal.' Here, the rasm of chastity uses a faculty (genus) and a Sharia limit (differentia), but it does not define the essence of chastity as a virtue.
Rasm is sufficient for rhetorical persuasion but not for scientific certainty.
5
Apply to rhetorical context
Why would an orator use a rasm rather than a hadd? To evoke praise or blame in an audience familiar with observable actions. For example, describing a leader as 'generous in feeding' (manliness) incites admiration without needing a philosophical account of generosity.
This passage exemplifies the khitaba (oratory) art within the five arts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ببذل المال والعفّة فضيلة يكون المرء بها في استعمال القوة الشهوانية البدنية على القدر الذي ترخض فيه الشريعة. والمروة فضيلة يعمل النبيل بالتوسع في الإطعام وصغر النفس. وكبر الهمة فضيلة يكون المرء بها فعالا لأفعال عظيمة المنزلة من الجهد واللين فضيلة في الرأي يكون المرء بها حسن التعقل والمشورة نحو الخيرات والجميل. وما يكون في المقابلة لكل واحد منها من الرذائل فهو الجور والجبن والدناءة والفجور والبذالة والسفالة والحماقة؛ وهذه رسوم بحسب الظواهر لهذه القضايا التي يمدح بها. ولا يبعد أن يصدر عن الشجاع فعل لا يصدر إلا عن شجاع أو يلحقه انفعال لا يكون إلا للشجاع، وكذلك قد يصدر عن السخي وغيره، ويلحقه أيضًا ما لا يكون إلا له.
Spending wealth and chastity is a virtue by which a person is in the use of the bodily concupiscent faculty to the extent permitted by the Sharia. And manliness is a virtue by which the noble person acts by expanding in feeding and smallness of self. And high aspiration is a virtue by which a person is doer of actions of great station. And effort and gentleness is a virtue in opinion by which a person is good in reasoning and consultation towards good things and beautiful. And what corresponds to each of these from vices is injustice, cowardice, baseness, debauchery, profligacy, lowness, and foolishness; and these are descriptions according to appearances of these qualities that are praised. And it is not far that an action issues from the brave that does not issue except from a brave, or an affection befalls him that is not except for the brave, and likewise it may issue from the generous and others, and it is also accompanied by what is not except for him.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage provides apparent definitions (rusum) of key virtues and their corresponding vices, as used in rhetorical praise. The author defines each virtue by the soul's faculty it governs and the Sharia-determined measure. For example, chastity (al-‘iffa) regulates the concupiscent faculty. Manliness (al-muruwwa) involves generosity in feeding and humility. The vices are the opposites of these virtues. The author then notes that certain actions or affections are exclusive to specific virtues, though some may overlap. This is not a logical definition (hadd) but a rhetorical description tailored for oratory.

Metaphor and Transition in Islamic Oratory
Author explains how the orator uses metaphor and transitions between praise and advice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the use of metaphor (isti‘ara) and transition (intiqal) in rhetorical speeches from Islamic oratory and explain how they serve persuasion.

Core Idea
Rhetoric uses metaphor and transition not to describe reality but to persuade the audience.

In this passage, the author explains how the orator (khatib) employs stylistic devices to make his speech more effective. You will learn to recognize two key techniques: using metaphorical language (isti‘ara) and skillfully transitioning between praise and advice. These are not just literary ornaments; they are tools of persuasion that shape the audience's perception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three examples of metaphorical inversion
The passage gives three cases: a wicked person called 'gentle in company' (meaning forbearing), an angry person called 'noble and generous', and a fool called 'sound-hearted'. List these and note the contrast between the literal description and the intended meaning.
Each example uses a positive term to describe a negative trait.
2
Determine the intended effect
Consider why the orator would invert descriptions. This technique is a form of euphemism or praise, often used to soften criticism or to flatter the audience. It can also be a rhetorical strategy to make the audience more receptive.
The effect depends on context; the orator may be aiming for ta'weel (favorable interpretation).
3
Examine the transition technique
The author mentions that the orator can transition from praise to advice and vice versa. Reflect on how this keeps the audience engaged and allows the orator to balance encouragement and guidance.
Transitions are a structural tool to maintain interest.
4
Analyze the emphatic language
The passage recommends using glorifying and exalting words, like 'unique of his age'. Identify such phrases in the examples and consider how they amplify the impact of praise or advice.
Emphatic language reinforces the message and adds authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الخطيب له أن يستعمل في الخطابة من الألفاظ ما يكون بطريق الاستعارة أو الكناية كما يقول للفسيق إنه لطيف العشرة يعني أنه حليم، وللغضوب القطوب إنه نبيل ذو سخت، وللأبله المغفل عن اللذات إنه سليم القلب. وله أن ينتقل من المدح إلى المشورة، ومن المشورة إلى المدح، وينبغي أن يؤكّد أمر المدح وأمر المشورة بألفاظ معظمة مفخمة كما يقال إنه فريد عصره وقدوة أهل زمانه مثلا.
Then the orator may use in oratory of words that are metaphorical or allegorical, as when he says of a wicked person that he is gentle in company, meaning he is forbearing, and of an angry frowning person that he is noble and generous, and of a stupid person heedless of pleasures that he is sound-hearted. And he may transition from praise to advice, and from advice to praise, and he should emphasize the matter of praise and advice with glorifying and exalting words, as it is said he is the unique of his age and the model of his time, for example.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage presents rhetorical techniques for the orator (khatib). It first describes the use of metaphor (isti‘ara) and allusion (kinaya) to invert descriptions: a wicked person is called 'gentle in company' meaning forbearing, an angry person is called 'noble and generous', and a fool is called 'sound-hearted'. This is a form of euphemism or praise through opposites. Then it advises transitioning between praise (madḥ) and advice (mashwara) to maintain audience engagement. Finally, it recommends using emphatic, glorifying language to strengthen the impact, exemplified by phrases like 'unique of his age' and 'model of his time'.
