Key Takeaway
Rhetoric in Islamic logic is an art of persuasion that uses metaphor, transition, and amplification to influence the audience, distinct from logical demonstration.

Seven Types of Voluntary Actions
Author enumerates seven causes of voluntary actions and explains their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the seven types of voluntary actions based on their causes as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Voluntary actions are driven by seven distinct causes: chance, nature, compulsion, character, thought, anger, and desire.

Understanding the classification of voluntary actions is essential for analyzing human behavior in Islamic ethics and psychology. In this passage, the author breaks down voluntary actions into seven categories based on their underlying causes. By learning this classification, you will gain a tool to systematically evaluate the motivations behind actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three broad categories
The author first groups voluntary actions into those following habit/character, animal impulse (desire or anger), and intellectual/logical impulse. Note these initial divisions.
2
List the seven specific types
From the initial divisions, the author enumerates seven types: accidental, natural, compulsive, character-based, intellectual, anger-based, desire-based. Write them down in order.
3
Define each type
For each of the seven, recall the author's definition: accidental (unintentional, no habit), natural (constant, frequent), compulsive (coerced), character-based (from acquired habit), intellectual (from thought, any purpose), anger-based (defense/victory), desire-based (pleasure).
4
Understand the characteristics
Note that accidental actions are not praiseworthy; natural actions are constant; actions from nature can create character but are distinct from innate dispositions.
5
Apply to a person's state
The author mentions that these causes vary by age, piety, wealth, etc. Try to imagine how a youth vs. an elder might exhibit different types.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفضي إلى الثاني منهما نحو الخوف والغضب والشهوة وغير ذلك. والأفعال الإرادية [أ] منها ما تتبع العادة والخلق [ب] ومنها ما يتبع سوقاً حيوانيا؛ إما نحو اللذة وهو الشهوة، وإما نحو الدفاع والغلبة وهو الغضب. [ت] ومنها ما يتبع سوقا فكريًا أو سوقاً منطقياً. ويشبه أن يكون المعنى بالفكري ما يصدر عن الفكر نحو أي غرض كان وإن كان الغرض غير عقلي أو غير جميل، وبالمنطقي ما يكون نحو الجميل العقلي، ويشبه أن يكون المعنى بالفكري التخيلي وبالمنطقي الفكري بالحقيقة.

وهذه الأقسام منحصرة في سبعة؛ [1] الاتفاقي، [ب] والطبيعي، [ت] والاستكراهي، [ث] والخُلقي، [ج] والفكري، [ح] والغضبي، [خ] والشهواني. ويمكن أن يكون قسمة هذه الأسباب بحسب الاختلاف بين الناس وأحوالهم وهممهم مثل قسمتهم إلى الأحداث والشيوخ، وإلى العباد والفساق، وإلى الأغنياء والفقراء، وإلى الأشراف والأحلاف مثلا. فإن لكل [٣٥٨] طائفة منهم من العادات والأخلاق ما يصدر عنه بالذات أفعال مخصوصة بها من الخير والشر، والأفعال الصادرة عن الاتفاق غير مضبوطة ولا محدودة ولا يكون من جملة ما يمدح به الفاعل؛ إذ لا يكون بسعته. وأما الصادرة عن الطبيعة فدائمة وأكثرية. وقد توجب الطبائع أيضًا أخلاقا متمكنة لا يجب أن تسلب الأعمال الصادرة عن تلك الأخلاق إلى الطبائع إلا بالعرض. ولا يظن أن الطبائع في هذا الموضع يعمل عملا السجايا. وأما الخارجات عن الطبيعة فقد علمتها والمبتكرة من جملتها.
It leads to the second of them, like fear, anger, desire, and other than that. Voluntary actions: some of them follow habit and character; some of them follow an animal impulse, either towards pleasure, which is desire, or towards defense and victory, which is anger; and some of them follow an intellectual impulse or a logical impulse. It resembles that what is meant by intellectual is that which issues from thought towards any purpose, even if the purpose is not rational or not beautiful, and by logical is that which is towards the rational good; and it resembles that what is meant by intellectual is imaginative and by logical is truly intellectual. These divisions are confined to seven: [1] accidental, [b] natural, [t] compulsive, [th] character-based, [j] intellectual, [h] anger-based, [kh] desire-based. It is possible that the division of these causes is according to the differences among people, their states, and their ambitions, like their division into youth and elders, into worshipers and sinners, into rich and poor, into nobles and allies for example. For each [358] group of them has customs and characters from which issue essentially actions specific to them of good and evil. Actions issuing from accident are not regulated nor limited and are not among that for which the doer is praised, since they are not by his effort. As for those issuing from nature, they are constant and mostly. Natures also may cause firmly established characters; it is not necessary that actions issuing from those characters be attributed to natures except accidentally. Let it not be thought that natures in this place act like innate dispositions. As for matters outside nature, you have known them, and the innovative are among them.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies voluntary actions according to their driving forces. It begins by distinguishing between actions that follow habit/character, those that follow animal impulse (towards pleasure [desire] or defense/victory [anger]), and those that follow intellectual or logical impulse. The author clarifies that 'intellectual' may mean imaginative, while 'logical' means truly rational. He then narrows these into seven specific types: accidental, natural, compulsive, character-based, intellectual, anger-based, and desire-based. He explains that these categories can vary per person's age, piety, wealth, or status. Accidental actions are unpraiseworthy and unregulated; natural actions are constant and frequent. Natures can produce firm characters, but actions from character should not be attributed to nature except accidentally. He warns against confusing natures with innate dispositions. Finally, actions outside nature are those known to be innovative.

Key Takeaway
The seven types of voluntary actions (accidental, natural, compulsive, character-based, intellectual, anger-based, desire-based) provide a framework for understanding human motivations in the Islamic tradition.

The Definition of Pleasure (Hadd al-Ladhdha)
Author defines pleasure with genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct the definition of pleasure into its genus and differentia and evaluate whether it is a complete hadd haqiqi.

Core Idea
Pleasure is defined as a movement of the soul toward a natural sensory impression.

In this passage, the author gives a precise definition of pleasure. As students of logic, we must learn to dissect such definitions to see if they meet the criteria of a complete real definition (hadd haqqi). This skill is essential for evaluating definitions in all Islamic sciences, from kalam to fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: اللذة (pleasure). This is what the definition aims to capture.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
Look for the broader category: 'حركة النفس' (movement of the soul). The genus places pleasure in the category of motion.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
Identify what distinguishes pleasure from other movements: 'عن أثر يؤديه الحس بغتة يكون طبيعيا لذلك الحس' (a sudden sensory impression natural to that sense). This is the differentia.
4
Check completeness
Does the definition include all pleasures and exclude all pains? The author also defines pain (المؤلم) as a contrary state, so the definition is exclusive.
The definition of pain is implicit as the opposite.
5
Determine definition type
Is this a real definition (hadd haqiqi) or a mere description (rasm)? Since it provides the genus and differentia, it qualifies as a hadd haqiqi.
A hadd reveals the essence; a rasm describes properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغاية في الأفعال الإرادية قد يكون حصول اللذة، وقد يكون حصول الغلبة، وقد يكون غير هذا؛ وذلك مثل أمثال الأوامر والنواهي في الأمور الشرعية.

ثم لكل لذة علة فبعض اللذات علتها الطبيعة، وبعضها علتها العادة حتى إن كثيرًا مما هو غير لذيذ بالطبيعة يعود لذيذا بالاعتبار. وبالجملة فإن الإقدام على شيء طوعا لارتياد خير ولذة حقيقية أو ظنية. وبالجملة فإن ابتغاء المنفعة هو خاصة للمروى لما أنه مستعمل الحد الأوسط إلى ما يرتاد من الخير عنده؛ وهذا الحد الأوسط هو المنفعة حتى إن الشر بالحقيقة أو بالظن، أو اليسير من الخير قد يطلب بالروية طلب النافع ليتوصل به إلى غايته هي خيرًا أو يرى خيرًا.

فحري بنا أن نتكلم في النافع اللذيذ لكن النافع قد مضى ذكره في باب المشوريات فبقي اللذيذ، واللذة حركة النفس نحو هيئة تكون عن أثر يؤديه الحس بغتة يكون ذلك الأمر طبيعيا لذلك الحس ظاهرا كان ذلك الحس أو باطنا. والشيء الذي يفيد هذه الحركة هو اللذيذ، والذي يفيد هيئة مضادة هو المؤلم؛ فالأمور الطبيعية كلها لذيذة، والمعتادة أيضًا هي لطبيعته. والعادة كأنها طبيعة مكتسبة، والمستكرهة مخافة لهما؛ فالكسل والاستراحة والتواني والترف والنوم من اللذيذات لأنها نحو الأمر الطبيعي. وأضدادها من المؤلمات لما أنها على خلاف الطبيعة. والمشتهيات كلها لذيذة نطقية أو غير نطقية وغير النطقية هي التي يتوجه إليها السوق لا عن فكرة ورأي واللذة [1] قد تكون طبيعية [ب] وقد تكون عقلية. وكل واحدة منهما [١] قد تكون بحسب ما يكون موجودًا بالحقيقة، [٢] وقد تكون بحسب ما يكون بالتخيل. وكذلك تختلف باختلاف الأوقات والأحوال والسجايا. والأسباب ظاهرة فإنها [١] قد تكون بحسب السمع، [٢] وقد تكون بحسب البصر، [٣] وقد تكون بحسب الوهم، [٤] وقد تكون بحسب الخيال. ثمّ الشيء إذا كان لذيذا فلا يلزم أن يكون لذيذا عند الكل بل يمكن أن يكون لذيذا عند البعض، ولا يكون لذيذا عند البعض كما مرَّ من قبل باعتبار المزاج وغيره.
And the goal in voluntary actions may be the attainment of pleasure, or the attainment of victory, or other than that, such as the likes of commands and prohibitions in religious matters. Then for every pleasure there is a cause: some pleasures have nature as their cause, and some have habit as their cause, until much that is not pleasant by nature becomes pleasant by consideration. In summary, undertaking something voluntarily is for seeking good and pleasure, real or apparent. In summary, seeking benefit is a property of deliberation, since it uses the middle term to seek good with it; and this middle term is benefit, so that evil in reality or in opinion, or a small good, may be sought through deliberation seeking benefit to reach its goal, which is good or seen as good. So it is fitting for us to speak of the beneficial and pleasant; but the beneficial has already been mentioned in the chapter on deliberative oratory, so the pleasant remains. Pleasure is a movement of the soul towards a state produced by an effect that the sense conveys suddenly, that thing being natural to that sense, whether that sense is external or internal. The thing that produces this movement is the pleasant, and that which produces a contrary state is the painful. So natural things are all pleasant, and accustomed things are also natural to it. Habit is like an acquired nature, and compulsory things are contrary to both. Thus laziness, rest, idleness, luxury, and sleep are among pleasant things because they are towards the natural thing. Their opposites are among painful things because they are contrary to nature. Desired things are all pleasant, rational or non-rational; non-rational are those to which the impulse is directed without thought or opinion. Pleasure may be natural or rational. Each of them may be according to what exists in reality, or according to what is by imagination. Likewise they differ according to times, states, and innate dispositions. The causes are apparent: they may be according to hearing, sight, estimation, or imagination. Then, if something is pleasant, it does not necessarily follow that it is pleasant for everyone; rather it can be pleasant for some and not pleasant for others, as has been mentioned before with regard to temperament and other factors.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage discusses the goals of voluntary actions, focusing on pleasure. It begins by stating that the end of voluntary action can be pleasure, victory, or something else like religious commands. It then notes that pleasure has causes: nature or habit, and things not naturally pleasant become pleasant through habit. The author emphasizes that all voluntary action seeks perceived good or pleasure. Since the beneficial (al-nāfiʿ) was already covered in the chapter on deliberative oratory (al-mushawwirāt), he now turns to the pleasant (al-ladhīdh). He defines pleasure as a movement of the soul towards a state produced by a sudden sensory impression, natural to that sense (external or internal). The object causing this is pleasant; its opposite is painful. Natural things are inherently pleasant; habits become like second nature. Thus laziness, rest, etc., are pleasant because they align with nature; their opposites are painful. Desires are pleasant, both rational (nāṭiqī) and non-rational. Pleasure is classified as natural or rational, and each can be real or imaginary. It varies by time, state, and disposition. The causes of pleasure are through hearing, sight, estimation, or imagination. Finally, pleasure is subjective: what is pleasant to one may not be to another due to temperament.

Key Terms
اللذة
الألم
اللذيذ
المؤلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The soul
B) Movement of the soul
C) A state
D) A sensory impression

Key Takeaway
The definition of pleasure is a real definition (hadd haqiqi) that uses 'movement of the soul' as genus and 'natural sensory impression' as differentia.

Classification of Pleasure: Natural, Rational, Real, and Imaginary
Author classifies pleasure into natural and rational, real and imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify pleasures according to their causes (natural vs rational) and their modality (real vs imaginary) as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Pleasure varies by cause, perception, and individual temperament.

After defining pleasure, the author categorizes it based on its cause (nature or reason) and its mode (real or imagined). This classification is crucial for understanding the psychology of voluntary actions and for analyzing arguments in rhetoric that appeal to pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The passage divides pleasure into two main types: natural (طبيعية) and rational (عقلية). Note that 'rational' here means stemming from reasoning, not just intellectual.
2
Recognize the sub-division
Each of these can be real (بحسب ما يكون موجودًا بالحقيقة) or imaginary (بحسب ما يكون بالتخيل). Real pleasure corresponds to an actual sensory input; imaginary pleasure arises from imagination.
3
List the causes
The causes of pleasure are four: hearing (سمع), sight (بصر), estimation (وهم), and imagination (خيال). Estimation is a faculty that perceives meanings, like a sheep perceiving the enemy in a wolf.
These correspond to external and internal senses.
4
Understand variation
Pleasure is not universal; it differs by time, state, and innate disposition (سجايا). What is pleasant to one may be painful to another, based on temperament.
This is a key philosophical point that the author emphasizes.

Evaluating Witness Testimony in Rhetoric
Author discusses the use of witness testimony in rhetorical argument, how the judge evaluates signs of acceptance or rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the signs (amarat) that judges use to evaluate witness testimony in Islamic legal rhetoric.

Core Idea
Witness testimony is judged by external signs of credibility, not the statement alone.

In Islamic legal and rhetorical tradition, witness testimony (shahada) is a powerful but fallible argument. A judge must assess its credibility by examining external signs (amarat) that suggest acceptance or rejection. This lesson explores the specific signs mentioned in our passage and their application in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being discussed
Locate the term 'الشهادة' (shahada) in the Arabic passage. Note that it is presented as a type of rhetorical argument that may be true or false.
Shahada is not just a statement but carries evidentiary weight in Islamic law.
2
Recognize the judge's task
The passage states the judge must consider 'أمارات القبول والرد' (signs of acceptance and rejection). Understand that the judge's role is to apply these signs to determine the testimony's reliability.
3
Extract the specific signs
The text gives one clear example: a witness is suspect if he is a friend of the one he testifies for or an enemy of the one he testifies against. Identify these as the primary signs mentioned.
Other signs may exist in fiqh, but this passage focuses on personal relationship.
4
Apply the signs to a scenario
Imagine a court case where a witness testifies for his close friend. According to the passage, the judge should suspect the testimony and consider rejecting it unless corroborated.
This aligns with the principle of 'tazkiya' (witness vetting) in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشهادة فالشهادة قد تكون على وجه الصواب، وقد لا تكون فيحتاج الحاكم إلى أن ينظر فيما يكون من أمارات القبول وفيما يكون من أمارات الرد فإن من الشهود من كان متهما في الشهادة بأن كان صديقا للمشهود له أو عدوا للمشهود عليه.
As for witnessing, the testimony may be correct, or it may not be, so the judge needs to consider what are signs of acceptance and what are signs of rejection. For among witnesses is one who is suspect in testimony because he is a friend of the one for whom testimony is given or an enemy of the one against whom testimony is given.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage addresses the second type of rhetorical argument: witness testimony (shahada). The author states that testimony can be either correct (on the face of truth) or incorrect. The judge must therefore examine signs (amarat) that indicate whether the testimony should be accepted or rejected. For example, a witness may be suspect if he is a friend of the party he testifies for or an enemy of the opposite party. This aligns with the principles of Islamic legal procedure (qada) where the credibility of witnesses is assessed based on their relationship to the parties.

Witness testimony in rhetoric is not automatically accepted; judges must use signs like personal relationships to assess credibility.

Covenants as Rhetorical Arguments and the Role of the Sage
Covenants in rhetoric and the sage's evaluative role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a covenant functions as a rhetorical argument and evaluate its validity according to reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Covenants are human agreements; the sage judges them against divine law.

This passage introduces the third type of rhetorical argument: covenants ('uhud). These are agreements made by groups that can be used by disputants to support their claims. However, the author warns that such covenants may contradict divine law and that the sage, as vicegerent of reason and revelation, must scrutinize them. Let's explore the dynamics and how to properly evaluate such arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputant's use of the covenant
Notice that when a covenant agrees with a disputant's claim, he uses it to adorn his own position and belittle his opponent. Recognize that this is a rhetorical strategy, not a logical proof.
The passage says 'when they agree with the claim... give him room to adorn his own affair and magnify it'.
2
Understand the nature of a covenant
A covenant is defined as 'a law that a group of the community legislates among themselves.' Recognize that it is human-made and may fall outside the divine law.
The author explicitly states it may be outside the requirement of the shari'a.
3
Analyze the disputant's possible objection
If the covenant opposes his desire, he may reject it by saying 'Every covenant not in the Book is innovation, and every innovation is misguidance.' This is a rhetorical move that appeals to an established principle.
The disputant uses the hadith about bid'ah to invalidate the covenant.
4
Define the sage's role and method
The sage is the vicegerent (khalifa) of both reason and revelation. His duty is to investigate the conditions of the imposed covenants and judge according to either reason or revelation.
The phrase 'fitted that he explore' indicates active scrutiny, not blind acceptance.
5
Classify the argument type
The passage concludes that these are 'rhetorical syllogisms' (qiyasat khatabiyya). Note they are persuasive but not demonstrative; they lack the certainty of qiyas burhani.
This classification contextualizes covenants within Aristotelian/Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العهود فإنها إذا وافقت دعوى المشاجر أعطته مجالا في تزيين أمر نفسه وتعظيمه؛ إذ قد حافظ على المشاق، وفي تحقير أمر صاحبه؛ إذ حقر به. وبالجملة فالعهد شريعة شرعها طائفة من الأمة فيما بينهم فربَّما كان ذلك خارجًا عن موجب الشريعة. فالذي يجده مخالفا لمراده فله أن يقول كل عهد ليس في الكتاب فهو بدعة، وكل بدعة ضلالة؛ وقد كفانا عقد الشرع عقد العهد الذي هو مخادعة. ثم يقول للحكيم الحكيم خليفة العقل والشرع، وفي ذمة عهده الاستكشاف. فبالحري أن يستكشف أحوال العهود المفروضة، ويحكم على وفق العقل أو الشرع، وهذا كلها هي القياسات الخطابية.
As for covenants, when they agree with the claim of the disputant, they give him room to adorn his own affair and magnify it, since he has observed the hardships, and to belittle the affair of his companion, since he belittled him by it. In summary, a covenant is a law that a group of the community legislates among themselves, and it may be outside the requirement of the divine law. So whoever finds it opposing his desire may say: Every covenant not in the Book is innovation, and every innovation is misguidance; the contract of the divine law suffices us over the contract of the covenant which is deception. Then he says to the sage: The sage is the vicegerent of reason and revelation, and on his covenant is exploration. So it is fitting that he explore the conditions of the imposed covenants, and judge according to reason or revelation. And all these are the rhetorical syllogisms.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of rhetorical argument: covenants ('uhud). Covenants are agreements made by a group of people; they may serve the disputant's claim if they align with it. However, these covenants can contradict divine law (shari'a). The author notes that a disputant may reject a covenant by labeling it an innovation (bid'a) if it is not found in scripture. The sage (hakim) is described as the vicegerent (khalifa) of both reason ('aql) and revelation (shar'). He must investigate the covenants and rule according to either reason or revelation. The passage concludes by noting that these are all rhetorical syllogisms (qiyasat khatabiyya), meaning they are persuasive but not demonstrative.

Key Terms
العهد
الحكيم
القياسات الخطابية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a binding law.
B) It is an innovation and misguidance.
C) It must be followed as it is a covenant.
D) It is a valid rhetorical argument.

Key Takeaway
Covenants are human agreements that can be used rhetorically, but they must be scrutinized by the sage using reason and revelation to ensure they align with divine law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider a covenant made by a group of scholars stating that it is permissible to combine prayers without a valid excuse. Using the method from the passage, construct a rhetorical syllogism that a disputant might use to support this, and then show how a sage would critique it using reason and revelation (e.g., citing the hadith on combining prayers only for travel or rain).

Rhetorical Arguments: Investigation and Oaths
Discussion of investigation and oaths as rhetorical arguments requiring scrutiny.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the reliability of an oath, warning, or reward as a rhetorical argument by examining the speaker's piety or recklessness, following the author's principle of not relying on outward appearance.

Core Idea
Outward oaths can deceive; credibility requires investigating the speaker's piety or recklessness.

In this passage, the author expands on two types of rhetorical arguments: investigation through warnings, rewards, and punishments (which are considered akin to testimonies), and oaths. The key lesson is that these arguments are not accepted at face value. If they align with the claim, they may be used; otherwise, one must wait. Oaths, in particular, may be based on genuine piety or reckless falsehood. The author warns against being swayed by outward sayings, deeds, or forms, and insists on deeper scrutiny—similar to evaluating witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of investigation
Notice the author lists warnings (إنذار), excuses, encouragement, intimidation, punishment, and reward as methods of investigating conditions. They are categorized as 'testimonies' (شهادات).
These are rhetorical tools that appeal to hope or fear, not logical proofs.
2
Apply the acceptance condition
The author says: if the investigation agrees with the claim, retain it as evidence; otherwise, suspend judgment until clarity emerges. This shows that these tools are conditional, not absolute.
This mirrors the cautious approach in Islamic jurisprudence toward circumstantial evidence.
3
Analyze the nature of oaths
Oaths may be based on تقوى (piety/righteousness) or on تهور (recklessness). Their reliability is not intrinsic to the oath itself but depends on the character and context of the swearer.
The author explicitly says: 'فلا يكون مقولا عليه من حيث هو هو' – it cannot be said about it as it is in itself.
4
Apply the principle of critical scrutiny
The conclusion: do not rely on the outward appearance of sayings, deeds, or forms. One must examine the circumstances of the swearer and other evidence to uncover truth or falsehood.
This aligns with the broader Islamic principle of avoiding hasty judgment and seeking verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التفحص عن الأحوال بالإنذار والإعذار، وبالترغيب والترهيب، وبالعقاب والثواب فهي أيضًا من جنس الشهادات. فإن كان موافقا للدعوى احتفظ به حجة واعتمد عليه وإلا فلا بل يتوقف إلى أن يظهر. وأما اليمين والقسم فكل ذلك قد يكون عن تقوى، وقد لا يكون بل يكون على سبيل تهور فلا يكون مقولا عليه من حيث هو هو بل من اللوازم أن ينظر في أحوال الحالف وغيره إلى أن يظهره صدقه أو كذبه كما مر في الشهود. فإن من الأيمان ما يكون ترويجا للكذب كما أن من الصلاح ما يكون ترويجا للزهد الكاذب فلا يعتمد إذن على ظاهر الأقوال والأفعال والأشكال؛ وهذا ظاهر.
As for investigating conditions by warning and excusing, by encouragement and intimidation, by punishment and reward, these are also of the kind of testimonies. If it agrees with the claim, he retains it as evidence and relies on it; otherwise, he suspends until it becomes clear. As for the oath and the swearing, all that may be based on piety, or it may not be but rather be based on recklessness, so it is not said about it as it is in itself, but from the implications, one must examine the conditions of the swearer and others until his truthfulness or falsehood becomes apparent, as was done in the case of witnesses. For among oaths are those that promote falsehood, just as among righteousness are those that promote false asceticism. So do not rely on the outward of sayings, deeds, and forms; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage discusses the remaining types of rhetorical arguments: investigation (tafahhus) through warnings, rewards, punishments (which are akin to testimonies), and oaths. The author states that if these agree with the claim, they can be used as evidence; otherwise, one must wait. Regarding oaths, they may be truthful or reckless; their credibility cannot be judged by outward appearance alone. The author warns against judging by outward signs, as oaths can be used to promote falsehood just as piety can be used to promote false asceticism. The conclusion is that one must examine the circumstances of the swearer and other factors to determine truthfulness, similar to the evaluation of witnesses. This reinforces the rhetorical nature of these arguments: they are persuasive but require critical scrutiny.

Key Terms
اليمين
التفحص
الترغيب والترهيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Outward appearance is sufficient to determine truthfulness.

C) Oaths are always reliable if sworn with sincerity.
D) Oaths are never to be trusted.

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical arguments like oaths and investigations require scrutiny of the speaker's character and circumstances, not mere outward appearance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Evaluating Testimony and Oaths as Rhetorical Premises
Author discusses evaluating testimony and oaths in rhetorical argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the indicators of acceptance and rejection for testimony and oaths in rhetorical argumentation.

Core Idea
Testimony and oaths are not self-validating; their reliability depends on circumstantial examination.

In rhetorical syllogisms, premises often come from testimony (shahada) and oaths (yamin). But not all testimonies or oaths are equally convincing. The author of this passage explains that a judge must look beyond the surface—examining factors like friendship, enmity, piety, or recklessness—to decide whether to accept them. This lesson will guide you through the logical criteria for evaluating such premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Locate where the passage discusses testimony and oaths. Note that both can be used as premises in rhetorical syllogisms, but their acceptance depends on external signs.
The passage mentions 'shahada' and 'yamin' explicitly.
2
Extract criteria for testimony
The author says the judge must look at 'amarat al-qabul' (indicators of acceptance) and 'amarat al-radd' (indicators of rejection). A key indicator of rejection is if the witness is a friend of the plaintiff or an enemy of the defendant.
Friendship and enmity are examples of bias that undermine reliability.
3
Apply similar logic to oaths
Oaths may arise from piety (taqwa) or recklessness (tahawwur). The author warns that oaths can promote falsehood just as piety can promote false asceticism. So, one must examine the swearer's circumstances.
Outward form alone is insufficient; inward state matters.
4
Summarize the principle
The passage concludes: 'One should not rely on the apparent words, deeds, and forms.' This means that rhetorical premises from testimony/oaths require critical evaluation, not blind acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشهادة فالشهادة قد تكون على وجه الصواب، وقد لا تكون فيحتاج الحاكم إلى أن ينظر فيما يكون من أمارات القبول وفيما يكون من أمارات الرد فإن من الشهود من كان متهما في الشهادة بأن كان صديقا للمشهود له أو عدوا للمشهود عليه. وأما العهود فإنها إذا وافقت دعوى المشاجر أعطته مجالا في تزيين أمر نفسه وتعظيمه؛ إذ قد حافظ على المشاق، وفي تحقير أمر صاحبه؛ إذ حقر به. وبالجملة فالعهد شريعة شرعها طائفة من الأمة فيما بينهم فربَّما كان ذلك خارجًا عن موجب الشريعة. فالذي يجده مخالفا لمراده فله أن يقول كل عهد ليس في الكتاب فهو بدعة، وكل بدعة ضلالة؛ وقد كفانا عقد الشرع عقد العهد الذي هو مخادعة. ثم يقول للحكيم الحكيم خليفة العقل والشرع، وفي ذمة عهده الاستكشاف. فبالحري أن يستكشف أحوال العهود المفروضة، ويحكم على وفق العقل أو الشرع، وهذا كلها هي القياسات الخطابية.
وأما التفحص عن الأحوال بالإنذار والإعذار، وبالترغيب والترهيب، وبالعقاب والثواب فهي أيضًا من جنس الشهادات. فإن كان موافقا للدعوى احتفظ به حجة واعتمد عليه وإلا فلا بل يتوقف إلى أن يظهر. وأما اليمين والقسم فكل ذلك قد يكون عن تقوى، وقد لا يكون بل يكون على سبيل تهور فلا يكون مقولا عليه من حيث هو هو بل من اللوازم أن ينظر في أحوال الحالف وغيره إلى أن يظهره صدقه أو كذبه كما مر في الشهود. فإن من الأيمان ما يكون ترويجا للكذب كما أن من الصلاح ما يكون ترويجا للزهد الكاذب فلا يعتمد إذن على ظاهر الأقوال والأفعال والأشكال؛ وهذا ظاهر.
As for testimony, testimony may be correct or not, so the judge needs to examine what are indicators of acceptance and what are indicators of rejection, for among witnesses some are suspect in testimony, being a friend of the one for whom testimony is given or an enemy of the one against whom it is given. As for covenants, if they agree with the claim of the litigant, they give him scope to embellish his own matter and magnify it, since he has adhered to the difficult matters, and to belittle the matter of his companion, since he belittled by it. In summary, the covenant is a law legislated by a group of the community among themselves, and perhaps that is outside the requirement of the divine law. So whoever finds it contrary to his intent may say: Every covenant not in the Book is an innovation, and every innovation is misguidance; and the contract of the law has sufficed us from the contract of the covenant which is deceit. Then he says to the sage: The sage is the deputy of reason and law, and under the guarantee of his covenant is exploration. So it is fitting that he explores the conditions of prescribed covenants and judges according to reason or law, and all these are the rhetorical syllogisms.
As for examining states by warning and excusing, and by encouragement and intimidation, and by punishment and reward, these also are of the same kind as testimonies. If it agrees with the claim, he retains it as proof and relies on it; otherwise, no, but he waits until it appears. As for the oath and swearing, all that may be from piety, or may be from recklessness, so it is not accepted on its own terms, but it is necessary to examine the states of the one swearing and others until his truth or falsehood appears, as has passed regarding witnesses. For among oaths are those that promote falsehood, just as among righteousness are those that promote false piety. So one should not rely on the apparent words, deeds, and forms; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage discusses the use of testimony (shahada) and oaths (yamin) in rhetorical syllogisms (qiyasat khatabiyya). The author explains that testimony can be true or false, so a judge must examine indicators of acceptance (amarat al-qabul) and rejection (amarat al-radd). Witnesses may be suspect if they are friends of the plaintiff or enemies of the defendant. Similarly, covenants (uhud) can be used by litigants to embellish their case, but they may be outside the shari'a. The author then mentions that one can argue that any covenant not in the Book is an innovation (bid'a) and thus misguidance (dalala). The sage (hakim) is the deputy of reason and law, so he should investigate covenants and judge accordingly. All these are rhetorical syllogisms. Also, emotional appeals like warnings, rewards, etc., are akin to testimonies. Oaths may be based on piety or recklessness, so their apparent form is not sufficient; one must investigate the circumstances. The passage concludes that one should not rely on outward appearances. This is part of the broader art of rhetoric, where premises are not certain but persuasive.

Key Terms

يمين
أمارات القبول
أمارات الرد
قياسات خطابية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Examine indicators of rejection because of potential bias.
D) Ignore the friendship since it is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Testimony and oaths require circumstantial evaluation; their outward form is not sufficient for acceptance.

Covenants and the Sage's Role in Rhetorical Argumentation
Author discusses covenants as rhetorical premises and the sage's role in evaluating them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how covenants ('uhud) function as rhetorical premises and how the sage (hakim) evaluates them according to reason and shari'a.

Core Idea
Covenants outside shari'a may be bid'a; the sage uses reason and law to assess them.

In the previous lesson, we saw that testimony and oaths require scrutiny. Here, the author turns to covenants ('uhud)—agreements made within a community that may or may not align with divine law. He explains that such covenants can be used by litigants to embellish their cases, but they may be innovations (bid'a) outside the shari'a. The sage (hakim), as deputy of reason and law, must explore their conditions and judge accordingly. This lesson will help you understand the logic behind evaluating such premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise type: covenant
The passage discusses 'ahd' (covenant) as a law legislated by a group among themselves, potentially outside the shari'a. It can be used rhetorically to support a claim.
Covenants are considered rhetorical premises (muqaddimat khatabiyya) because they are persuasive but not certain.
2
Understand the potential conflict with shari'a
The author warns that a covenant may be outside 'mu'jib al-shari'a' (the requirement of divine law). Then he presents a possible objection: 'Every covenant not in the Book is an innovation (bid'a), and every innovation is misguidance (dalala).'
This is a common argument in usul al-fiqh against practices not rooted in revelation.
3
Analyze the role of the sage
The sage (hakim) is described as the deputy (khalifa) of reason and law. He must explore (istikshaf) the conditions of prescribed covenants and judge according to 'aql or shari'a.
The sage uses rational and legal criteria to evaluate the covenant's validity.
4
Recognize the rhetorical nature
The passage concludes: 'All these are the rhetorical syllogisms.' This means the evaluation process itself—weighing the covenant against reason and law—constitutes a rhetorical argument.

وأما التفحص عن الأحوال بالإنذار والإعذار، وبالترغيب والترهيب، وبالعقاب والثواب فهي أيضًا من جنس الشهادات. فإن كان موافقا للدعوى احتفظ به حجة واعتمد عليه وإلا فلا بل يتوقف إلى أن يظهر. وأما اليمين والقسم فكل ذلك قد يكون عن تقوى، وقد لا يكون بل يكون على سبيل تهور فلا يكون مقولا عليه من حيث هو هو بل من اللوازم أن ينظر في أحوال الحالف وغيره إلى أن يظهره صدقه أو كذبه كما مر في الشهود. فإن من الأيمان ما يكون ترويجا للكذب كما أن من الصلاح ما يكون ترويجا للزهد الكاذب فلا يعتمد إذن على ظاهر الأقوال والأفعال والأشكال؛ وهذا ظاهر.
As for testimony, testimony may be correct or not, so the judge needs to examine what are indicators of acceptance and what are indicators of rejection, for among witnesses some are suspect in testimony, being a friend of the one for whom testimony is given or an enemy of the one against whom it is given. As for covenants, if they agree with the claim of the litigant, they give him scope to embellish his own matter and magnify it, since he has adhered to the difficult matters, and to belittle the matter of his companion, since he belittled by it. In summary, the covenant is a law legislated by a group of the community among themselves, and perhaps that is outside the requirement of the divine law. So whoever finds it contrary to his intent may say: Every covenant not in the Book is an innovation, and every innovation is misguidance; and the contract of the law has sufficed us from the contract of the covenant which is deceit. Then he says to the sage: The sage is the deputy of reason and law, and under the guarantee of his covenant is exploration. So it is fitting that he explores the conditions of prescribed covenants and judges according to reason or law, and all these are the rhetorical syllogisms.
As for examining states by warning and excusing, and by encouragement and intimidation, and by punishment and reward, these also are of the same kind as testimonies. If it agrees with the claim, he retains it as proof and relies on it; otherwise, no, but he waits until it appears. As for the oath and swearing, all that may be from piety, or may be from recklessness, so it is not accepted on its own terms, but it is necessary to examine the states of the one swearing and others until his truth or falsehood appears, as has passed regarding witnesses. For among oaths are those that promote falsehood, just as among righteousness are those that promote false piety. So one should not rely on the apparent words, deeds, and forms; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage discusses the use of testimony (shahada) and oaths (yamin) in rhetorical syllogisms (qiyasat khatabiyya). The author explains that testimony can be true or false, so a judge must examine indicators of acceptance (amarat al-qabul) and rejection (amarat al-radd). Witnesses may be suspect if they are friends of the plaintiff or enemies of the defendant. Similarly, covenants (uhud) can be used by litigants to embellish their case, but they may be outside the shari'a. The author then mentions that one can argue that any covenant not in the Book is an innovation (bid'a) and thus misguidance (dalala). The sage (hakim) is the deputy of reason and law, so he should investigate covenants and judge accordingly. All these are rhetorical syllogisms. Also, emotional appeals like warnings, rewards, etc., are akin to testimonies. Oaths may be based on piety or recklessness, so their apparent form is not sufficient; one must investigate the circumstances. The passage concludes that one should not rely on outward appearances. This is part of the broader art of rhetoric, where premises are not certain but persuasive.

بدعة
ضلالة
حكيم
قياسات خطابية

Defining Universal Friendship and Its Rhetorical Context
Author defines universal friendship and discusses its role in rhetoric.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia of 'universal friendship' from the definition given and assess its completeness as a hadd.

Core Idea
True friendship is defined by willing good for the other’s sake, not one’s own.

This passage from a classical work on rhetoric and logic explores how an orator must understand a judge's personal inclinations to persuade effectively. It then provides a careful definition of 'universal friendship' (al-sadaqa al-kulliyya) that serves as a tool for analyzing human relationships in judicial and rhetorical contexts. By dissecting this definition, you will learn how classical scholars constructed precise definitions using the method of genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Look for the phrase 'الصداقة الكلية' (universal friendship) in the Arabic. The author says it is 'حالة للإنسان من حيث يهوي الخير لإنسان آخر لأجل ذلك الآخر' – a state of a person insofar as he wills good for another for that other's sake.
The definiendum is 'صداقة كلية'.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The author places universal friendship under the genus 'state' (حالة) or 'disposition' (ملكة). Specifically, he says it is a 'حالة' and then a 'ملكة داعية' – a disposition that calls to action.
Genus: a psychological state/disposition.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is the specific characteristic: willing good for another human for the sake of that other, not for oneself ('لأجل ذلك الآخر لا لأجل نفسه'). This distinguishes true friendship from self-interested relationships.
Differentia: willing good for the other’s sake.
4
Assess the definition as a hadd
Check if the definition includes all instances of universal friendship (e.g., friends who love and are loved, share joy/sorrow) and excludes non-instances (e.g., utilitarian relationships). The author adds further characteristics (لoving and being loved, participating in joy/sorrow) which may be necessary or accidental.
The definition seems complete but could be considered a hadd naqis if the additional traits are not implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يتدرج به القضاة ومن عنده من السامعين
ومن جملة المخاطبات ما يتدرّج به القضاة ومن عنده من السامعين؛ وذلك ما قد يختلف بحسب مراتب الحكام في أذهانهم، آرائهم، أو أضداد ذلك. وخصوصا في المشوريات. وأما الخصومات فيشبه أن يكون الاعتماد فيها على السنن المحفوظة أكثر منه على القرائح المميزة. فإذا كان الخطيب خبيرا بحال الحاكم وحال خصمه انتفع بذلك فإن الحكام لا يتساوى ميلهم إلى من يحبونه وإلى من يسبونه. فلا يبعد أن يصدر منه ما لا يكون موافقا لحاله بعرض ما؛ فالامتحان في أصالة اللب وذكاء الخليقة وغير ذلك من الفضائل. وفي أضدادها كذلك نافع منفعة عظيمة في الخطابيات فإنه يحصل منه الوقوف على الألفة والصداقة والحقد والعداوة. فإن من الأعداء من تكون سيرته سيرة الأصدقاء فيجب الاحتراز عن صنيعه على سبيل؛ وهذا هو الحرم كما قيل الحرم سوء الظن.
ثم الصداقة والعداوة والخوف والأمن والشجاعة والجبن كلها على أنواع مختلفة. والصداقة الكلية الشاملة لكل نوع منها هي حالة للإنسان من حيث يهوي الخير لإنسان آخر لأجل ذلك الآخر لا لأجل نفسه؛ فتكون له ملكة داعية إلى فعل الخير لذلك وبه. والصديق هو الذي يحِبُّ ويحبُّ معا ويشارك في السراء والضراء لأجل صديقه لا لأجل نفسه. وإنما يظهر صدق الصداقة عند الارتباح بما يسرُّ الصديق والاغتمام بما يسيوؤه، والمحبُّ هو المحسن في الحقيقة. ومحب المحب، وعدو العدو. والذي ببغض العدو أو يبغضه العدو، والذي هو سليم القلب من الناس فذلك من الأصدقاء لما أن الناس في الأمن عن عوائلهم. وكذلك الذي له عقل وفضل فإن ذلك مما يكفّه عن
Chapter: On what judges and those present with them gradually employ.
Among the types of addresses are those that judges and those present with them gradually employ; and that varies according to the ranks of the rulers in their minds, opinions, or their opposites, especially in matters of consultation. As for disputes, it seems that reliance in them is on preserved traditions more than on discerning natural gifts. So if the orator is knowledgeable about the state of the judge and the state of his opponent, he benefits from that, for judges are not equal in their inclination towards those they love and those they revile. So it is not unlikely that something not in accordance with his state may issue from him by some means. So examination in the genuineness of intellect, the sharpness of nature, and other virtues, and their opposites, is greatly beneficial in oratory, for by it one attains familiarity with intimacy, friendship, rancor, and enmity. For among enemies are those whose conduct is the conduct of friends, so one must beware of his action on account of...; and this is caution, as it is said: caution is suspicion.
Then friendship, enmity, fear, security, courage, cowardice – all are of different types. Universal friendship, encompassing every type, is a state of a person insofar as he wills good for another human for the sake of that other, not for himself; so he has a disposition calling him to do good for that one and by him. The friend is one who loves and is loved together, and participates in joy and sorrow for the sake of his friend, not for himself. And the sincerity of friendship appears only in being delighted by what pleases the friend and being grieved by what harms him. And the lover is the benefactor in reality. And the lover of the lover, and the enemy of the enemy. And he who hates the enemy or is hated by the enemy, and he who is sound of heart among people, that one is among the friends, since people are secure from their families. And likewise he who has intellect and virtue, for that restrains him from...
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a chapter (fasl) on how judges and listeners gradually adapt their reasoning. It explains that the orator's (khatib) knowledge of the judge's inclinations is beneficial because judges are not impartial; they favor those they love and dislike those they revile. Examination of intellectual qualities and virtues is important in oratory. Then the author defines universal friendship (al-sadaqa al-kulliyya) as a state of willing good for another for that other's sake, not for oneself. This is a disposition (malaka) to do good. A true friend (sadiq) loves and is loved, shares in happiness and sorrow. The sincerity of friendship is shown by delight at the friend's joy and grief at his harm. The lover is the true benefactor. The author also describes the friend as the lover of the lover and the enemy of the enemy. And he who is sound of heart (salim al-qalb) and has intellect and virtue is among the friends. This definition of friendship is used in the context of rhetoric to understand the dispositions of judges and opponents.

Key Terms
صداقة كلية
خطيب
حاكم
ملكة
حالة

Key Takeaway
A precise definition in the Islamic logical tradition requires identifying the genus (broader category) and the differentia (specific characteristic) that together distinguish the definiendum from all else.

How Speakers Adapt to Judges in Rhetoric
Author explains how orators adjust speech based on judges' dispositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key factors an orator (khatib) must consider when adapting speech to a judge, and distinguish between reliance on tradition vs. natural talent in different rhetorical contexts.

Core Idea
Rhetoric in Islamic logic is about adapting speech to the judge's character, not just persuasion.

In this passage, the author discusses a specific type of discourse: how orators adjust their speech based on the dispositions of judges and listeners. This is particularly important in consultative settings (mushawarat) and disputes (khusumat). The orator must understand the judge's inclinations, the opponent, and whether to rely on traditions or innate talent. The passage also warns against deceptive enemies and emphasizes caution (hazm). Let's explore this rhetorical technique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of discourses mentioned
The passage distinguishes between consultative matters (mushawarat) and disputes (khusumat). In consultative matters, the speech varies according to the judges' ranks, opinions, or opposites. In disputes, reliance is more on preserved traditions.
The passage says 'especially in deliberations' and 'as for disputes', so these are the two contexts.
2
Understand the roles of tradition vs. natural talent
For disputes, the author states that reliance is on preserved traditions (sunan mahfuzah) more than on natural talents (qaraih mumayyizah). This implies that for disputes, the orator should study established traditions rather than rely on quick intuition.
Natural talent may be more relevant in other contexts.
3
Recognize the importance of knowing the judge and opponent
The orator benefits from expertise about the judge's state and the opponent because judges are not equally inclined to those they love and those they revile. The orator can adjust his speech accordingly by understanding the judge's character and the opponent's position.
This is not manipulation but effective communication based on known character.
4
Analyze the principle of caution (hazm)
The passage warns that some enemies behave like friends, so one must be cautious. It quotes the maxim 'caution is suspicion' (al-hazm su' al-zann). The orator must be vigilant against deceptive appearances.
This ties into ethical considerations in rhetoric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ فيما يتدرج به القضاة ومن عنده من السامعين ]
المنطق الكبيرة
ومن جملة المخاطبات ما يتدرّج به القضاة ومن عنده من السامعين؛ وذلك ما قد يختلف بحسب مراتب الحكام في أذهانهم، آرائهم، أو أضداد ذلك. وخصوصا في المشوريات. وأما الخصومات فيشبه أن يكون الاعتماد فيها على السنن المحفوظة أكثر منه على القرائح المميزة. فإذا كان الخطيب خبيرا بحال الحاكم وحال خصمه انتفع بذلك فإن الحكام لا يتساوى ميلهم إلى من يحبونه وإلى من يسبونه. فلا يبعد أن يصدر منه ما لا يكون موافقا لحاله بعرض ما؛ فالامتحان في أصالة اللب وذكاء الخليقة وغير ذلك من الفضائل. وفي أضدادها كذلك نافع منفعة عظيمة في الخطابيات فإنه يحصل منه الوقوف على الألفة والصداقة والحقد والعداوة. فإن من الأعداء من تكون سيرته سيرة الأصدقاء فيجب الاحتراز عن صنيعه على سبيل؛ وهذا هو الحرم كما قيل الحرم سوء الظن.
Section: [On what the judges and those with them from the listeners adapt by]. The great logic. Among the types of discourses is what the judges and those with them from the listeners adapt by; that is what may vary according to the ranks of the rulers in their minds, their opinions, or their opposites. Especially in deliberations. As for disputes, it seems that reliance in them is on preserved traditions more than on distinct natural talents. So if the orator is expert in the state of the ruler and the state of his opponent, he benefits from that, for the rulers are not equal in inclination towards those they love and those they revile. So it is not unlikely that something may issue from him that is not in accordance with his state, by way of something presented; thus examination in soundness of core, sharpness of nature, and other virtues—and likewise their opposites—is of great benefit in rhetorical matters, for from it one attains knowledge of familiarity, friendship, rancor, and enmity. Indeed, among enemies are those whose conduct is the conduct of friends, so one must guard against their action as a precaution; and this is caution, as it is said: caution is suspicion.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage defines a rhetorical technique for adapting discourse to judges and listeners. The author begins by stating that among the types of discourses (al-mukhatabat) is that which judges and listeners adapt to, varying according to the ranks of the rulers in their minds, opinions, or opposites, especially in consultative matters. In disputes, reliance is more on preserved traditions (sunan mahfuzah) than on innate talents (qaraih mumayyizah). The orator who knows the state of the ruler and his opponent benefits because rulers are not equally inclined to those they love and those they revile. It is not unlikely that the ruler may issue a judgment not in accordance with his state due to some presented matter. Therefore, examining the soundness of mind, sharpness of nature, and other virtues—and their opposites—is greatly beneficial in rhetoric, as it reveals familiarity, friendship, rancor, and enmity. Some enemies behave like friends, so one must be cautious; as it is said, caution is suspicion.

Rhetorical Premises: Closeness to the Mind vs. Distance in Dialectic
Author defines rhetorical premises and distinguishes them from dialectical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rhetorical premises from dialectical premises based on their proximity to the desired conclusion and the listener's mind.

Core Idea
Rhetorical premises must be close to the mind; distant premises weaken persuasion.

This passage clarifies that rhetoric uses premises that are neither too obvious nor too remote, but that immediately produce opinion when mentioned. Unlike dialectic, which may employ distant premises, rhetoric requires a balance to achieve its aim of persuasion. The author contrasts the two types to show that rhetorical premises are tailored to the audience's immediate understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual purpose of rhetoric
Notice that rhetoric is used for praise, blame, and repelling blame. This sets the context for the kind of premises it needs—those that can sway opinion quickly.
2
Extract the contrast with dialectical premises
The passage states that dialectical premises may be far from the desired conclusion (بعيدة عن المطلوب), but rhetoric cannot use such distant premises. This is a key distinguishing feature.
Distant premises would overwhelm the listener in rhetoric.
3
Understand the balance condition for rhetorical premises
Rhetorical premises are neither fully present in minds (like 'the sun is shining') nor completely absent; they strike a middle ground. When mentioned, they immediately trigger an opinion.
This ensures the listener can relate to them without extended reasoning.
4
Apply the example provided
The saying 'How bad is greed!' illustrates a rhetorical premise: it is simple, instantly evokes a negative opinion, and suits the nature of common people.
Such examples are effective in sermons and public speeches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمطلوب في الخطابة والخطابة كما تكون للمدح والذم فكذلك لدفع الذم؛ والمطلوب في الخطابة غير المطلوب في الجدل لما كانت المقدّمات الخطابية غير المقدّمات الجدلية. ولا يبعد أن تكون المقدّمات الجدلية هي البعيدة عن المطلوب بخلاف الخطابة فإنه لا يصح أن تستعمل فيها المقدمات البعيدة حدا؛ ولا الشرط فيها أن تستعمل المشهورات الحقيقية فقط، ولا المقدمات البعيدة عن ظنون الجمهور بل إنها تستعمل فيها ما لا يكون حاضرا في الأذهان بالفعل مثل كون الشمس مشرقة، ولا غائبًا عنها كذلك وإلا لكانت الأذهان متقاعدة عن الحكم فيها بوجه بل هي التي عند ما تذكر يتقدم فيها ظن أو يقدح منها ذلك إذا ذكرت مفردة أو مع قرينة كما مر . وهذا مثل قولهم بئس الشيء الطمع؛ وما يكون مناسبًا بطباع العامة فهو مثل هذا الكلام
For the aim in rhetoric: just as rhetoric is used for praise and blame, so too for repelling blame; and the aim in rhetoric is different from the aim in dialectic because rhetorical premises are different from dialectical premises. It is not unlikely that dialectical premises are far from the aim, unlike rhetoric, for it is not valid to use distant premises in rhetoric at all; nor is the condition that only true accepted opinions be used, nor premises far from the opinions of the public; rather, it uses what is not actually present in minds, such as the sun being shining, nor absent from them likewise, otherwise minds would be unable to judge them at all; but they are such that when mentioned, an opinion precedes them or arises from them when mentioned alone or with a context as has passed. And this is like their saying: 'How bad is greed!'; and what is appropriate to the nature of the common people is like this speech.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of rhetorical premises. The author begins by stating that rhetoric serves both praise/blame and repelling blame. The aim in rhetoric differs from dialectic because the premises used are different. Dialectical premises are often far from the desired conclusion, but rhetoric cannot use such distant premises. Rhetoric does not require only true accepted opinions (mashhurat haqiqiyya) nor premises far from public opinion. Instead, it uses premises that are neither immediately present in minds (like the sun shining) nor completely absent, because if they were completely absent, minds would not be able to judge them. When such premises are mentioned, an opinion is formed. An example is the saying 'How bad is greed!'—such statements suit the temperament of the common people.

Key Terms
المقدمات الخطابية
المقدمات الجدلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A premise that is far from the desired conclusion, used in dialectic.
B) A premise that is either fully present or fully absent in the mind.
C) A premise that, when mentioned, immediately evokes an opinion and is neither too obvious nor too obscure.
D) A premise that must be a true accepted opinion (mashhurah haqiqiyyah).

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical premises must be immediately understood and plausible to the audience, unlike dialectical premises which can be more remote.

Three Types of Rhetorical Speech
Author categorizes rhetorical speech into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sophistry, gradual persuasion, and acknowledgment in rhetorical arguments.

Core Idea
Rhetoric uses three distinct modes: fallacious persuasion, stepwise concession, and direct acknowledgment.

In this passage, the author classifies rhetorical speech into three categories. Understanding these categories helps you recognize the purpose and validity of arguments in public speaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the Arabic, locate the three terms: al-mughālata, al-istidrāj, al-i‘tirāf.
2
Define each term
Explain the meaning of sophistry (deceptive argument), gradual persuasion (requires a question to lead the listener), and acknowledgment (based on a concession).
3
Compare the modes
Notice how they differ in intention and effect. Sophistry deceives, istidrāj persuades step by step, i‘tirāf builds on an admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكلام في الخطابة قد يقع بوجه المغالطة، وقد يقع بوجه الاستدراج وهو الذي يفتقر في الإفادة إلى سؤال، وقد يقع بوجه الاعتراف كما مر من قبل أن استعمال الضمائر المحرفة التي ليست حقيقية قد يكون خطابيا: فمنها ما تحريفه بسبب اللفظ كالذي يكون فيه لفظ مشترك أو ما يجري مجراه، ومنها ما تحريفه بسبب الكل وهو أن لا يكون القول يلزم عنه الأمر بحال لكن القائل يتكلف وينتقل عن القول إلى النتجية فيروجها. فمن ذلك ما يكون باشتراك الاسم الصرف كمن يمدح الكلب فيقول ألا ترى أن الكلب الذي في السماء سائر الكواكب نورا فمن ذلك ما يكون بسبب التركيب والتفصيل كمن يقول فلان يعرف الحروف والهجاء فيعرف إذا الشعر، وكقولهم كيف يكون عن شرير خير. وقد يقال هذا على جهة التوبيخ ، ويقال على جهة التثبيت؛ ومن ذلك أن يترك الأمر وينتقل إلى غيره مثل المفكّر أنه فعل شيئًا اتهم به إذا لم يكن عنده حجة يبين بها أنه لم يفعله؛ فإنه يأخذ في تقبيح من يفعل ذلك الفعل ويعظم صنعه، وهذا نوع من الاحتجاج المظنون لأن الحاكم إذا كان كون الأمر ولا كونه مشكلا لديه لا يتضح له فعُومل ما ذكرنا فيشتغل عن استثبات الحال وانتقل إلى اعتبار ما يخاطب به، أو يتراءى لديه فلم يلبث أن يصدق. فهكذا يجب أن يفهم هذا الموضع
Then speech in rhetoric may occur by way of sophistry, and may occur by way of gradual persuasion which requires a question for its benefit, and may occur by way of acknowledgment as has passed before: that the use of distorted pronouns that are not real may be rhetorical. Among them is distortion due to wording, like when there is a homonym or its equivalent; and among them is distortion due to totality, which is that the statement does not entail the thing at all, but the speaker expends effort and moves from the statement to the conclusion and promotes it. Some of that is due to pure homonymy, like one who praises a dog saying: 'Do you not see that the dog in the sky is the light of all the stars?' Some of that is due to composition and division, like one who says: 'So-and-so knows letters and spelling, so he knows poetry,' and like their saying: 'How can good come from an evil person?' This may be said by way of rebuke, and said by way of confirmation. And among that is that one leaves the issue and moves to another, like the thinker who did something he is accused of, if he has no proof to show he didn't do it; he begins to make the act seem bad and exaggerates it. This is a type of probable argument because the judge, if the existence or non-existence of the matter is problematic for him and not clear, is treated as we mentioned so that he becomes occupied from verifying the situation and moves to considering what he is addressed with, or it appears to him, and he quickly believes. Thus it should be understood.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage describes the various ways rhetorical speech can occur. The author categorizes it into three types: by sophistry (maghalata), by gradual persuasion (istidraj, which requires a question), and by acknowledgment (i'tiraf). He then focuses on distorted expressions, which can be due to wording (like homonyms) or due to totality, where the statement does not entail the conclusion. Examples include praising a dog by confusing the word 'kalb' (dog) with the constellation Canis Major, or using composition/division fallacies like claiming knowledge of letters implies knowledge of poetry. Another type is changing the subject, as when an accused person without proof attempts to vilify the act itself, diverting the judge's attention. This is a probable (maznun) argument.

Key Terms
المغالطة
الاستدراج
الاعتراف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of rhetorical speech requires a question to be effective?
A) Sophistry
B) Gradual persuasion (istidrāj)
C) Acknowledgment (i‘tirāf)
D) Distorted expression

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical speech can be classified into sophistry, gradual persuasion, and acknowledgment, each serving a distinct function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the three rhetorical modes is being used in the following debate excerpt: 'You agree that God is just, do you not? ... Then how could He punish an innocent person?' Answer: Gradual persuasion (istidrāj).

The Homonymy Fallacy in Rhetoric
Distortion due to homonymy with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and critique the fallacy of homonymy (ishterāk al-lafẓ) in rhetorical arguments.

Core Idea
Distorting meaning by exploiting a shared word deceives the listener into accepting a false conclusion.

The author gives an example of praising a dog by confusing the word 'kalb' (dog) with the constellation Canis Major. This fallacy occurs when a word with multiple meanings is used to trick the audience.

Guided Path
1
Locate the fallacy
Find the Arabic phrase praising the dog: 'ألا ترى أن الكلب الذي في السماء سائر الكواكب نورا'.
2
Identify the ambiguous term
The word 'kalb' refers both to the animal dog and the constellation Canis Major.
3
Analyze the fallacy
The premise uses 'kalb' in one sense (constellation) to support a conclusion about the other sense (animal). This is the fallacy of equivocation (homonymy).
4
Correct the argument
Reformulate to avoid ambiguity: specify which sense is intended. The brightness of the constellation says nothing about the animal dog.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Composition/Division Fallacy
Distortion due to composition/division with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and critique the fallacy of composition and division in rhetorical arguments.

Core Idea
Assuming that a property of parts applies to the whole (or vice versa) leads to invalid reasoning.

The author gives an example: 'So-and-so knows letters and spelling, so he knows poetry.' This assumes that knowing individual components (parts) guarantees knowledge of the composed whole (poetry), which is a fallacy of composition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the example
Read the Arabic: 'فلان يعرف الحروف والهجاء فيعرف إذا الشعر'.
2
Identify the parts and whole
Letters and spelling are parts; poetry is a whole composition. The argument transfers the property of knowing parts to knowing the whole.
3
Analyze the fallacy
This is the fallacy of composition: because the parts have a property (known), the whole is assumed to have that property. However, knowing letters does not suffice for knowing poetry, which requires additional skills.
4
Consider division
The reverse (division) would assume that if the whole has a property, each part has it—also fallacious. The author mentions both composition and division (التركيب والتفصيل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Red Herring Fallacy (Changing the Subject)
Distortion by changing the subject with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize when a speaker avoids proof by shifting focus to a tangential issue.

Core Idea
When lacking evidence, divert attention by attacking the act itself instead of proving one's innocence.

The author describes a person accused of an act who, lacking proof, begins to vilify the act itself, diverting the judge's attention. This is a type of probable argument (iḥtijāj maẓnūn) that relies on psychological manipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read: 'المفكّر أنه فعل شيئًا اتهم به إذا لم يكن عنده حجة يبين بها أنه لم يفعله؛ فإنه يأخذ في تقبيح من يفعل ذلك الفعل ويعظم صنعه'.
2
Understand the diversion
Instead of addressing the accusation (whether he did it), the speaker attacks the moral character of the act, making the judge focus on the act's evil rather than the evidence.
3
Recognize the fallacy
This is a red herring fallacy: a change of subject that diverts attention from the original issue. The author calls it 'probable argument' because it is not certain but can influence the judge.
4
Analyze the effect
The judge, confused by the shift, may accept the speaker's credibility and fail to verify the facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
